VOLUME XV.
THE SENATE.

---

Tuesday, 26th January,
1932.
Tests for Tuberculosis in Cows.
"That
Motion for Adjournment.
MR. LONG: I beg to move,
this House do now adjourn until such
date as Mr. Speaker shall appoint."
Mr. BARCLAY: I wish to ask the
Deputy Leader of the House whether the
Government are satisfied with the present
arrangements for testing cattle for tuberculosis? I have given the Deputy Leader
previous notice of this question, and I
think it is a question that should be
answered, as it is a very important one.
aware
Mr. LONG: The veterinary officers of
the Ministry of Agriculture are
that a cow may give a positive reaction
when tested by the ordinary
ordinary subcutaneous tuberculin test, and that when
subsequently retested by the same method
at a short interval may pass the test.
This phenomenon is well recognised as
one of the main disadvantages of the sub-
Adjournment---
Tuberculosis in Cows
22 DECEMBER 1931
cutaneous method of testing. The difficulty is commonly surmounted by
veterinarians by increasing the test dose
of tuberculin when using the subcutaneous method in cases where tuberculin is known or suspected to have been
recently injected.
This and other disadvantages of the
subcutaneous method led the Medical
Research Council of the Privy Council to
investigate and recommend an intradermal test in preference to the subcutaneous test in cattle. In their 1928
Report (page 42) they state:-
"One important advantage of the
double intradermal test
is that
it is found to be a reliable test on repetition . . . the discrepant results
shown by repetition of subcutaneous
tests under field conditions are not
encountered.
Several veterinary surgeons in dilating on the merits of the double
intradermal test express the opinion
that it is one which does not lend
itself to nefarious practices; in other
words "doping" by previous injections
of tuberculin cannot be employed to
nullify the test as can be done in the
case of an animal about to undergo
the subcutaneous test."
The intradermal test is that prescribed
for the testing of herds producing Grade
A (Tuberculin tested) Milk, and in the
Ministry's experience has given good
results. It may be observed, too, that
the Ministry also encourages the use of
the double intradermal test for premium
bulls. The intradermal method is, in
addition, being used to an increasing
extent by private veterinary practitioners.
All available evidence goes to show that
the intradermal test can be applied at
any time without impairing its accuracy.
It is highly probable that purchasers of
valuable animals would obtain greater
security if they bought subject to the
beasts concerned passing a tuberculin
test by the intradermal method rather
than by the subcutaneous method.
Mr. HILL DICKSON: This is a very
interesting subject, and I am glad to hear
the Deputy Leader of the House give the
information he has just given to the
House. But I would like to remind the
Deputy Leader and his advisers that
many of the veterinary surgeons have not
been able to carry out this intradermal
test. It is a test that is new to
veterinary surgeons, and only those who
have had special training have been able
to carry it out. You, Mr. Speaker, will
remember that the veterinary surgeon of
an institution that both you and I are
connected with told us distinctly that he
was not able to use this means of testing.
Therefore, it is clear that veterinary
surgeons will have to be specially trained
before they will be in a position to carry
out this test. It is admitted that in connection with the ordinary tuberculosis
test an animal can be doped and filled
with tubercle bacilli and then she will not
re-act to the test, but this intradermal
test is perfectly safe, although it is very
costly and very technical to administer.
Colonel SHARMAN CRAWFORD .
May I ask the Deputy Leader of the
House if he can give us some idea of the
date when the Second Reading of the
Valuation Bill will be taken.
Mr. LONG: I understand the Valuation Bill will come before the Senate for
Second Reading about 26th January.
With regard to Senator Hill Dickson's remarks as to the difficulty of administering the double intradermal test. I would
point out that this is the reason why the
Ministry have not yet prescribed this as
the standard test. Veterinary surgeons,
however, are rapidly coming to understand it and
unskilled persons,
including farmers in a good many
districts, apply the test. The time is
rapidly coming when the Ministry will
prescribe this as the standard test.
even
Might I point out that it does not
necessarily follow that because a cow reacts to a tuberculin test it is giving milk
containing tubercle bacilli. The estimate
of the Ministry is that not more than one
per cent. of the milch cows in the area
are giving diseased milk. I wish to
assure the House that the Ministry are
giving close attention to this matter.
On Question, Motion, "That this House
do now adjourn until such date as Mr.
Speaker shall appoint," agreed to.
Adjourned accordingly at
Half after Twelve o'clock
until 26th January, 1932.

of

Laid before the House (pursuant to

Valuation Acts Amendment Bill.
Order read for resuming adjourned
debate on Question, "That the Bill be
now read a Second time."-(Mr. Long.)
Question again proposed.
Mr. LESLIE: I think I might begin
by saying that the Bill, which I rise to
support this afternoon, has met with an
amount of misapprehension, misconstruction, and misrepresentation to an extent
hitherto almost undreamt of. I do not
propose to waste the time of the House
this afternoon by dealing with any complaints made against the Bill, which are
obviously prompted by nothing more than
motives of self interest. I do not propose to say much about the Financial
Relations Committee, on which I had the
privilege of serving, but I must be permitted to remind the House that the revaluation of buildings was one of the
principal, if not the principal, recommendations of that Committee,
in making that recommendation that
Committee was following out recommendations of the earlier Committee
under Sir Ernest Clark, which sat in the
year 1923.
and
The hon. and learned Senator (Mr.
Campbell) when opposing the Bill yesterday afternoon-I am not quite sure that
the opposition was carried so far as to
propose the normal Amendment that the
Second Reading be deferred for six
months or not, but, at all events, in making his speech in opposition to the Bill,
Senator Campbell quoted sundry passages
from the Report to which I have alluded,
and I think, perhaps, that the quotations
which he selected were not altogether very
happily chosen. To take from a Report
of that character an individual statement
without seeing how that individual statement fits in to the general tenor of the
Report is not unlikely, as I think, to
occasion more of that misrepresentation
and misconstruction to which I have referred. Again, he gave us various figures.
I am not aware of any figure that he
quoted with which I was able to agree.
I
could not follow the figures, and I cannot say that I accept them.
And, again, I understood the hon. and
learned Senator to say definitely that
pastural land was excluded-if I may put
it this way, excluded from the exclusion
provided for in the Bill. I hope I am
not misquoting him, but that is my recollection of what he said. There are
definitions given in the Bill, and I think
that those definitions are sufficiently explicit. The definition of agricultural land
does not seem to me to leave any room
for doubt. Again, we were told, I think,
that in dealing with agricultural hereditaments the value of the house was taken
as two-thirds of the value of the whole.
If I heard correctly that was made as a
general statement. I do not think that
either of those statements are such as
should be allowed to pass even for a
moment. I do not want to take up time
by referring to the numerous communications I have received, probably the same
as many other hon. Members have received, and I do not want to pillory any
particular individual or any particular
public body. I do not say that provocation has not been given, but I do not
think it is necessary to carry on a controversy of that sort.
I should prefer to spend such time as
I shall occupy this afternoon in endeavouring to examine the causes of the discontent
and to show how little justification they
really have, and I want, too, to lay more
stress than has hitherto been laid on what
I may venture to describe as the Imperial
repercussion of the Measure before us.
Valuation Bill-
Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Mr. Leslie]
If i may say so, the Senator on my left,
who sits also on the beast City Council
(Mr. Joseph Cunningnam), grasped that
point, when I have called the imperial
repercussion, in the speech he delivered
yesterday afternoon, and until he spoke
I cannot say I had heard any observation,
read anything in the newspapers
recognising what seems to be a cardinal
fact.
or
There are complaints of all sorts against
the Bull. Some of them have been dealt
with already, and I want if I can to leave
those out. To some extent one can divide
the complaints into local-those which
come from the local authorities on direct
questions affecting the rates-those which
come from the public-I will not say the
but from particular
general public, but from
sections of the public, and which relate
more to taxes, and then there are other
and more general complaints which apply
to both rates and taxes, and which it is
impossible to allocate to either one or
other of those two divisions. But I want
to take first the matters more
or less
concerned with rates. It has been said
repeatedly that rates are too high already.
The observations in the Financial Relations Committee Report to the effect that
the amount that can be in equity extracted from the ratepayers is near its
limit has been quoted again and again. I
agree, of course, that the limit has been
very nearly reached, but I shall show in
a minute that that is somewhat beside the
question.
It is also said that if valuations do go
up, as it is expected that they will go up,
the rates will not go down. Well, as to
that I do wish to point out that the ratepayers have the matter in their own
hands. Local authorities are democratically-controlled bodies, and if the ordinary
ratepayer will not interest himself
sufficiently to secure the election of
economically-minded people to represent
him, I do not think that he can blame
Parliament for whatever result
may
happen. Certainly there is no argument
there for asking Parliament to maintain
an out-of-date system in order to preserve
-if I may put it quite plainly-the ratepayers from the consequences of their own
ineptitude.
It is also urged that what are commonly
called the derating grants will suffer.
That is a complete misapprehension of
the whole situation. Hon. Members will
no doubt remember a discussion which
took place in this House in the autumn
of 1928, when the Minister of Finance
was with us, and the result of that discussion was, if I may say so, to show
most plainly that these grants to rates
are not bonuses or bounties to the rates.
They are more in the form of an indemnity to the rate fund for the money
that the rate fund loses owing to derating,
not only of agricultural land, but derating
of agricultural land, and to the extent
provided by statute, the derating of the
industrial, freight transport and fisheries
hereditaments. Those matters seem to
me to be commonly forgotten; in fact,
invariably forgotten, and they are of
importance. I think, perhaps, that the
question of the misunderstanding of the
derating grant is a fundamental error
common to almost all the opponents of
this Bill. The effect of that fundamental
error is, if I may say so, that the main
issue is obscured, and that they are encouraged to waste their time and energies
in pursuing the will-o'-the-wisp of an
imaginary expediency instead of accepting the guidance of the polestar of
principle.
Another point that is made-I think
the hon. and learned Senator made it
revaluation
yesterday again-is that
every five years is too frequent; that it is
unsettling as regards the value of property,
and unduly expensive to the ratepayers.
Now that point is one which, at first sight,
I think, is calculated to commend itself.
It is rather a strong argument, but it is a
very specious one, indeed. It has been
most effectively dealt with by the Commissioner of Valuation in the reservation
which he appended to the final Report of
Sir Ernest Clark's Committee, which was
issued in 1924. Mr. Ward dealt with the
matter most clearly and concisely.
He said that in recommending as he
did revaluation every five years, he had
had in mind the failure of the Act of
1852 to attain its object. The provisions
there were "of a permissive and discretionary character, and I believe that it is
to that that their failure is due." He
commends the quinquennial system in
London and goes on to say of revaluation
at comparatively short intervals that it
has three advantages. The first is that the
revaluation does not create a far-reaching
disturbance in values. That is the judgment of experience on that particular
point, disturbance of values. The rise or
fall in values is comparatively small, and
Valuation Bill-
Second Reading
27 JANUARY 1932
is local rather than general, and uncertainty in the property market is thus
avoided. The second advantage of a
frequent valuation is that the labour is
less, and the third is that the cost is
reduced. These, as I have said, are the
findings of experience, and I venture to
say the answer to that part of the problem is complete and conclusive.
I
Again, the point is made of the cost,
not only of the original general revaluation, but of the annual revisions. It is said
that the burden to be cast on the rates is
undue and that it is higher than the rates
have had to bear hitherto. Hitherto the
matter has been governed as the House
will remember by the provisions of the
Act of 1874. I think the Parliamentary
Secretary alluded to that yesterday.
do not suppose it is generally remembered
that a proposal was made in 1926 to raise
the cost to some figure more nearly resembling the actual cost. I think the
figure talked of at that time, instead of
£3,405, was £6,750. That total figure
of £6,750 was the figure that first appeared
in this Bill when brought into another
place, but since then the Minister has
found it possible to reduce that sum
practically by one-half. I think I am
right in saying in every case but one the
charge has been reduced by 50 per cent.,
and the net result is that County Tyrone
will pay neither more nor less than it
paid under the Schedule of 1874, and the
other county remains at the same figure.
These are the facts, and, moreover,
may I venture to remind the House there
is a further protection in Sub-Clause
(2) of Clause 16 of the Bill. I
think we may leave the rates, but
before we do so it would seem not unfitting that we should do here what
might have been done before, and that is
express in some slight way our sense of
the generosity with which the Government
has dealt with this particular question.
And now turning to taxes, taxes have
this difference from rates-and I mean by
taxes income-tax-you do not pay incometax if you have no income. We do not
impose income-tax, and it makes no
difference for that matter whether we do
or do not, because I think taxes differ
from rates in one way though they resemble them in another; that is, it is
the rate of the taxation, not the basis,
which is the measure of the hardship.
Admittedly income-tax is too high. It is
crippling in its operation, but that is no
argument for any false basis. Now the
product of the income-tax-I regret to
mention this-the product of the incometax of the Six Counties, after deductions
with which the House is by this time
familiar, does not go into the English
Treasury as has been said. Northern
Ireland pays nothing to the English
Treasury, one reason being perhaps
that England has not got a Treasury
to receive it. Anything available
is paid into the Exchequer of the
United Kingdom of Great Britain and
Northern Ireland in accordance with the
system which has been accepted as a right
system to govern the relations on that
particular matter.
Now, that system was given effect to in
what was called the Colwyn award. That
is out of date at the moment. It has,
however, been recently re-adopted and
promulgated. The Committee of the
Privy Council in reporting on the question of an Imperial Contribution from the
Islands of Jersey, Guernsey, and Man,
adopted the following thesis. It laid
down as a basis:-
Where there are serious differences
in relative wealth, the most equitable
method of apportionment would seem
to be to estimate the yield of a justly
graduated system of taxation applied
on identical terms to both countries, to
deduct therefrom the cost of internal
government, assumed to be conducted
on similar principles in both, and to
treat the surplus as the proper contribution of each to the common purpose.
That is the system to which the Government is certainly committed, and of
which most of us are in hearty support.
I think it is fair for me to ask from any
opponent of the Bill does he want to alter
that system, and if he does, what does
he propose to put in its place. And if
he does not want to alter it-and I do
not believe the majority of them do-how
does he propose to meet that phrase "a
justly graduated system of taxation
applied on identical terms to hoth
countries"? We must recognise our interdependence on Great Britain, and if
we do that, I think we must recognise
also that we cannot pay our debts in a
spurious coinage. That is what you are
doing if you are paying them, and in so
far as you are paying them, on a false
system of valuation.
In any case, during the
the current
financial year, we have the existing figure of £75.000 as the amount
of the Imperial Contribution. I do
Valuation Bill-
Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Mr. Leslie]
not know whether hon. Members
reflecting on what is done directly or indirectly by the Colonies or Dominions,
will consider that £75,000 is a proper
contribution for Northern Ireland, which
is neither a Colony nor a Dominion, but
is one of the root fibres of the Empire.
It is
That
Some complaint is made as to the
action of the Imperial Treasury in pressing this matter on our attention.
even suggested that the matter is entirely
for ourselves and that they have no power
to compel us. May I venture to remind
the House of a particular Section of the
Income Tax Act of 1918 which gives
complete power to require a revaluation
if the valuation for poor-rate purposes is
not carried out according to law.
particular Section occurs in Part nine of
the Act of 1918, that is the part specially
applicable to Ireland. So far from making
any complaint of the action of the Im
perial Treasury, I think we ought to
recognise that in what they have done.
they have been acting up to their duties
as trustees of the United Kingdom, to
which I have referred, and indeed to the
Empire as whole.
a
We have been
treated, must admit, with great
leniency, and it ill becomes us to make
any complaint.
we
Leaving both rates and taxes, I am not
going to say anything about this accusation, that this Bill is for the balancing of
the Budget, although I do not regard
balancing the Budget as anything to be
ashamed of. It is suggested that it is to
save our own Exchequer something in
these derating payments. Now, the Financial Relations Committee, very guardedly
indeed, suggested that in that way we
might perhaps save £50,000, and valuation of Northern Ireland is approximately £5,250,000 on the present basis.
That £50,000 I must warn the House
would not fall in the same way on town
and country, because of the difference in
the way in which payments are made and
the basis on which they are calculated.
More urgently than that is urged that
there are anomalies and we could deal
with this matter simply by correcting the
anomalies. I wish that this word
"anomaly" had never been used. It is
most unfortunate. After all, the use of
the word "anomaly" does suggest some
nomos or other to which we are referred. But there is no nomos left.
Valuations have been shown more than
once to be out of gear because some have
remained since 1860 or thereabouts without alteration, and the rest represents
practically every year since then. Revaluations have not been carried out not because
our collectors did not do their duty, but
because they appear to have neglected the
revaluation of any hereditaments which
are not either new or structurally altered.
They left out of account altogether an
important class of buildings that changed
their value because the locality in which
they stood changed its value.
We hope
as a result of the Bill that will pass away.
Again, something was said on the basis
of the recommendation of Sir Ernest
Clark's Committee that valuation should
be postponed until decontrol was complete. Sir Ernest Clark's Committee re-
1924 and
ported in
one result was
to add enormously to these irregularities
which occurred, because as a result of that
report Parliament passed a temporary
Measure, the Revaluation Act of 1924.
which established a purely fanciful
method of valuing buildings on the
1914-15 standard and adding 15 per cent.
That introduced entirely fictitious values,
and naturally aggravated the irregularities already existing. That is now
about to be removed. But it was never
intended, may I remind the House, that
that Bill should be other than a temporary Measure. But it was passed in
1924, and this is 1932 and that Bill is not
yet repealed, although it will be when
this Bill becomes an Act and comes into
full operation.
As regards decontrol I must point out
that since 1924 a great advance has been
made on the lines of decontrol, and decontrol continues. Probably before this
Bill gets actually to work decontrol will
be very nearly complete. At all events.
those houses which are most likely to
be affected by the variations in valuations
will be decontrolled. As regards the
smaller houses affected-I believe this
question arises in Belfast, as the Lord
Mayor referred to-it may be that the
Belfast Corporation may have to come to
Parliament for some slight variations in
their local Acts regarding the arrangements which they make as regards the
rating on some of the very small houses,
those of £8 valuation I think were referred to. That seems to be a matter for
the Belfast Corporation to deal with, and
they appear to have ample time to do that
during the next three or four years by
introducing a local Bill.
Valuation Bill-
Second Reading
27 JANUARY 1932
Again, it has been urged-we heard
it yesterday that the Bill should be postponed. It has been postponed too long
already. The only effect of the postponement that I can see would be to
enable some people-I do not know who
they are to escape from their proper
share of payment for some years. I do
not agree with that argument at all.
I think I have done with all the complaints against the Bill. I want to say a
word or two about the complete omission
of the revaluation of land. The revaluation
of land was recommended by Sir Ernest
Clark's Committee, and it was not recommended by the Financial Relations Committee for the reasons given in their
report. I need not go into those reasons,
but I, of course, adhere to that report
and I say the Government is right
not to cumber the present Measure with
the revaluation of land. Therefore, it
follows that one cannot properly use this
occasion as an opportunity for introducing one of the recommendations of Sir
Ernest Clark's Committee, and that is
that the person who thinks himself unjustly treated as regards land hereditament should have the same right as the
other hereditament owner of appealing
for a revaluation. There is something to
be said for that. Although land is derated it is not derated as regards
malicious injury and drainage, and the
alteration in the income tax, the increase
in the amount, and the extension of the
net have introduced a new factor. All
the same I think it right to leave out this
question of value which really affects
those who like myself have the misfortune to have a large proportion of heavy
land which is generally supposed to be
over-valued. I cannot pass from the
question of land without pointing out
that if it had been decided to revalue land
the value probably would be lower rather
than greater, and, therefore, the State
would have made a great saving in derating grants. I think the Lord Mayor
told us that the other day. We cannot
leave land without expressing our gratification as regards what has been done in
reference to the agricultural buildings.
I am getting down to that stage of my
speech, which is near the end, when one
asks for replies to questions, but I do not
want a reply to any question this afternoon. These matters are much better dealt
with in Committee, but I think I may say
-I wish to be corrected if I am wrongthat I make out the effect as regards agricultural buildings is that for practical purposes they will be neither better nor worse
off than at the present time. There may
be, of course, some slight variations
affecting taxation perhaps rather than
rating.
I now come to some of the intricacies
of the Bill. It is a very intricate Bill
and some provisions are very complex,
but taken all round I do not think there
is any very serious difficulty in following
most of the provisions. There are one
or two suggestions I wish to make. I
begin with the first Clause. I think we
ought to be told now something more about
the pomp
and circumstances of a general
revaluation. Hitherto, as you will remember, the Commissioner has been required
to send or transmit copies of the various
documents to the persons concerned. It
seems to me we are now inviting the publication of a new Doomsday Book. Something ought to be done to ensure the
deposit of a complete record and public
notice ought to be given that the issue
is about to be made. The simplest
method, I think, is to ask the Minister
to make a regulation on the subject. Connected with that subject is the question
of the fixed date of 1st March. There
are obviously great advantages in having
a fixed date, but there are also disadvantages, and one of the disadvantages is that
the 1st March sooner or later will fall on
a Sunday. In those rare cases where
Parliament require things to be done on
a fixed date they make provision for it.
I think we might get round this matter
in the Committee Stage.
The next thing I want to call attention
to is Sub-Clause 2 of Clause 6, and as to
that I hope the Minister will not be unduly perturbed if I put down a formal
motion to omit it. It is fairly obvious
that it was not inserted without reference
to Section 72 of the English Local Government Act of 1929. The fact remains
that the sub-clause does seem to me to
be worded rather ambiguously, and I
think we ought to ask the Minister to
elucidate it a little more. A formal
Motion to omit the paragraph will perhaps furnish the matchbox on which the
Minister can strike the match.
Mr. POLLOCK: The whole of Sub-
Section 2?
Mr. LESLIE: Sub-Clause 2. It is rather
difficult to follow. We pass to Clause 12,
which has been objected to as requiring

Finally, I venture if I may to commend

have been paying and have not done so.

An effort has been made before the Bill

of extra taxation-of an extra charge-is
placed on the local authorities by the
Government. It is only just to the loca!
authorities that the atmosphere should be

a
This Bill is unique in another aspect.
It is to all intents and purposes a Finance
Bill, and I think I am correct in saying
that never before has a Bill been introduced into Parliament by the Minister
of Finance without single estimate or indication or approximate idea
being given of what it is going to cost in
money to the ratepayers and taxpayers of
Northern Ireland. I say without hesitation that this is a leap in the dark; it
is a blinder leap in the dark than that
taken by the same Members of the same
Government when they undertook to pay
eight millions a year as a contribution
to the Imperial Parliament. Is it not
admitted now that that was a leap in
the dark, a wild step for which there was
no justification? I say that this is a
madder leap in the dark even than the
undertaking to pay eight millions a year
to the Imperial Parliament.
It has been stated here that there has
been no revaluation since 1852. To those
of us who meet the people professionally
it is a well-known fact that two of the
greatest hindrances to the development
of building hereditaments in Northern
Ireland have been, in the first place, the
fact that the people fear to make the
slightest improvement, such as putting a
porch to their dwelling-house or any ornamentation on the front of their buildings,
lest there would be an increased valuation
put on their premises, and, in the second
place, the insecurity of tenure of leaseholders. The fear of increased taxation
for making improvements has been one
of the drawbacks to the development of
Northern Ireland. If that has been so
up to the present, what is going to happen now? We have heard a great deal
of talk about paying our way to Great
Britain.
Let me remind the House-and I
wonder did the Chancellor use this in
his argument when he faced the Imperial
authorities-that about 1895 the Childers
Commission sat. On that Commission
was the leader of the then Unionist party,
Colonel Saunderson, and also members of
divergent political parties. That Commission found we were taxed to the tune
of £3,000,000 a year too much. If you
take that figure up to 1920, Ireland was
overtaxed by Great Britain to the extent
of £360,000,000. That is the figure found
by a Commission set up by the British
Government. If we apportion one-third
as the share of Northern Ireland, we
find we have paid Great Britain in overtaxation on the finding of the English
Commission the sum of £120,000,000 up
to 1920. Those are figures which are on
record.
With regard to derating, so far as the
farmers are concerned there is no misconception about that, as the hon. and
learned Senator, Mr. Leslie, would suggest. The Derating Bill is the cause to
a great extent of the present position.
It has created chaos in the local
authorities, and to 90 per cent. of the
farmers of Northern Ireland, that is, the
small farmers, it has been of no advantage, good, bad, or indifferent. On the
contrary, such a farmer has paid more
than the £3 or £4 which he saves in rates
in the cost of transport for himself and
his produce.
Yet in addition to the chaos that Act
has wrought and the position in which
the farmers find themselves to-day, a Government official is to go in on any farmstead and undo any good the Derating
Act has done by putting whatever value
he likes upon the dwelling-house of the
farmer. I say this Bill is handing over
the people body and soul so far as
taxation and rating value are concerned
to half-a-dozen or a dozen officials-I do
not know how many will be appointed.
It is handing over their property, income,
and all other things to a few valuers to
be appointed by the Government.
is a very serious and dangerous step.

with all the arguments that have been

most inopportune time, but I hope to

We are told-this is the Government
case that this is an opportune time because there is trade depression, because
industries are not flourishing. That may,
although I do not believe it, indirectly
affect the question of valuation, but let
us come to the point, and this is the
germane point, the dominating point.
If there is going to be a revision of the
valuations of the taxpayers of Northern
Ireland that valuation will not be fixed
on depression or prosperity in trade, but
on the rent, which is the letting value of
the premises. And what is the fact?
Rents have been soaring to a dangerous
height far beyond the economic rent, to
a height dangerous to the safety, and
peace, and contentment of the State, so
much so that for a number of years past
the Government have had to pass special
legislation in the way of Rent Restriction
Acts to keep down that unhealthy soaring of rents beyond what the people can
pay.
This
The time special legislation has to be
passed in order to try to keep down an
unhealthy and uneconomic rise in rents
is the time chosen by the Northern Government to bring about a revaluation in
Northern Ireland. I would like the
Minister of Finance when he comes to
reply to address himself to that argument.
The valuation is bound to be according to
the letting value, that is the rent.
Bill is introduced by the Northern Government at a time when rents are soaring so high that the people cannot pay
them and several Acts have been passed
to try to restrict such an unnatural, uneconomic, and impossible rise in rents.
Therefore, I say beyond doubt, beyond
challenge, beyond argument, there could
not have been a more inopportune time
chosen to impose this burden upon the
taxpayers and ratepayers of Northern
Ireland.

I can
ing through the administration.
well understand the position of many men

duty to the State, and setting aside personal friendship, they put on record their
profound belief and conviction that this
Bill is going to have a most serious and
damaging effect upon the people of Northern Ireland, householders, shopkeepers,
and everybody else.
Take the case of the small shopkeeper.
His rent has mounted until it is now
three times what it was in 1920 owing to
the shortage of houses. He is holding on
by the wall. He is able to exist at the
present time in these times of depression,
but if this impost is going to be put upon
him, if his house is going to be valued
at three times its present value, that small
business man is going to be put entirely
out of business. I believe that will occur
in innumerable instances in Northern
Ireland.

Mr. McCORKELL: I crave the indulgence of the House for a short space,
because I am here, like the Lord Mayor
of Belfast (Sir Crawford McCullagh), not
as what I may call a regular Senator, but
as an ex-officio Member as chairman of
the body which I represent. I hope when
I come to the close of the few brief remarks I have to make I shall have been
able to make a concrete suggestion which
may be of value, and which I hope will be
accepted. First of all, however, so much
has been said about public bodies and
the resolutions they send out that I feel
justified in giving a very brief sketch of
what happened at the public body with
which I am connected. I will endeavour
to tell it in lighter vein.
What happened in Derry was that a
little dog was sent down to us. The public
said, "It is a pup. Let us drown it or
send it back. We know its breeding. It
is by Extravagance out of Ineptitude."
That was the public spirit at the time,
but the Corporation of Derry is not a
rash body. They said "No. Let us
examine the dog. So they gave him over
to the Finance Committee. That Committee said "Well, he may be a little bit
smelly, but he is probably not so bad,
and we will make more inquiries about
him." They did so and we found that
the pup had been sent over from Westminster with a little note to say that he
was a pretty useful little puppy, and
would become a good financial hound, and
that if we failed to walk him we would
no longer be considered as members of the
Imperial hunt. Well, I think that was
only fair, because I believe that the
longer we remain members of the Imperial
hunt the better it will be for us, and I
also believe that in the long run when we
have negotiated the last fence we shall be
up with the hunt and not, as the hon.
Senator on my left (Mr. McAllister)
fears, at the tail.
But now to take a more serious view
for the moment. What was the resolution sent up by the Derry Corporation?
It was simply that we respectfully suggested that the time was inopportune for
the introduction of this revaluation; and
we suggest that still. We do not suggest
that the time is inopportune for making
a revaluation. What we say is that,
even if it were possible to make it instanter, it would be inopportune to apply
it at the moment. And why? We are
living with an enormous income-tax, a
terrific bank rate, and a jellified pound,
and this is not the time to impose fresh
burdens. That is our feeling. But we
think that the very fact that times are
bad makes it a reason why, if we must
have a revaluation, as we believe we must,
the present is the best time to make that

Mr. DORMAN: Some years ago
heard the story of an Irishman who was
stranded on what he thought to be a
lonely island in the midst of the Atlantic.
As he clambered over the rocks another
castaway looked over, and the question
my country-man put to him was, "Is
there any Government here?" The other
derelict answered "Yes," and the Irishman said "Then I am against the Government." On this particular occasion I
am not against the Government. I am
in favour of the Government.
We con-
Valuation Bill-
Second Reading
27 JANUARY 1932
stantly hear the expression "Imperialism"
used in this House and by supporters of
the Government throughout the Province.
Some few years ago the supporters of the
Government, who were then under the
Imperial Parliament of Great Britain and
Ireland, armed themselves for the purpose
of resisting the placing upon the Statute
Book of an Act of Parliament passed by
the Imperial Parliament. The slogan of
the followers of the party which now constitutes the Government in this area was
then the Churchillian slogan, "Ulster
will fight and Ulster will be right."
appears to me that on this particular
occasion a more appropriate slogan for
these professed supporters of the Government and for the Imperialists who are
clamouring for the rejection of this Bill
by this House would be, so far as
Imperialism is concerned, "Ulster will
play, but Ulster will not pay."
It
I have received, as other hon. Senators
have, a sheaf of resolutions all of a
uniform character. When I received
those resolutions I asked myself two questions: Who has sent these resolutions, and
why? They are undoubtedly men of
property in the various areas in which
they occupy representative positions. The
fact of their ownership of property in the
area has given them a social status, and
that social status has commended them to
the electors as probably the most suitable
men to trust with the trusteeship of the
communal property in the area and the
administration of all the Acts of Parliament placed on the Statute Book for the
protection of the life, health, and wellbeing of the community as a whole. These
men are elected for the specific purpose
of looking after the purity of the air we
breathe, the purity of the food we eat,
the just weight and measure of all commodities exchanged in the area. They
are elected as administrative bodies, not
as law-making bodies.
Now it appears to me that it would be
entirely wrong to mention the name of
any one of those representative men,
however desirable it might be in the interests of the public so that the public
might see the inspiring influence behind
the opposition to this measure. Tf I
mentioned one of those names you. Mr.
Speaker, would object on the ground that
it would be a prostitution of the position
which we enjoy as Members of this
House to hold un any public representative to public disapprobation under the
cloak of the privileges which we possess.
But it appears to me that if the electors
who sent these men to represent them in
these various public bodies would take
the trouble to inquire what proportion of
the property in their several areas is possessed by these men who are clamouring
against this Bill, if they would take the
trouble to ascertain the rateable value of
that property, and then again, as I have
taken the trouble, to go round the area,
see the tenants occupying these men's
property, and ascertain the rack rents
being paid by the tenants in occupation
of those premises, the electors would look
upon this subject from a different point
of view to that from which they are
alleged to look upon it to-day in consequence of the difficulties which are presented and the assumed wrongs which will
be imposed upon them if this Measure
passes into law.
I do not wish to occupy the time of the
House to any unnecessary extent, but
since this Bill was introduced I have
taken the trouble to inspect a series of
properties outside a half-mile radius from
the centre of the city. I have called into
properties that may be regarded as residential, into lock-up shops, into shops,
and into shops and residences. For the
information of the House I will speak of
some of the things I have discovered outside a radius of half-a-mile from the
centre of the city. This is a residential
house. I will not take up the time of
the House by quoting the rents of the
various rooms occupied by the present
tenants. Pre-war this house was let at
£30, and the landlord to my knowledge
has been in receipt of a weekly rent for
the last six or seven years of £2 13s per
week, or £137 16s per annum. The valuation of the premises is £26. He is therefore not assessable to income tax on
£111 16s, which he would have to pay if
he earned that income instead of receiving it as the owner of this hereditament.
Here is the case of lock-up shops, also
half-a-mile out. The rating of the four
shops is £41, and the landlord is in receipt of a rack rent of £240. Might I
say in passing that the rack rent in the
first case which I quoted is five times
greater than the valuation? The rack
rent of these shops is £240, and with a
valuation of £41 the landlord gets off
paying income tax on £199, the difference
between the rack rent and the valuation.
Here is another case. A shop and residence valued at £39. The landlord is in
receipt of a rack rent of £160; he there-
M
Valuation Bill-
Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Mr. Dorman]
fore gets off with income tax on £121.
Here is another case. The valuation is
£39, and the rack rent £80 or more than
twice the valuation.
I discovered that in the centre of the
city of Belfast there was an equal disparity between the valuation and the rack
rent received of £400, £500, £600, or
£800 more than the valuation; that
applies to property in the neighbourhood
of Donegall Place, premises which I
visited this morning. I saw and spoke
to the tenants and got from them the
valuation. I found that on a valuation
of £25 the occupiers are paying a rental
of £125, £150, and £200 per annum,
that the owner in that case is not paying
income tax on £175, as he would be if
he were assessed in the ordinary way at
the rate ruling in Great Britain at the
present time. There never has been a
Chancellor of a Federal Exchequer who
has not had the same difficulties to contend with as we are facing here in con
nection with the Bill that is before us for
consideration to-day.
SO
The same difficulty prevailed in Canada,
and if hon. Members will take the trouble
to go to the dining-room in this House
they will see there a medal cast in commemoration of the sixtieth anniversary of
the Confederation Act. Seven or eight
years after the passing of the Confederation
Bill into law the same difficulties had to
be met by the Federal Government, and
the Imperial Government had to send an
Irishman to Canada in order to unify the
various provinces and make them recognise their right to make an equitable
contribution not merely to the upkeep of
the province in which they resided but to
the Federal Government as well. I do
not see how any Chancellor of the Exchequer could do otherwise than ask our
Minister of Finance and the Government
of Northern Ireland to pass this Bill and
to enforce it in the area under their control.
I have taken the trouble to examine
the figures with regard to various towns
across the water with a population of
under 500,000, and to compare critically
their rating value with that of the city
of Belfast. Take, for instance, Bradford, with a population of 121,000 fewer
than Belfast. It is valued at £716.000
more than the value of Belfast. The
amount per head of the population in
Bradford is £8 13s 94d, and the amount
per head of the population in Belfast is
£4 8s 21d. That is to say, the value of
hereditaments in Belfast is 50.8 per cent.
of that of Bradford. If any hon. Member of this House knows Bradford he
knows that it is a textile centre, and if
he walked round the mills and round the
suburbs of that city, and compared them
with the mills and suburbs of Belfast, he
could not believe that the value of Belfast was only 50 per cent. of that of
Bradford. I could go on giving cases.
There is not a town in Great Britain of
under 500,000 inhabitants with as low a
valuation as Belfast. Belfast's valuation
is 78 per cent. of the valuation of the
town across the water the valuation of
which is the nearest approach to that of
this city. The valuation of Belfast is
only 32.6 per cent. of the valuation of
some of the towns with a population of
over 400,000. In these circumstances I
do not see how we can refuse to pass this
Bill.
There is another feature I should like
to mention, and that is, that our social
services are in danger if our valuation is
permitted to continue as it is at present.
A few weeks ago in this House I elicited
an answer to a question which showed
that for the last six years the Minister
of Finance has been in receipt of £541,000
a year in order to make up the deficiency
in the Unemployment Fund of Northern
Ireland. So far as I know, there has
been no public meeting held in the Six
Counties to enable the citizens to discuss
this matter and to enlighten them as to
the consequences of this Measure. I believe that the working classes of the Six
Counties are too self-respecting to continue to allow our Minister of Finance to
act the part of Lazarus standing at the
gate of the Imperial Exchequer from year
to year to receive the amount of the deficiency in our social services, because of
the under valuing of the area in which we
live. Therefore, I support this Bill.
Colonel PAKENHAM: I shall not detain the House very long, but I wish to
offer a few remarks on the Bill which is

to congratulate them on their courage in
bringing forward this Bill, which is
bound to be a very unpopular Measure
among a certain class of people.
government has no courage it is not
worth calling a government. All the
more credit therefore to our Government

for taking the step they are taking. It
has been common knowledge for years
that we have been proceeding on a false
valuation, that we have been neglecting
our property and allowing it to fall into
very crooked and wrong ways.
Whatever may be said, the Government were
bound to take this matter into consideration, and I am only sorry that they did

But, having taken it into consideration
now I think they are bound to receive
the support of the people who have the

There may be no doubt that a certain

The people who will have to pay more are

I do not want to go over the facts

On the other

I
hand, I do not join with the opponents
of this Measure who seem to think that
in every case valuation must go up.
do not think that is at all likely.
think that in a great many cases the valuation will go down. Therefore some
people will have to pay more, and some will
save on the revaluation. I do not think
Senator Campbell was right in talking
about dictation from Whitehall. We
are a subordinate Parliament, and as
Senator Dorman said the British Parliament is a Federal Parliament. There are
certain laws and regulations of the Federal
Parliament which we as a junior Parliament have to follow; if we do not the
Federal Parliament will say, "If you do
not do your job we will do it for you."
What would be the position of any
Government that had to submit to other
people coming in to do the job for them?
What would the people of the country
think? They would say it is a rotten
Government; they cannot do their own
job, and have to bring in somebody else
who can do it. We would be doing an
injustice to our own people if we had
gentlemen brought in to value property
on the basis of Bond Street and Oxford
Street instead of on the basis of Donegall
Place, which, of course, is not as valuable
as Oxford Street. It has been said that
we are a poorer country than England.
Our valuers know that. All I have to
add is that I most heartily back the Government in bringing forward this Bill,
and I shall vote for it as long as I have
power to do so.
The MINISTER OF FINANCE (Mr.
Pollock): Before I enter into a detailed
reply to the various criticisms which have
been offered by those who are in opposition
to the principles of this Measure, I should
like to refer to the specific question put
by my hon. Friend the Mayor of Derry,
and by my right hon. Friend the Lord
Mayor of Belfast, for both of whom I
entertain very high personal regard.
Therefore I cannot turn down without
consideration any question that they ask
or any suggestion that they make. In
regard to the extension of the period, I
cannot give a definite, positive undertaking because of the fact that it would
appear to me that any attempt of the
kind to rid ourselves of our liability to
undertake this work in a serious manner
might be regarded on the other side as
shuffling on our part. But if the two
hon. Members to whom I have referred
will allow me to consider the question
between now and the Committee Stage of
the Bill I will undertake to give a
definite answer on the subject.
Passing from that matter, I may refer
for a moment to the admirable and exhaustive speech of my hon. and learned
Friend, Senator Leslie, who approached
the subject from a larger point of view
than I have been accustomed to from
speakers on this Bill. I am sure hon.
Senators will realise that after listening
to the masterly speech of Senator Leslie.
Therefore, I propose to take up the discussion of the question from the standpoint of the detailed attacks that have
been made upon the Measure, and also
the attacks that have been made upon
the Government for lending itself to the
prosecution of this Bill, apart from the
action of the Imperial Government.
I turn my attention at once to the
gentleman who initiated the discussion
yesterday, my hon. and learned Friend,
Senator Campbell. With a wealth of
rhetoric which does not harmonise altogether readily with prosaic and practical
matters, such as the discussion of financial
and fiscal matters, he entered into the
various features of the Bill and objections
to it with a wealth of detail which calls,
out of courtesy alone, for a reply from
The first point the hon. and learned
Senator touched upon was the fact that
the Report of the Financial Relations
Committee, of which Senator Leslie was
chairman, was the inspiring motive of
this Measure; in other words, bv
implication, that the wish of the Imperial
me.
Valuation Bill-
Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Mr. Pollock]
Government was not an element in suggesting to our Government that it was
necessary that Ulster should be revalued.
I want to assure the hon. and learned
Member that although the Committee in
question did report upon the propriety and
advantage of revaluation, this matter we
are now discussing was, to use a familiar
term, on the stocks before the Report of
this Committee was made, and before we
knew what the mind of the members of
that Committee was on the subject. Therefore, I wish to relieve the minds of hon.
Senators in respect of that matter; that
is to say, it was not the inspiration of this
Committee which suggested this Bill.
Events had transpired on the other
side of the water, emanating from the
Chancellors of Great Britain, and not
from the bureaucracy as suggested by the
hon. and learned Senator, although bureaucracy is a very valuable adjunct to the
Government of any country. Some five or
six years ago, possibly more, I cannot remember, I had a very courteous letter
from the then British Chancellor of the
Exchequer reminding me of the conditions that existed in Northern Ireland,
and of the necessity for placing the
valuation of the Province upon a fair,
just, and equitable basis. That was the
first direct initiation of this matter. But
the real time when valuation might and
should have been made, if it had to be
made at all, was in the year 1924 when,
not using the word "anomalies" to which
Senator Campbell has an objection, but
when so many injustices had arisen because of the inequalities that necessarily
arose from the action of the valuers who
were bound by law to have regard to the
rental value of property. Under the
conditions that existed in 1924 the valuations of property that came under revision
were of such a character as to create
injustices of the very worst kind.
In order that revaluation might not be
imposed at that time I took certain action,
and this is a matter I was myself responsible for as the person in charge of the
finances of the Province and as a member
of the Government. From my experience
I felt at that time that the very mention
of the word revaluation would have
repercussions of a very unpleasant
character-the very mention of the word.
Therefore, in order that this might be
avoided, I submitted to Parliament a
Measure resulting from the recommendations of a small Committee I had
appointed deliberately for the purpose of
recommending Measures to rectify these
injustices. This Measure, which I presented to Parliament, provided that
valuers would have regard to the value of
property in 1906. The result of that was
that all these injustices that had been
disclosed as a result of the applications of
the different parties interested were removed and matters went on perfectly
smoothly and postponed what I may
regard as the evil day. But the recommendation of that Committee at that time
definitely stated that a revaluation of the
Province was the only method by which
equality of burden could be provided for
the people of Ulster in this respect.
As I say, the Government did not feel
called upon to carry out that recommendation for the very good and sufficient
reason that it was an uncomfortable thing,
and for the very much more important
reason that, following the war, matters
had not reached stability. There was
fierce inflation on the one hand and fierce
deflation the other hand, causing
disturbances of values to such an extent
that the possibility is that if revaluation
had been undertaken at that time very
grave injustices might have occurred as a
result of that action.
Senator Campbell spoke of the lack of
backbone displayed by the Government in
not refusing to carry out the mandate of
the Imperial Government. I wish to
assure the House that the operation of
backbone in a matter of that sort cannot
be sustained by argument. and, after all,
argument and logic must be introduced in
matters with which we are daily concerned. What reply, may I ask the
House, could be made to a Chancellor of
the Exchequer either Mr. Churchill or
Mr. Snowden, because they were both
personally concerned in this matter with
myself personally-what reply could I
make to the argument that Northern
Ireland had not been valued for a period
of eighty years, and that from information
received naturally through the Inland
Revenue Department, they were aware of
the gross inequalities existing here, which
inequalities should be remedied. Hon.
Members have endeavoured to emphasise
that all this was undoubtedly an act on
the part of the Imperial Government to
grab some money from Northern Ireland.
Will hon. Members just think of this for
a moment. British Chancellors of the
Exchequer are concerned with matters of

as
In the course of that argument the hon.
and learned Member says, "Why should
we not have control of our fiscal system?"
Does he realise that fiscal autonomy is
not only the symbol but the actual practice of independent nationality, and that
no federal State in the world has control
of its fiscal arrangements, and could not
possibly have. Thank God we have not
got such control, because if we had the
first thing that would happen would be
a boundary of some kind between the
North of Ireland and Great Britain, and
under the conditions that we see coming
into operation at the present time we
would be in the position of having to face
a tariff against some of our commodities,
just as the Irish Free State will undoubtedly have to do before very long.
That possibility has induced the Members
of the Free State Government to go for
the first time hat in hand to claim from
Great Britain some special treatment on
which they depend absolutely and entirely for their very life. That is the
position in regard to our fiscal system.
The hon. and learned Member also
spoke of assimilation to Great Britain's
policy, but let me remind the House that
assimilation of our conditions to those of
Great Britain does not imply that any
special burden is to fall upon us.
You
might as well say that the inhabitants of
the county of Sutherland or of the Island
of Skye should pay more than the inhabitants of the rest of the British Isles.
If
the inhabitant of Sutherland or the Isle
of Skye has got an income he pays income
tax on it, and if he has no income he does
not pay.
That is exactly the position we
Occupy here. Where then, does the
additional burden come in? If a man
has not got the means to pay he is not
asked to pay, and he is exactly in the
same position as an inhabitant of the
City of Bradford or Leeds, or of any
other town where the wages of the people
are on the same basis as the wages we
pay in Northern Ireland. Therefore, so
far as assimilation is concerned, it cannot
press hardly upon us provided the calculations and the rates are the same.
Reference was made to the fact that
revisions had been made from time to
time since the last general revaluation, and that these revisions must necessarily impose hardship and produce
inequalities. But what is the fact?
Under that Act of 1924, to which I have
alluded, valuers were bound to have regard to the pre-war value. Therefore, no
injustice could have arisen from that, because the valuer could not go outside that
Measure which I passed through Parliament. That point, therefore, falls to the
ground entirely. Then Senator Campbell
drew a most lurried picture of the ruin of
the Belfast Corporation's undertakings,
gas, electricity, and so on, which would
all come down under the terrible weight
of taxation. But these enterprises of the
Belfast Corporation or any other Corporation pay on exactly the same basis as you
or I do. If they make a profit they
pay income-tax on that profit. If the
Belfast Corporation imposes rates on
those undertakings, as they probably do,
they pay the rates, but in regard to income-tax they are not burdened to the
extent of one penny more than I or any
hon. Member of this House. They simply
pay on their profits. We all groan under
a five shilling rate-I think it is terrible
-but they are not in any different
position to any other industry. There-

fore, to talk of ruining any of these public

The question of the rates in Belfast
and elsewhere has been referred to as part
of the general argument. The fact is
that during the past two or three years
annually in the Corporation that splendid
chairman of the Finance Committee, Sir
William Coates, who, to my great regret,
and I am sure to the regret of everyone,
is no longer with us, proudly claimed that
Belfast was rated so much lower than any
other community in the United Kingdom,
and the next day there appeared in the
newspapers leading articles, giving such
comparisons as an hon. Member opposite
has called attention to, showing that the
imposition per head of the population in
certain cities across the water was a half,
a third, and so on, more than the burden
imposed on the citizens of Belfast. Could
any public body in Great Britain responsible for the connection between Great
Britain and Northern Ireland refuse to
act upon figures such as those. Why are
the rates in Belfast so much lower than
the rates in Leeds? That is the question,
and are they able to bear any more.
I do not want them to bear any more.
. We want them lowered, and the action of
this Bill, if it passes and comes into law,
must necessarily lower them if those responsible act with that degree of prudence
which would be expected from any public
body, and which I hope will be reached
by the public bodies of Ulster.
Now I come to another question that
has been very prominently before the
people, and I am sorry to say that attempts have been made to arouse their
feelings on it, that is, the agricultural
interest. It has been touched upon
to-day here as it has been elsewhere. An
endeavour has been made to convince the
farmers of Ulster that they are going to
be hurt in some terrible way by this
Measure. There is not a single farmer
in the whole of Great Britain who will be
prejudiced to the extent of one penny.
He is fully derated and the action of derating will be maintained even when this
Measure comes into law. The rates he is
paying at the present time are, in my
judgment, higher than the rates he will
be called upon to pay when revaluation
of his small house takes place.
Let us not forget that in the Province of
Ulster there are 80,000 farmers of under
twenty acres. Hon. Members can very
readily picture the value of that small
house, and they can also if they read the
newspapers day after day see the value of
the primary commodities produced by the
farmer. Do you suppose that condition
of things will lend itself to a higher
valuation than two-thirds the value of
the whole hereditament that exists at the
present time? I call attention to these
things in detail, because they are used
through the country to arouse opposition
and to paint pictures of the most fearful
kind in regard to the operation of this
Measure; pictures that are totally apart
from actuality, and I think, if I may say
so, they should not be used to prejudice
the people against the Government, who
are doing to the best of their ability what
they can do in the interest of the whole
of the country.

But rent restriction will obtain through

reached.

Summarising the
position, if the people of Ulster will only
trust the Government, that the only
object the Government has, having regard
to the fact that this thing had to come
sooner or later and perhaps the sooner
the better, the only interst the Government has is to make it as easily borne
as it is possible for the Government to do,
by every citizen of our Northern Ireland.
The Government has no other aim and
none of the sinister objects with which
they have been charged by different
public bodies and by others. I think it
is very hurtful to Members of the Government, after trying and doing their
best for every interest as far as a Government can do their best, to be subjected
to such animadversions and allegations of
impure motives. It is a most hurtful
thing to suggest that there is something
of a sinister and objectionable character
underlying their action.
It is sometimes even suggested that I
was anxious about the result of our
accounts for the present year, and therefore this was for the purpose of getting
more money. I reply to that that no
result can be had from this for five years.
How then could it affect our accounts this
year or the year after? It is a matter
of principle, and no result can possibly
accrue in respect of finance. Finance has
nothing to do with it in the meantime
whatever may ultimately happen. It will
be five years before it can have any effect
whatever. Let me remind hon. Members
that during the currency of these five
vears there will be no entry on the valuation lists. That is to sav. until the full
completion of the whole valuation of the
Province takes place no one will be asked
to pay on a different valuation from that
which operates at the beginning. In
other words, the Department will bring
the whole thing to an end, and if the
conditions at the end of the period are
different in any way from those that
obtain earlier the people will get the advantage.
Trade prosperity may have returned.
Business may be booming, and the valuer
would be required to have regard to these
things at the end of five years. The Government is only using its judgment in this
thing and we came to the conclusion that,
having to do it, and being pressed to do
it, the next four or five years that elapse
are likely, as far as human foresight can
determine anything, to be the most satisfactory and the most favourable from the
point of view of those concerned with regard to valuation.
I hope the House will unanimously give
a Second Reading to this Measure, and
when the Committee Stage comes along
there may be some small points-I do
not think there are many-that can be
worked out quietly, calmly and sensibly,
and the Government so far as they can
will facilitate the working of the
Measure. They do not want to have any
difficulty in this thing. I hope we shall
be able to receive any suggestions that
may be made or that occur to hon. Members in Committee. I thank you for
listening to me so patiently for twenty
minutes or half-an-hour I have occupied
of your time, and as I say, I hope the
Second Reading will be carried unanimously.

Mr. CAMPBELL: Might the Minister
enlighten the House on one point? Does
he consider it essential that after the first
general valuation there should be a
general revaluation every five years?
Mr. POLLOCK: Yes, for two very
cogent reasons. The first is the cost. I
am advised by our valuers that the first
quinquennial valuation following the
general revaluation would be of such a
negligible character that they will be able
to carry out the work without any
additional cost other than that borne
ordinarily at the expense of the Department. That is one important reason. The
second is the matter of convenience. With

The Senate met at a quarter after Three
of the Clock, Mr. HILL DICKSON
(Deputy Speaker) in the Chair.
Paper Presented to Parliament.
Ministry of Home Affairs:
Report for the year 1931 on the retreat
established in Northern Ireland under the
Inebriates Acts, 1879-1900.
Presented to the House pursuant to
Act, and ordered to lie on the Table.
Valuation Acts Amendment Bill.
Order read for the House to be put
into Committee.
Moved, "That this House do now resolve itself into Committee on the said
Bill." (Mr. Long.)
On Question, Motion agreed to.
In Committee accordingly.
[Mr. HILL DICKSON in the Chair].
CLAUSE 1 (Provision for quinquennial making of general revaluation)-
(1) The Commissioner of Valuation shall
make a general revaluation, throughout
Northern Ireland, of rateable hereditaments under the Irish Valuation Acts
as soon as may be after the passing of
this Act, and shall subsequently make
such general revaluations from time to
time, so that the interval between the
dates on which one general revaluation
and the succeeding general revaluation
are respectively issued by the said
Commissioner shall be a period of five
years.
(2) The first and each subsequent
general revaluation shall be issued on
the first day of March next after the
completion thereof.
Mr. CAMPBELL: I beg to move in
page 1, line 14, to leave out "five"
("period of five years") and insert
"seven." May I take this the first available opportunity of saying I regret a
mistake I made in my speech on the
Second Reading of the Bill last week. I
was then under the misapprehension that
under the Derating Act of 1928 the value
of the dwelling-house on an agricultural
hereditament represented two-thirds of
the agricultural hereditament. That was
a mistake on my part. The hon. and
learned Senator Leslie challenged it, and
I am grateful to him for the correction.
I have looked into the matter, and I find
I should have said it represented twothirds of the value of the agricultural
buildings.
The effect of the Amendment which I
now move, if adopted by the House, would
be to make the interval between the
general revaluation and the succeeding
general revaluation seven years instead of
five years.
The Valuation Act of 1852,
which is our principal Act, contemplated
a revaluation every 14 years, not a compulsory revaluation, but a valuation at
the suggestion of the then grand juries,
now the county councils. My Amendment
involves one-half of that period of 14
years. I do submit to the House that it
would not work any injustice and would
not involve any more expense than a five
years' interval will. It would give the
tax-paying public a larger breathing
space between one general revaluation
and another one.
Mr. POLLOCK: With regard to this
Amendment I explained to the House
during the Second Reading period that
I was advised that five years, a quinquennial period, was the most suitable in
many respects, and more especially from
the point of view of cost. The valuation
authorities have no doubt whatever that
a valuation following a general revaluation is a matter of minor consequence.
The period that elapses involves so few
changes in regard to values that the work
of the Department is greatly lessened,
and, therefore, as the Department itself
will be able without outside help to carry
on the valuation every five years, it is
desirable it should remain a quinquennial
period. Practical experience on the other
side has proven beyond a doubt that the
quinquennial period is the most suitable
for the purposes of valuation. Therefore,
I suggest that the Committee will regard
my explanation as sufficient to enable
them to pass the Clause.
Mr. CAMPBELL: I do not press the
amendment.
Amendment, by leave, withdrawn.
Valuation Bill-
Committee
2 FEBRUARY 1932
Mr. LESLIE: I beg to move in page 1,
line 16, to leave out from "on" ("on
the first day of March") to the end of
line 17. On the Second Reading I drew
attention to some of the disadvantages
and some of the advantages that followed
from the doctrine of an absolutely fixed
date. I pointed out that if we were to
choose the 1st March, or any other date
for that matter, sooner or later we would
strike Sunday, and there are also perhaps
other difficulties of an administrative
character which might ensue if Parliament provided something insufficiently
elastic. The amendment on the Paper is
an attempt to get round the difficulties
I mentioned, that is, to keep the advantages and lose the disadvantages. I beg
to move.
Mr. POLLOCK: In the period since
last week, when the Bill was before the
House for Second Reading, I have had
interviews with the municipal authorities
in Belfast in respect of one of these
Clauses, and particularly in respect of
the amendment which my hon. Friend
Senator Leslie has submitted. It has
been found that the first of March is a
very inconvenient date for the local authorities, that is to say, the period for the
deposit of the valuation lists, the period
between the 1st of March and the 31st
of March is obviously quite inadequate
to enable the local authorities to prepare
all the material required for the purpose
of sending out the assessments, and after
considerable thought on the matter it was
suggested that some date later in the year
than the 1st March should be selected
for this purpose, say, 1st May or 1st
September.
That would have the advantage that it
would give the local authorities very
ample power indeed to make all the
arrangements for the changes that would
have to take place, and the only solution
therefore in respect of the Clause which
is in the Measure is to insert a provision
in the Bill enabling the first revaluation
lists to be issued at some more convenient
date. As the Bill stands at present the
local authorities would have to arrange
everything in a short time, as I have said.
But I propose, on the Report stage, to
introduce an amendment to the Bill to
give effect to these proposals. I propose
to take that course, because subsequent
Clauses in the Bill are affected by the
change, and the whole matter will have
to be dealt with comprehensively. Clauses

4, 19, and 21 all would have to be dealt
with in the revised draft, which I am
having prepared.
I would, therefore, ask my hon. Friend
Senator Leslie to be good enough to withdraw this Amendment, and also the
following one dealing with the same subject, having regard to the explanation I
have given of the proposals it is intended
to adopt.
Mr. LESLIE: By leave of the House I
most readily withdraw the Amendment,
and as the Minister has suggested, it
would be better I should not move the
following Amendment on the Paper, or,
indeed, one or two other ones which all
have some relation to this one.
I am
quite sure it will be much more convenient
to deal with the matter comprehensively
on the Report stage.
Amendment, by leave, withdrawn.

CLAUSE 4 (Making of rate according
to revaluation):
--
(1) The county and urban district
council shall, in any year in which
valuation lists containing a general revaluation are issued to them, make any
rate made by them after the first day of
March in that year according to
the said lists, and may levy and recover any sum due on account of any
rate so made, notwithstanding any
pending appeal or any notice of appeal
with respect to valuation; but where
the valuation of any hereditament is
altered on appeal the council shall, if
the valuation is decreased, repay any
sum paid in respect of the rate in

shall apply to the making of a rate by

The following Amendment stood on the
Paper in the name of Mr. Leslie :-
Page 3, line 1, leave out "the first day
of March in that year" and insert "such

Amendment not moved.
Mr. CAMPBELL: I beg to move in
page 3, lines 20 to 22, to leave out subsection (4). The object of the Amendment I have the honour to submit to the
Committee is, in substance, to prevent the
creation of anomalies and privileges in
favour of the Belfast Harbour Commissioners. This Bill is said to be for the
removal of anomalies, but sub-clause (4)
in my judgment creates anomalies in
favour of the Harbour Commissioners.
It sets out that nothing in this Section
shall be construed as affecting the provisions of Section 13 of the Belfast
Corporation Act, 1925. The effect of that
Section of the Belfast Corporation Act is
that the property of the Belfast Harbour
Commissioners is assessed in a different
way from the property of any other public
body or any private individual throughout
our area. The property is assessed on the
amount of charge on foreign shipping
arriving in the port of Belfast, the registered tonnage of the shipping, also the
amount of the registered tonnage of other
shipping entering the port. In all other
cases as far as I know property will be
assessed according to the method laid down
in the Bill, that is, on the value of the
premises in net rent from year to year.
The Harbour Commissioners will not be
assessed on their property in that sense
or by that means, and the effect will be
that the rating and valuation will vary
from year to year according to the amount
of tonnage entering the port. I understand that the amount in the past year
was only a slight increase on that of the
year before.
We know that shipping is
not in a prosperous state and is not likely
to be for some time. The tonnage might
go down in which event, while the valuation of other properties in Northern Ireland will be, it is thought, under the
operation of this Bill, appreciating, the
valuation under which the Belfast Harbour Commissioners will be rated will be
actually going down and depreciating. I
do think that under a Bill the main justification for which is said to be the removal
of anomalies we should not set up fresh
anomalies in favour of a body whose
election is not based on a democratic
franchise.
Mr. POLLOCK:
I regret that I am
unable to accept this Amendment. Unless this Sub-section appears in the Bill
the effect of the Measure will be to sweep
entirely away the principle that exists in
connection with the rating arrangements
of the harbour with the Belfast Corporation. Even the Belfast Corporation have
not made any suggestion of this kind, and
I think it is highly probable that the
matter can be more satisfactorily dealt
with under any Consequential Provisions
Bill that will necessarily appear following
this Bill in order to clear away quite a
number of small matters which the hon.
and learned Member has described as
'anomalies. But to remove this Subsection from the Bill would place both
the Corporation and the Harbour Board
in very great difficulty. Therefore, I
cannot accept the Amendment.
Mr. CAMPBELL: I will withdraw the
Amendment.
Amendment, by leave, withdrawn.

CLAUSE 9 (Amendment as to appeals
to Commissioner of Valuation).
Mr. LONG: I beg to move, in page 5,
line 36, at the end of Sub-clause (1) to
add the following Sub-Clause :-
(2) A notice of appeal under the said
section nineteen, in respect of a list
relating to the first general revaluation
received from the Commissioner of
Valuation by the secretary of a county
council or clerk of an urban district
council, may be sent or delivered to
such secretary or clerk within forty-five
days from and after the receipt of the
list by such secretary or clerk.
As stated in my Second Reading speech,
the Government proposed, in the Committee stage, to introduce a provision
enabling appeals to be made to the
Commissioner of Valuation within a
period longer than the statutory twentyeight days embodied in the present
Valuation Act. Accordingly, this Clause
proposes to increase that period to fortyfive days so far as the first revaluation
is involved. This should remove all complaint that estate agents and others would
be too rushed in putting in properly
considered appeals. I might mention
that the British Rating and Valuation
Act of 1925 only gives twenty-five days.
*
*
Amendment agreed to.

(3) If any person on whom notice has
been served in pursuance of the provisions of this section fails, without
reasonable excuse, to comply with the
notice, he shall on summary conviction
be liable in respect of each offence to a
fine not exceeding twenty pounds and

in respect of each offence to a fine not

Mr. POLLOCK: I am sorry I cannot
accept this Amendment, because its effect
would be that a property owner who had
received notices in respect of several or
possibly a number of hereditaments, and
had failed to make any return in respect

contemplated that a penalty should be

many of whom might make a blunder or
error in their return that might expose
them to prosecution. I think a fine not
exceeding £10 would amply meet the ends
of justice and serve the purposes of the
Bill.

Government cannot accept this Amendment. It must be remembered that these

that it is quite within the discretion of
the Court to impose a penalty in accordance with the character of the offence. I
understand that similar penalties are
provided for in the British Rating and
Valuation Bill, thus giving us a precedent
for our proposals in this Measure.
