This Amendment, I think, ought to
commend itself to the good judgment of
the
Committee. With this proposed
Amendment the Sub-clause will read-
If any person wilfully makes, or
causes to be made, a return under this
section which he knows to be false in
any material particular, such person
shall, without prejudice to any liability
be proceeded against under any other
enactment, be liable on summary conviction in respect of each offence to a
fine not exceeding fifty pounds.
The reason I propose this Amendment
is, that in the case of this Bill there will
be a large number of notices of increase
of valuation to be served on individual
ratepayers, many of whom will be poor
people, working-class people, not familiar
with these notices. In good faith, but
in sheer ignorance, they may make mistakes or blunders. I suggest that it
would be a wise thing, in dealing with
such a large section of the community,
to make it perfectly clear that the intention is to punish and penalise only
those who make returns which they know
to be false. These returns, involving
figures and calculations, will be a puzzle
to many people, and they are looked upon
as a serious matter, because the penalty
in respect of each offence is a fine not
exceeding £50.
The word "wilfully" is used, and
doubtless the Minister of Finance will
say that it covers the same ground as
this Amendment, and that it means returning a wilful falsehood. I think it
may not bear that meaning, and it is in
order to prevent any uncertainty of that
kind that I venture to submit my Amendment to the Committee. The Clause
states that a person shall, without prejudice to any liability to be proceeded
against under any other enactment be
liable to a fine not exceeding £50. What
other enactment is referred to? Is it
that a person is liable to a fine of £50
and also to be proceeded against under
the Perjury Act? Now a man is not liable
to conviction for perjury unless at the
time he makes the statement he knows
it to be a false, or does not believe it to
be a true statement. For the purpose of
bringing this Bill into line with the
Perjury Act I submit this Amendment to
the Committee.
Mr. MCALLISTER: I rise to support
the Amendment. I think no harm can
be done by accepting this Amendment
and doubly guarding people against the
risk of being convicted of an offence of
which they are innocent. I think the
right hon. Gentleman will agree that
the insertion of these words cannot
affect the intention of the Government,
but on the other hand will effectually
safeguard and put beyond doubt what
should be the intention of the Committee,
that an innocent person should not be
convicted and subjected to a penalty of
£50. The penalty should be imposed only
on the person who knowingly does this
thing. This is simply a safeguard, and
does not in any way run counter to the
intentions of the Government.
Mr. POLLOCK: This Sub-clause is
also taken from the British Rating and
Valuation Act, and notwithstanding the
argument of the hon. and learned Senator
(Mr. Campbell) I submit that the word
wilfully "whatever that may be—
covers the point.

Mr. CAMPBELL: It is a puzzle.
Mr. POLLOCK: It would be a greater
puzzle to discover what was in a man's
mind. I submit to the Committee it
would be quite impossible that a conviction would ensue if those words were
inserted.
Mr. CAMPBELL: They are part of
a perjury charge.
Mr. MCALLISTER: They are part of
the licensing law also.
Mr. POLLOCK: From the experience
of the other side of the Channel in valuation matters, I think the Government is
justified in following the practice which
exists there.
Amendment, by leave, withdrawn.
Question proposed: "That the Clause
stand part of the Bill."
Mr. ARMSTRONG: I am not able to
find in the Bill anything providing that
the individual ratepayer shall have notice
of any alteration that may occur in his
valuation. Under the existing law ratepayers often find it very inconvenient
when they discover for the first time
Valuation Bill-

2 FEBRUARY 1932
from the demand for rates that their
valuation has been altered. I daresay it
is posted up somewhere, at church doors
or some other place, but I think it is very

are varied.
Mr. MCALLISTER: May I say that
the same question has frequently occurred
to my mind At the present time people
may not know when their valuation is
raised, and in what way it is raised, and
I think that between now and the Report

through no great neglect on his part.

this Section the valuation of any agricultural land shall be excluded.
Mr. POLLOCK: I am sorry I cannot
accept this Amendment, nor can I see the
equity of it if we approach the question
from that point of view. Perhaps I
should explain to the Committee the effect
of the Sub-section as it stands. It is a
little difficult and complex, but I hope
I will be able to make it clear. When
the revaluation list is issued it will
contain not only the revalued figures
of all buildings, but also the existing
valuations of agricultural land and
Neither of these two
farm buildings.
items, namely, agricultural land and
buildings, is being revalued, but in
order to exclude the agricultural buildings from revaluation, they, unlike agricultural land, will cause a considerable
amount of work to the valuation staff;
in fact, possibly even more than if they
had been actually included in the
revaluation. It is, therefore, only proper
that in estimating the share of the
administrative expenditure to be borne by
the local authorities, that is say, the
county boroughs and the county councils,
the valuations of agricultural land should
be deducted before the apportionment is
made. The valuation of agricultural
buildings will remain, seeing that, as I
have explained, their exclusion from revaluation will cause a considerable amount
of work. The deletion of the Clause
would be inequitable, as it would force
the county councils to contribute their
share as though the agricultural land
itself had been revalued.
The purpose of this Amendment is, I
should judge, to relieve the Belfast
Corporation of some fractional part of
the burden which will be imposed upon
local authorities in connection with the
revaluation expenses, but, viewing the
whole question from the point of view of
the equity of the case, I think the proportion suggested is perhaps the more
equitable of the two, and the very small
difference that it will make to the Corporation of Belfast is not one that they
cannot bear.
Amendment, by leave, withdrawn.
Clause, as amended, ordered to stand
part of the Bill.

The next two Amendments are intended to clarify the wording of the

Valuation.

provisional valuations is made in accordance with the valuation appearing in the
next revision list. In the absence, therefore, of some provision on the lines of
that contained in Clause 19 of the Bill,
there is a danger that some ratepayers
might be rated on the basis of the new
valuations for a period before the new
valuation lists came into effect generally.

Mr. CAMPBELL: I beg to move, in
page 13, line 10, after "farm" ("and is
mainly farm land")
land") to insert "or
pastoral."
My endeavour is to make clear beyond
ambiguity the meaning of agricultural
land. Under Clause 2 of the Bill, agricultural land is excepted from the general
revaluation. What is agricultural land?
Agricultural land under Clause 21 is defined in this form:
"'Agricultural land' means any
hereditament or portion thereof which-
(a) is situate outside the boundary
of any urban district, and is mainly
farm land, demesne, market
gardens . .
""
etcetra. I need not read the remaining
portion, and I do not propose to read
paragraph (b), which deals simply with
land within the boundary of an urban
district. The object of this Amendment
is to make clear that pastoral land is
brought within the scope of this Bill.
The Irish Land Acts are a code very
familiar to Irish farmers, and are still
within the memories of the older Ulster
farmers. In those Land Acts one after
another, beginning with the great revolutionary Act of 1881-and even going back
to the Act of 1870, which is so important
to our tenant farmers dealing, as it does,
with the Ulster tenant right-and coming
down to the Act of 1896, you will find
land always dealt with in this form of
words:
"agricultural or pastoral land."
That phrase occurs again and again, and is
looked upon as the main definition of land
under the Irish Land Code. Those are
the two great boundaries or divisions that
apply to and embrace land of all classes,
and which leave no loophole for ambiguity.
In this Bill the words "agricultural or
pastoral land" do not occur from beginning to end. In our Rating and Valuation Act of 1928 agricultural land is
defined in a very clear way that leaves no
room for ambiguity. Agricultural land
under that Act is taken to be any
hereditament outside the boundary of an
urban district which is entered as land in
the valuation lists and which is not part
of an industrial hereditament.
You see
that there the broad and all-embracing
definition is given that it means all land
which is entered as land in the valuation
lists. That comprehensive definition takes
in both agricultural and pastoral land.
The Local Government Rating and
Finance Act of 1929 adopts the definition
of agricultural land from the Act of 1928,
and, therefore, you have the same definition in both the Northern Ireland Acts.
If you look at the English Act, to which
this matter is largely analogous, you will
find that in the English Rating and
Valuation Act of 1928, in Section 2, Subsection (2), land is defined in a very
clear way that leaves no room for misapprehension in any mind, either legal or
lay; and now that we have followed the
English example so very closely in other
matters connected with this Bill, I hope
I will be able to carry conviction in this
matter. Their experience in England has
made them wise in matters of this kind,
to use the argument that has already been
used against me, and I now ask the
Government to follow the English Act in
this connection. The definition in the
English Act is that agricultural land
means land used as arable, meadow, or
pastoral.
Mr. POLLOCK: Where is that quoted
from?
Mr. CAMPBELL: That is from the
English Rating and Valuation Apportionment Act, Section 2, Sub-section (2). I
ask the Committee simply to adopt the
definition in the English Act in which
land is defined in a way that leaves no
room for mistake. It means land that is
used as arable, meadow, or pastoral, and
covers all varieties of land. I need not
read the rest of the definition. If there
is any mistake in this matter, or any
loophole for ambiguity, and a person
comes up for an interpretation of it the
result might be that a large portion of
the land in Ulster-as for example,
dairy farms and grazing holdingsmight be held not to come within the
scope of this Bill at all. I do not for the
moment see why we have departed from
the terminology so well established under
our Irish Land Acts, or why we should
adopt a different definition here to that
contained in the English Act. Land,
according to this Bill, is merely farm
land. That, I submit, to a townsman's
mind, means land that is cultivated or
Valuation Bill-
Committee
2 FEBRUARY 1932
tilled. After all, "farm" is an old Anglo-
Saxon word feorm," which means
"food," so that if you look at it in its
English signification it may not extend to
pastoral land. And if you take the Latin
derivation of agriculture it means land
that is cultivated. There is room for
ambiguity as the definition stands, and
it is simply to remove any misconception
that may arise that I move the Amendment.
Mr. POLLOCK: I cannot think for a
moment that the draughtsman had anything sinister in his mind in respect of
the omission of the words "or pastoral
land" when he framed this definition of
agricultural land as being
"situate outside the boundary of any
urban district, and is mainly farm land,
demesne, market gardens, nursery
grounds.
and so on.
""
Mr. CAMPBELL: I do not suggest
that there was anything sinister behind
it.
Mr. POLLOCK: I know you do not,
but I think most hon. Senators present
would regard farm land as inclusive of
land under grazing. Any farm of which
I have any knowledge is either a grazing
farm or partially a grazing and partially
an arable farm. However, I do not see
that there is any particular significance
in the Amendment, and to satisfy the
hon. and learned Member I think the
words could reasonably be introduced into
the definition. I do not see any objection
to it at all.
Mr. LESLIE: I wonder would the
hon. and learned Senator (Mr. Campbell)
mind substituting the words "including
pastoral" for "or pastoral." I think
that would make it a little safer.
Mr. CAMPBELL: I am quite content
to accept that.
Amendment, as amended, agreed to.
Mr. CAMPBELL: I beg to move, in
line 18, after "farm" ("used as farm
land") insert "including pastoral.”
Amendment agreed to.

CLAUSE 23 (Saving for proceedings
on appeals to courts).
The coming into operation of any
provision of this Act shall not affect
any proceedings taken by way of appeal
to any court against or in respect of
the valuation of any hereditament and
pending at the date on which such
provision comes into operation, and
paragraph (n) of article thirty-seven
of the Local Government (Adaptation
of Irish Enactments) Order, 1899, shall
apply in the case of any such pending
appeal.
Mr. LONG: I beg to move, in page 15,
line 19, at end, add the following Subsection: :-
"(2) Nothing contained in this Act
shall affect the operation of section
twenty-two of the Telegraph Act, 1868,
or section one hundred and ninety-four
of the Public Health (Ireland) Act,
1878."
The reason for this addition is that
the Telegraph Act of 1868 standardised
the valuations of telegraph installations
at the figure at which they were taken
over by the Government, and rates are
paid on the valuations then in force. It
is not possible for this Parliament to alter
this provision inasmuch as the Post Office
is a reserved service, hence this saving
provision. The second part of the Subsection continues the somewhat gruesome
protection afforded to burial grounds by
the Public Health Act of 1878, which
fixed the valuation of such places as
though they were agricultural land.
Amendment agreed to.
Clause, as amended, ordered to stand
part of the Bill.

given, not press the Amendment.

Questions to Minister.
FINANCES OF NORTHERN
IRELAND.
Mr. CAMPBELL asked the Leader of
the House whether he can state the annual
revenue of Northern Ireland on the existing basis of taxation, the present public
debt of Northern Ireland, the total assets
now held by the Government, and the
total loans guaranteed to date under the
Loans Guarantee Acts in favour of (a)
shipbuilding concerns, and (b) other
concerns.
Mr. LONG: The estimated revenue in
the current year is £11,478,000. The
present public debt, including the estimate
of accrued interest, is £9,089,000.
The total assets now held amount to
£3,409,000, excluding Pensions and
Health Insurance Funds, which amount
to £2,278,000, giving £5,687,000 in all.
The figures given are based on the present
depreciated market values of the stocks
held.
The total of loans guaranteed to date in
favour of shipbuilding concerns is
£11,326,587, of which £2,862,244 has
been repaid, leaving £8,463,743 current;
and in favour of other concerns is
£129,070, of which £45,300 has been
repaid, leaving £83,770 current.
There is a contingent liability in respect
of Ulster Loans Stock for £6,000,000, of
which £2,358,000 lent to the Exchequer
is included in the amount of the public
debt.
LAW RELATING TO COMPANIES.
Mr. LESLIE asked the Leader of the
House if he can state whether it is the
intention of the Government to make any
further alteration in the law relating to
companies before the Consolidating Bill is
reintroduced.
Mr. LONG: The Government is aware
that there is a feeling in some quarters
that the law governing companies should
be strengthened in certain directions, so
Oral Answers
Oral Answers
9 FEBRUARY 1932
as to prevent abuses such as came under
the notice of the Courts in Great Britain
during the last year or two. Opinion as
to the amendments that should be made is
not fully crystallised as yet, but the
Government is of opinion that there is no
reason why the Consolidation Bill should
not be proceeded with during the next
Session. Apart from other considerations
it is desirable that company law in
Northern Ireland should conform
closely as possible to that in Great
Britain.
GRANT TO ROAD AUTHORITIES.
as
Colonel SHARMAN - CRAWFORD
asked the Leader of the House whether he
can state by what amount the grant from
the Government to the Road Authorities
will be reduced this year.
Mr. LONG: It is not proposed to alter
the rate of percentage grants from the
Road Fund payable to Road Authorities,
i.e., 60 per cent. for class I roads, 30 per
cent. for class II roads, and 15 per cent.
for class III roads, which are generally
used for motor traffic.
At the same time, owing to the depleted state of the Road Fund, it would
not be possible to pay the grants at these
rates on the basis of road programmes for
the last two years, and it is hoped, by
negotiation and consultation with the
Road Authorities, to arrange for a curtailment of programmes of road works by
deferring works which are not of
immediate importance.

THE WHEAT QUOTA.
Mr. CAMPBELL: In putting the
question standing in my name, may I state
that we have heard something in this
House during the past fortnight of what
has been called the Imperial repercussion
on the Valuation Bill. I hope in the
information the Deputy Leader of the
House will give to-day we will hear something of the Ulster repercussion on the
wheat quota. It is a matter of very
great importance to the people of Ulster,
especially to the farmers. I observe the
Prime Minister last night said the
National Government had made a good
beginning. Well, as far as Ulster
farmers are concerned, it has begun with
the wheat quota. It is a matter of very
great consequence to the farming population of Ulster. I beg to ask the Leader
of the House whether, in the year 1931,
3,035 acres were under wheat in Northern
Ireland, yielding 2,842 tons; whether
wheat grown in Northern Ireland is unsuitable for flour for breadmaking;
whether representations have been made
to the Government by the Belfast Millers'
Association that the wheat quota proposed
by the Imperial Government will raise
the price of bread per 4 lb. loaf by at
least one halfpenny; whether this rise
will mean an extra charge of approximately £600 a day or £200,000 a year on the
people of Northern Ireland; whether this
will involve a dead loss, as the farmers
can derive no possible advantage from the
quota, the entire cost being passed on by
importers and millers to the public; and
whether, in view of these facts, the
Government will make representations to
the Imperial Government on the subject.
Mr. LONG: The figures quoted by the
hon. and learned Member are correct.
It is true that wheat grown in Northern
Ireland is unsuitable for the milling of
flour for breadmaking. With regard to
the latter part of the hon. and learned
Member's question, the Government has
discussed with representatives of the
Belfast Millers' Association the effect of
Report Stage

Valuation Bill-
THE SENATE
[Mr. Long]
the proposed Wheat Quota Bill on the
Northern Ireland Milling trade.
As no
information is yet available as to the proposed price to be fixed for home-grown
wheat of milling quality under the Quota
Act, it is not possible to say what effect
these proposals will have on the price of
the 4 lb. loaf. The Government is continuing to take all possible steps to see
that Northern Ireland interests in this,
as in other matters affecting the Province,
are properly safeguarded. (Hon. Members: Hear hear.)
Valuation Acts Amendment Bill.
Order for receiving Report of Amendments read.
Moved, "That this Report be now

On Question, Motion agreed to.
CLAUSE 1 (Provision for quinquennial making of general revaluation).
(1) The Commissioner of Valuation
shall make a general revaluation,
throughout Northern Ireland, of rateable hereditaments under the Irish
Valuation Acts as soon as may be after
the passing of this Act, and shall subsequently make such general revaluations from time to time, so that the
interval between the dates on which one
general revaluation and the succeeding
general revaluation are respectively
issued by the said Commissioner shall
be a period of five years.
(2) The first and each subsequent
general revaluation shall be issued on
the first day of March next after the
completion thereof.
""
Mr. LONG: On page 1, line 13, I beg
to move to leave out from "revaluation
("general revaluation are respectively")
to "shall" ("shall be a period") in line 14,
and insert "respectively come into force."
This is the first of a whole series of
Amendments designed to bring about the
change in the Bill, which my right
honourable Friend, the Minister of
Finance, announced in the Committee
Stage, indicating at the same time that
the necessary drafting alterations would
be moved in the Report Stage of the
Bill. As the Bill at present stands it
provides for the issue of a general revaluation on the 1st March, and its coming
into operation for rating purposes one
month later, namely, on 1st April. The
Government is now satisfied that in the
case of the first revaluation such a short
interval of time is likely to be wholly
inadequate to enable the local authorities,
especially the Corporation of Belfast, to
carry through the many, and, it may be,
radical changes which will have to be
made in rate books, adjustments between
contributing areas and so forth. These
difficulties will not, however, be recurrent, because the alterations that will take
place on the occasion of the later revaluations will, obviously, be of a more normal
character.
Accordingly, the changes in the Bill,
which will be moved, relate only to the
first general revaluation, and are designed
to extend the period between the date
when the list is issued and the date when
it becomes operative for rating purposes.
It will be observed that the date of issue
of the first list is left blank, that is, it
is to be prescribed by regulation of the
Ministry of Finance. This will allow the
question to be thoroughly examined in
the interval with a view to deciding
whether an appropriate date will be as
close as possible to the 1st March,
originally proposed, or some date later in
the calendar year, but in either event the
list will not operate for rating purposes
until the calendar year following the date
of issue.
The Amendment will further provide
for the rate being struck on the old basis
in the year in which the first revaluation
list is issued. When the list actually
comes into force it will not be the list as
issued, but a revaluation list on which
have been grafted the annual revisions
which have taken place in the months
following its issue. With one or two
small exceptions the three pages of
Amendments, which the Senate now has
before it on the Order Paper, are all
directed to this end, and the House will,
doubtless, not expect from me any
detailed observations on most of the
changes, as they will in reality be obvious.
I beg, accordingly, to move the first
Amendment on the Order Paper.
Mr. LESLIE: As the Parliamentary
Secretary has intimated, this is the first
of a series of amendments, and as therefore it is a little difficult and perhaps
unnecessary to separate the amendments
in one's mind, one may as well say on
the first amendment as on any other what
one desires to utter. Taking this as the
first of a series, I should like to say the

Mr. LONG: I beg to move, in Clause
1, page 1, at the end of line 17, to add
the following sub-section :-
"(3) The Ministry of Finance shall
make regulations-
(a) prescribing the day on which the
first general revaluation shall be
issued;
(b) providing that notice of the first
general revaluation shall be given
to such persons, and in such form
as may be prescribed;
(c) prescribing the method of issuing
general revaluations;
""
(d) providing that public notices
shall be given before and after the
issue of a general revaluation;
(e) providing that a complete set of
the lists containing a general revaluation shall be deposited in some
suitable place for record purposes."
This new Clause is designed firstly to
leave open for regulation the date on
which the first General Revaluation is to
be issued. It also covers points made by
Senator Leslie in an Amendment which
he had on the Order Paper in Committee,
but which, at the suggestion of my right
hon. Friend, the Minister of Finance, he
did not move, namely, provision is made
for prescribing the method of issuing
revaluation, for giving public notice, and
for deposit of complete sets of the lists.
In sub-clause (b) there is also included a
provision which my right honourable
Friend the Minister of Finance, promised
to give consideration to when the point
was raised in Committee, namely, the
giving to interested parties of notice of
the effect of the first General Revaluation
list. These points were raised by Senators
Armstrong and McAllister on the Committee stage, and I trust this Amendment will be satisfactory.
Mr. ARMSTRONG: I am not quite
clear as to what this Amendment provides
for. Does it empower the Ministry of
Finance to give the individual ratepayer notice both before and after
Valuation Bill-
Report Stage
THE SENATE
[Mr. Armstrong]
the issue of a general revaluation,
because that is the point I wish to
press upon the Minister.
When rents
were being fixed postcards were sent to
the tenant and landlord, giving the date
on which the valuer would come on the
land, and also when the rent was fixed
they received postcards giving the amount
of the rent. There is a similarity between
that and this general revaluation. In the
former case it was a matter of fixing the
value and rent of a house, and now it is a
case of fixing the valuation for rating
and taxation purposes.
The system
worked very satisfactorily. Although
there might have been some little expense
it would be only half on this occasion,
because there would be only two postcards
in each case, whereas on the former
occasion there were four postcards. If
my suggestion is carried out I think it
would tend to simplify the working of the
Act and it would certainly give general
satisfaction. It may be the Ministry will
have power to do this under the Regulations, but I am not quite clear about it.

Mr. LONG: Paragraph (b) carries out

throughout the country and they are also
published in the newspapers. There is no
other form of public notice contemplated.

Mr. POLLOCK: We have had discussions with the public authorities in

Valuation Bill-
Report Stage
THE SENATE
[Mr. Long]
property owners are overpressed with

Mr. MCALLISTER: As a resident in
an urban and rural area I fail to see why
under this particular Clause different
treatment should be provided for the cities
of Belfast and Derry, as compared with
other areas. When such a differentiation
between these two cities and the urban
and country districts is made I think some
explanation should be forthcoming. I do
not see why the same facilities for appeals
should not be given to the people living
in the country and in the provincial towns.
as are afforded to the people in these
cities. No reason has been given for this
difference in procedure, and I do not
think there can be any justification for
it. Therefore, I would ask the Minister
of Finance to reconsider the matter before
the Third Reading and to give the same
facilities for appeals to residents in the
urban and rural areas as he proposes to
give to those in Belfast and Derry. I
think those who live in the country require
more time and greater facilities for
appeal than do those who live in the two
cities referred to. People resident in the
cities are immediately adjacent to the
source from which notice of appeal is to
be lodged. From day to day they are
within a moment's walk from a solicitor,
but the people living in a rural area are,
perhaps, only in the market town on
market days. If there is any justification
for different treatment, then it would be
better to extend the time to the people in
the rural areas rather than to restrict it.
I would ask the right hon. Gentleman to
consider that point between this and the
Third Reading.
Mr. CAMPBELL: I should like to
reinforce the argument that has been
made. Under this Amendment the city
of Londonderry will have ninety days for
the purpose of considering notice of
appeal to the Commissioner. The County
Council of Londonderry, a very important
body, will, however, only have forty-five
days, and all the other county councils
will have forty-five days. The little city
of Londonderry has a period of ninety
days to make up its mind to appeal. The
city of Belfast counts for a great deal,
but is it possible that in a matter of this
kind the county borough of Belfast and
the city of Londonderry will overshadow
all the other county councils of Northern
Ireland? I would like some other Member to stand up for the county councils.
Mr. POLLOCK: I do not think this is
a matter of shadowing or overshadowing.
It must be quite obvious to the House
that there is much greater concentration
of work in a large city like Belfast, and
also Londonderry, than in the rest of the
smaller urban areas. It has been found
by experience by the valuation office that
there is a tendency on the part of people
to leave appeals to the last moment, that
is to say, a tremendous rush comes along
just as the period of expiration comes on,
and, therefore, it is desirable, if this
additional time is permitted for the big
city of Belfast and the comparatively
large city of Londonderry, to avoid any
such rush as I have mentioned. I think
the House will agree that considerable
latitude is given here for the purpose of
appeal as regards the rural districts, and
no injustice can be done if forty-five days
in one case and ninety days in the other
case is provided.

Mr. CAMPBELL: I wish to ask, with

the exact effect of Sub-clause 2 will be.
It reads:
Provided that the Commissioner of
Valuation may make any such change

I take it the real intention of the Amendment is to make it clear that though the
farm offices and the land on which they
are built will not be liable-there is no
ambiguity about this-the farm dwelling
will be brought within the scope of the
Bill.

Mr. LONG: I beg to move in page 17,
line 18, at end (end of Article 3) to add :
(5) Where, in the case of any hereditament (or part of a hereditament)
being agricultural buildings, it is found
that by reason of paragraph (a) of
section three of the Rating and Valuation (Apportionment) Act (Northern
Ireland), 1928, or for any other cause,
such buildings have not been distinguished as agricultural hereditaments
in the valuation lists in force during the
year preceding the issue of the first
revaluation, the Commissioner of Valuation shall revise the valuation of such
buildings as if the first valuation were
not required to be issued in the following year, and the amount of the
valuation as so revised shall be entered
in the lists containing the first revaluation.
The addition of this Sub-clause (5) is
not consequential on the main Amendment, but is in reality to provide for an
eventuality which was not foreseen in the
original draft. It will be noted that
there are already provisions in Sub-clauses
(3) and (4) of this Article which will
enable corrections of fact to be made in
the valuation lists a power necessary
because of the rather "rule of thumb"
method adopted in the Rating and Valuation (Apportionment) Act, 1928 (which
was the preliminary stage to derating), in
order to save time and money-that is to
say the two-thirds and one-third arrangement. The existing valuation lists were
taken as actually representing the facts
at the time, even though a farm house
might have been converted into farm
offices or vice versa. No provision, however, was made in these two articles for
the case of buildings which, although in
reality agricultural buildings, were for
some reason or other not so described in
the apportioned lists.
The addition of this Sub-clause will
now enable the position to be regularised,
and is, in fact, in favour of the ratepayer.
This is to allow a correction to be made
where, for example, a dwelling house has
been converted into an agricultural hereditament but has remained in the lists as
a dwelling-house, and is, therefore, subject to revaluation and to payment of
rates. Senator M'Allister will, no doubt,
be aware of instances in which farmers
having two farms have discontinued the
use of a dwelling-house which has become
office houses. It is to meet a case of that
sort we have put this in.
Amendment agreed to.

spectively come into force.'

THE SENATE.

Thursday, 11th February, 1932.
The Senate met at a Quarter after
Three of the Clock, Mr. SPEAKER in
the Chair.
Papers Presented.
Ministry of Labour:
Order in Council made under the
Economy Act (Northern Ireland), 1931,
with respect to National Health Insurance.
Ministry of Commerce:
Lisburn Gas Order, 1932.
Laid before the House (pursuant to
Act), and ordered to lie on the Table.
Question to Minister.
THE PROPOSED TARIFF ON
IMPORTED MAIZE.
Mr. CAMPBELL asked the Leader of
the House whether 380,000 tons of maize
were imported into Belfast from foreign
countries in 1931; whether foreign maize,
meal and maize products are the principal
feeding stuffs used by farmers in Northern Ireland for all farm stock; and
whether the Government have had any
communication with the Imperial Government with regard to the latter's
proposal to place a duty of ten per cent.
on maize from March 1st next, or the
grave consequences of that tariff to the
farming and milling industries of Northern Ireland.
Mr. LONG: The quantity of maize
imported into Belfast port during 1931
was 374,000 tons. The statement of the
hon. and learned Member that foreign
maize and maize products are the principal feeding stuffs used by the farmers.
in Northern Ireland is correct. In view
of the paramount importance of the live
stock industry to Northern Ireland the
Government is taking every possible step
to secure the continued free importation
of all feeding stuffs for animals.
Mr. HILL DICKSON: I am aware
that it would not be in order on this
question and the answer given by the
Deputy Leader of the House to raise a
debate on the subject of tariffs, but I
think it would be most detrimental to the
interests of the agricultural community
if a tariff was placed upon maize. According to the Bill, as far as we understand it
at present, bacon and pig products are to
be allowed in free, but if there is going to
be a tariff put on the imported stuffs that
produce the bacon and pig products, it
would be the most iniquitous thing that
ever happened to the agricultural community in this district. I am satisfied,
from the answer given by the Government
that they are looking after the interests
of the agricultural community, and I hope
that the Agriculture Department of this
country will not sanction or agree to
anything in the nature of tariffs on maize.
Valuation Acts Amendment Bill.
Order for Third Reading read.
Mr. LONG: I do not propose to say
very much when moving the Third Reading of this Bill. The details have been,
I think, very fully discussed in the
previous stages, and the amendments
which were introduced have been made
with a view to meeting the reasonable
representations of interested parties. It
only remains for me to commend the
Measure to the House, and I, therefore,
beg to move.
Question proposed, "That the Bill be
now read the Third time."
Mr. CAMPBELL: Before we pass the
Third Reading of this Bill, I would like
to say
that we all appreciate the value of
the contributions made to our debates by
the right hon. Gentleman, the Minister
of Finance. (Hon. Members: Hear,
hear.) We see him here too infrequently,
and no one could be here during the
course of the debates on this Bill without
admiring his intellect. We have no
reason to revise our valuation of him.
The Third Reading may pass this
evening, or it may not. It all depends
on whether or not, even in the final
stage, hon Members pluck up courage
to assert their Own views against
the views of those who from their
Olympian mansions on Stormont look
down upon this Province. So far they have
won in this battle, and the elected public
authorities and the trade bodies have
been worsted. That much must be conceded. But this remains to be said. I
Valuation Bill-
Third Reading
11 FEBRUARY 1932
venture to think the Government dare
not go to the constituencies on this Bill,
such is the strength of the feeling so far
as it has found articulate expression
through the elected public bodies in this
Province or the interests of the mercantile,
trading, and industrial community.
The Minister of Finance thought it
ridiculous to suppose that Whitehall
would be concerned with a bagatelle of
£200,000 or so a year of extra revenue.
Does anyone really think that is the
height of the Treasury expectations? If
they do, all I can say is the force of folly
can no further go.
The Financial
Relations (Senator Leslie's) Committee
reported that the revaluation of buildings
would save the Northern Exchequer about
£50,000 a year in derating grants. That
burden would be shifted from the State
to what may be called the rate-paying
ratepayers. We are getting lost in words.
With regard to ratepayers, we have rated
ratepayers, derated ratepayers, underated
ratepayers, non-derated ratepayers until
we are beginning to think there are no
ratepayers. I am prepared to call those
who are left ratepaying ratepayers. That
burden will be transferred from taxes to
rates. It will be transferred from the
central authority to the local urban
authorities. I am informed by a person
in good authority that in his county alone
the transfer will mean an extra 2/- in
the pound on the rates of the urban districts. The time will come in 1937 when
the notices of increases in valuation will
begin to fall like snowflakes, and the
public here will see that this Bill has
set up a perfect machine, worked by expert hands, for the continuous extraction
by Whitehall of every penny of revenue
that can be extracted from Ulster. Ulster
will thenceforth pay, pay, pay.
our
Moreover, the assimilation of
standard of valuation with the British
standard will leave its mark on the prosperity of this Province. Our valuation
system is to be fused with the British
system. May I make use of a simile for
a moment? We talk of steel as a symbol
of strength, of tenacity. If engineers
want to get the utmost strength of
tenacity out of steel, what do they do?
They do not fuse it into one mass. No,
they draw it down into wires, and then
bind the wires together and make a rope
of steel, which will hold against a pull
that will snap a bar of steel like a thread
of silk. The Government has forged a
rigid bar of steel, likely to snap under
strain. This Bill shows we are not free
to shape our fiscal affairs. The Imperial
tariffs show that we cannot protect our
own farmers.
Mr. LESLIE: I find myself in complete agreement with the hon. and learned
Senator who has just sat down, in so
far as he has voiced on our behalf our
great obligations to the right hon. Gentleman, the Minister of Finance, whose
attendance throughout our debates has
been, as the hon. and learned Senator
has said, of the greatest possible value
to us, and, perhaps I may add, value to
the Province or to that part of it which
takes the trouble to read the debates in
this House.
But I am afraid that that compliment
to the right hon. Gentleman is the only
thing in the speech of the hon. and
learned Senator with which I find myself
in agreement. In all other matters I
must admit-and I admit it without
shame that I am entirely on the opposite
side. I think that this Bill when it has
become an Act, and when it has come
into operation, will be universally recognised to be not in any way an engine of
oppression, but it will be found of great
help to our ambassadors when they go to
confer with the Treasury authorities in
Great Britain.
The fact that we shall have a revaluation on an honest basis cannot fail to
be of the greatest service to us when those
whom I have called our ambassadors go
to confer across the water with those
opposed to them. With a fresh system
of valuation behind them these ambassadors need not be afraid to speak with
their enemies in the gate. As regards
matters to which the hon. and learned
Senator particularly alluded, that is
to say, that suggest that our standard
of values here in Northern Ireland shall
be adjusted to the standard valuation in
Great Britain, I would venture very respectfully to remind him that the
standard of valuation has since 1852 been
the same. It has been the letting value
-the annual value-as defined in Section
11 of the Act of 1852. Royal Avenue-
I am afraid it was not there in 1852had at all events its similarity to Bond
Street. Whether it was in Bond Street
or in any other street, the letting value
was the letting value. The rents need
not have been on the same scale. They

Mr. MCALLISTER: I had not intended to intervene in this debate at this
stage, except for the speech made by the
hon. and learned Senator (Mr. Leslie).
As the hon. and learned Senator is well
aware, every public body in Northern
Ireland-every great commercial association in Northern Ireland-has protested
against the passing of this Measure in
language unparalleled in its violence in
these ordinarily very meek associations.
I refuse to accept the view that the public
representatives, who are elected by the
population as men who know their needs
and as men of average intelligence, are
not men capable of forming a proper forecast of the efficiency with which this Bill
will operate. The hon. and learned
Senator will pardon me if I say with all
respect to him that these great commercial
associations and public bodies are people
who have a long record of administrative
work in Northern Ireland, and I think
have the opportunity of guaging what
will probably be the effect of any Bill that
comes before this House. I refuse to
accept the opinion that these people are
incapable of forming a perfectly logical
and, I believe, a true view of what is
going to be the effect of this Bill on the
people of the Province.
With regard to the question of letting
value, it is quite true that whether the
premises are here or in London, the test
or method by which they are to be valued
is the yearly letting value, but I do say
and repeat that no more inopportune time
could have been chosen for a false estimate
to be put on the property of the ratepayers and taxpayers of Northern Ireland.
As I said before, on the Second Reading,
the rents and value of property have
soared to such a height-a height which
the people of the Province could not bear
-that the Government have felt constrained to step in and pass temporary
legislation to try to save the people from
this false and artificial rising of the
letting value of property in the Six
Counties. No one in this House has more
respect for the Minister of Finance than
I have, but I do say that notwithstanding
all his skill and ability he cannot do the
impossible, and he cannot meet the argument that there could be no more
inopportune time to introduce this Bill
and no worse time in the interests of the
people of Northern Ireland.
Does the right hon. Gentleman really
expect the House to believe that it is only
on the question of £200,000 that the Imperial Government is forcing this Bill on
the people of Northern Ireland. Why,
if £120,000 is raided from the Road
Fund, which is a comparatively trifling
thing compared with the value of the
property to be dealt with under this Bill,
how many times over £120,000 will be

Mr. BARCLAY: I am not going to
occupy the time of the House very long.
There has been a good deal said for and
against the Bill, and I do not suppose I
am in a position to add anything to what
has been expressed. All I am going to
do is to associate myself with my three
friends who represent the working-class
people in Northern Ireland. They have
given their approval to the Bill. So do
I. I have observed the Press and I have
not found any trades union organisation
which has made a protest against the Bill.
I have had conversations with the
working-class people regarding the
Measure, and all I have come in contact
with have given it their approval. They
зeem to think that this Bill is the right
thing and one that is long overdue. The
hon. Senator (Mr. McAllister) said that
it is a most inopportune time to bring
forward a Bill like this. I think this is
the best time to bring it forward. He
mentioned the rising market of rents and
house property. No doubt rents have
risen, and the value of house property
has risen, but we are on the turn.
House property is coming down in
value and rents are coming down. I am
living in a rural district and I know that
many new houses have been put up within
the last twelve months, but they are
lying there with no one to buy them. In
fact there is no money to buy them.
People who bought houses some time ago
are sorry they did so. They are having
conversations with those who would like
to buy houses, and the conversations are
something like this: "Have nothing to
do with purchasing your own house; they
are too dear; hold back; the prices of
houses will come down." There is a
number of houses in my rural district the
valuation of which is anything from £10
Speaking on behalf of the working
I would
class people, I support this Bill.
like to emphasise what our friend (Mr.
Quigley) said on the last day. He made
a plea to the Minister of Finance to remember working-class houses with valuations from £8 to £26, to see that nothing
happens to them, and that their rents
will not be increased. Some of these
houses I have just mentioned are up to
£20 and £22 valuation. The valuations
are really too high. The reason for that
high valuation is that the houses were
built for the purpose of receiving a free
second class railway ticket for ten years.
They could not receive that ticket unless
the valuation was over £20, and the
valuation was put up to £20, £22, and
£23. I hope when the valuer comes
round he will not increase the valuations
of those houses. To my mind they are
£6 or £7 too high. I have great pleasure
in supporting the Third Reading.
The MINISTER OF FINANCE (Mr.
Pollock): Courtesy demands that I should
reply by making some comments on the
remarks that have been made by my hon.
Friend opposite. Senator Leslie has
summarised the Bill in language so unmistakably cogent and clear to the intelligence of anyone in respect to the
character of the Bill and its ultimate
effects that possibly what I may say may
be regarded as something in the nature
of repetition, but at anyrate, as I remarked, I think it is proper at this stage
that I should not permit the contentions
of my hon. Friend immediately opposite
to go without some categorical explanation
or contradiction. Senator Campbell
rather scorned the idea of Whitehall asking
that a paltry £200,000 should be drawn
from the inhabitants of Northern Ireland.
May I remark that the Whitehall authorities do not to-day draw a single penny
from Northern Ireland, and even if
£200,000 were the result of this Bill and
Valuation Bill-
Third Reading
THE SENATE
[Mr. Pollock]
were derived from this work, the denizens
of Whitehall, if I may use that word
without any attempt at lowering their
prestige, would not be receiving one single
penny from the inhabitants of Ulster.
Perhaps that is sufficient to satisfy even
my hon. and learned Friend (Mr. Campbell) that the operation of this Bill is
not going to work such tragic effects as he
apparently apprehends.
The hon. and learned Senator also
assumed that the people of Ulster were
opposed to this Measure as a whole. I
have reason to believe from my experience
gained during the past month or six
weeks that he is wrong in that assumption. The farmers, I know, are perfectly
satisfied that their position is secure so
long as derating continues, and there is
no likelihood of any resumption of the
status quo ante. So long as that is maintained agriculturists need not fear that
this Act will have any effect upon their
lives or interests.
The hon. Senator (Mr. Barclay) has
given some very effective figures in respect
of the possible effect of this Measure upon
the working man. Let me remind the
House of something which, perhaps, is
forgotten, as it is very easy to forget it.
There are 75,000 houses in Belfastlimiting our discussion to Belfast for the
moment that are under control. If you
assume the normal population in each
house you get at least three-fourths of
the inhabitants of Belfast, and not one of
these will suffer any hardship; and from
the contact I had a few days ago with the
most intelligent body of working men I
have had the pleasure to meet for a long
time, I am perfectly satisfied that the
working people of this Province not only
do not fear the result of this Measure,
but actually feel that it is something that
is long overdue. Therefore, if you take
these rough figures I am offering to the
House will find the great mass of the
you
people of Ulster are, if not satisfied with
the Bill itself, at least quite satisfied that
their interests are not going to be affected,
else it is perfectly obvious we would have
heard something from them long before
this.
We must admit that property owners
may be affected. Assuming that the
divergence between the valuation of their
property and the rents receivable by the
owners exists, obviously they must pay
taxation upon the same basis as all other
citizens of the community. Although I
do not hold a brief for any portion of the
community, it cannot be argued that any
small section in the Province should be in
such a privileged position that they should
escape the necessary taxation which the
vast majority of the people who are
within the income-tax limits have to pay.
I think that is a very effective argument
for the prosecution of this Measure.
But, after all, five or six years, while
they are very short in retrospect are very
long in anticipation, and it is difficult for
me or anyone in this House to prophesy
what the relations of the local authorities
and the Government will be at that time,
because the changes in recent years have
been so kaleidoscopic that no human being
can properly estimate what will be the
ultimate relationship of the local authorities and the Government. We too often
forget, I think, in discussing the question.
of the local authorities vis-à-vis the
Government, that the people of Ulster,
whether they are ratepayers or taxpayers,
if not necessarily the same individuals
are the same people as a whole, and if
money is to be raised by taxation or by
rates, as the case may be, to a large
extent it is the same people who pay.
Therefore, this question of imposition
upon the ratepayers is really largely out
of place until we can discern and discover
the exact relationship existing between
the Government and the authorities
carrying on the local affairs of the
Province.
Now, in regard to the general question
of the Bill, I think we may safely leave
it over to the time the Measure comes
into operation. The opportuneness or
inopportuneness of the time when the
valuation lists will be in the hands of the
local authorities I do not think need concern us to-day. As the hon. Senator who
spoke last said, house rents are falling
and during the period in which investigation will be made in regard to the
values of property here in Ulster, that is,
the four or five years that lie ahead of us,
the anticipations from those who
apparently know, or think they know,
that is the estate agents and those people
familiar with property, are that we are
on the eve of a very important reduction
in the rentals of house property. Therefore, we need not anticipate the terrifying
fears which have been indulged in by my
hon. Friends.
I thank you, Sir, and the Members of
the House for the patient way in which

Papers Presented to Parliament.
Ministry of Labour:
The Unemployment Insurance (Economy) (No. 1) Order (Northern Ireland),
1931-providing for an increase in the
rates of Unemployment Insurance contributions and a decrease in the rates of
Unemployment Insurance Benefit, with
effect from 5th and 8th October, 1931,
respectively.
The Unemployment Insurance (Economy) (No. 2) Order (Northern Ireland),
1931-providing for the limitation of
Unemployment Benefit to 156 days in a
benefit year, subsequent payments to be
termed transitional payments and to be
made from the Exchequer to the extent
that the Public Assistance Committees
certify that the claimants are in need of
assistance by way of such payments.
The Unemployment Insurance (Economy) (No. 3) Order (Northern Ireland),
1931-providing for the removal of certain anomalies in the operation of the
enactments relating to Unemployment
Insurance.
The Unemployment Insurance (Insurance Industry Special Scheme) (Amendment) (No. 2) Special Order, 1931.

Prorogation of Parliament.
Moved, "That the Gentleman Usher of
the Black Rod be commanded to inform
the Commons that the Proclamation proroguing the present Session of Parliament
is to be read forthwith, and inviting the
Commons to attend in order to hear the
same."—(Mr. Long).
On Question, Motion agreed to.
The Commons being at the Bar, with
their Speaker, the Proclamation was read
to both Houses of Parliament by the
Speaker of the Senate (in pursuance of
His Grace's command), as followeth :
:-
BY HIS GRACE THE GOVERNOR
OF NORTHERN IRELAND.
A
PROCLAMATION
For Proroguing the present Parliament
of Northern Ireland.
WHEREAS it is provided by Section
eleven of the Government of Ireland Act,
1920, as modified by and in pursuance of
certain enactments amending the said Act,
that the Governor of Northern Ireland
shall in his Majesty's name summon,
prorogue and dissolve the Parliament of
Northern Ireland:
AND WHEREAS it is desirable that
this present Parliament of Northern
Ireland should be prorogued to the eighth
day of March, One thousand nine hundred
and thirty-two:
NOW I, JAMES ALBERT EDWARD,
DUKE OF ABERCORN, KNIGHT OF
THE MOST NOBLE ORDER OF THE
GARTER, KNIGHT OF THE MOST
ILLUSTRIOUS ORDER OF ST.
PATRICK, Governor of Northern Ireland, by this Proclamation, do publish
and declare his Majesty's Pleasure that
the said Parliament be, as from the
reading of this Proclamation in the
Parliament of Northern Ireland, prorogued to Tuesday, the eighth day of
March, One thousand nine hundred and
thirty-two.
Proclamation
End of Session
18 FEBRUARY 1932
AND I do hereby further declare his
Majesty's Royal Will and Pleasure to be
that the said Parliament shall on the said
eighth day of March, One thousand nine
hundred and thirty-two, assemble, and
be holden for the despatch of divers
urgent and important affairs, and the
Senators and Members of the House of
Commons of Northern Ireland are hereby
required to give their attendance accordingly.
Given this thirteenth day of February,
One thousand nine hundred and thirtytwo.
(Signed) ABERCORN.
Signed by his own hand.
GOD SAVE THE KING.
End of the Third Session (opened

Tuesday, 22nd November, 1932.
The Fifth Session of the Third Parliament of Northern Ireland appointed to
meet at Stormont on 22nd November,
1932, in the Twenty-third year of the
Reign of King George V., was opened by
the Governor (His Grace the Duke of
Abercorn, K.G., K.P.) in person.
The House met at Twelve of the Clock,
Mr. SPEAKER (the Right Hon. Viscount
Bangor, O.B.E., D.L.) in the Chair.
KING'S SPEECH.
His Grace the Governor, being seated
on the Throne, commanded the Gentleman Usher of the Black Rod to let the
Commons know "It is His Grace's
pleasure that they attend him immediately
in this House."
The Commons being at the Bar with
their Speaker, His Grace delivered the
following Speech from the Throne:-
Members of the Senate and of the
House of Commons.
It gives me particular satisfaction to
inaugurate this Session of the Parliament of Northern Ireland while the
hearty welcome to His Royal Highness
The Prince of Wales is still ringing in
our ears.
His gracious act in coming to us and
declaring this building open, on behalf
of His Majesty The King, and his
visiting the people, has endeared him
still further to them, who deeply
appreciated the opportunity afforded
them of again demonstrating their undying devotion to the Throne. The
enthusiasm and rejoicings of the community were striking features of the
happy occasion.
I desire to record my appreciation
of the services rendered by the Forces
of the Crown and to congratulate the
Royal Ulster and Special Constabulary,
as well as the Stewards who lined the
route, on the admirable arrangements
made for the convenience of the public.
I congratulate you on the amenities
of your new habitation, in which you
will be privileged in future to conduct
the legislative affairs of this part of the
United Kingdom.
To turn to matters affecting the
future-the state of agriculture, linen
and ship-building, continues to be
governed by those adverse World conditions which for several years past
have exercised so baneful an influence
upon every country. It is my ardent
hope that the ratification of the Agreements reached at Ottawa may mark the
beginning of fresh prosperity for the
farming community and those of our
industries upon which the well-being
of the people so largely depends, and
may lead in turn to the removal of
some of the obstacles which have
hitherto impeded the development of
A
King's Speech
THE SENATE
our trade with countries outside the
Empire.
While the number of persons seeking
employment through the Employment
Exchanges showed an increase during
the first half of the year, I am gratified
to learn that in the summer a turning
point was reached, since when a steady
fall has been recorded. The increased
employment was mainly due to the
activity in the linen trade and available evidence leads me to expect that
this may be maintained.
As regards shipbuilding, there is an
improvement to record and it is to be
earnestly hoped that, with the progressive obsolescence of tonnage, new
construction will become inevitable and
that our local firms will secure the
share to which their high reputation
entitles them.
I should like to refer to the valuable
contribution to employment which
local authorities and public bodies are
continuing to make, by putting in
hand, with the assistance of my
Government, works of public utility,
which will prove of general and lasting
benefit to the community.
gress
I am pleased to learn that, as a
result of anxious consideration and
negotiations which have been in profor some considerable time
between my Government and the responsible local bodies, steps have been
taken to formulate a revised basis or
standard of outdoor relief in respect of
certain areas which should alleviate
the present distress.
My Government have carefully considered the views of local authorities on
the subject of proposals for local government reform. In deference to their
views and the fear that any comprehensive scheme might involve additional burdens being imposed on the
rates, my Government have decided
not to do more than introduce
measure dealing with matters of an
urgent character and remedying
certain defects and anomalies in the
various codes bearing upon local
government administration.
a
Legislation will be introduced during the session to enable schemes for
the marketing of agricultural produce
to be promoted in Northern İreland.
A measure will be laid before you
to give effect before the 31st March
King's Speech
next to the various operations on our
funds necessary to implement the Budget proposals approved last Session,
including power to make advances
from the Consolidated Fund to the
Road Fund.
A Bill will also be introduced for
the better regulation of moneylenders,
together with measures dealing with
the following matters:-
Amendment of the Bankruptcy
Law.
County Officers and Courts.
Dangerous Drugs.
Destructive Imported Animals.
Fisheries.
Regulation of Stormont Estate.
Unemployment Insurance.
Members of the House of Commons.
The Estimates for the Public Services will be laid before you in due
course.
Members of the Senate and of the
House of Commons.
Notwithstanding the many difficulties with which our manufacturers
have been confronted the industrial
life of the community continues to be
substantially free from interruption by
trade disputes. The accommodating
spirit shown by employers and employees in settling questions of wages
and conditions without recourse to a
stoppage of work is most praiseworthy.
And I pray that the blessing of
Almighty God may rest upon your
deliberations.
Then his Grace was pleased to retire,
and the Commons withdrew.
House adjourned during pleasure.

Address in Reply to His Majesty's
Most Gracious Speech.
Mr. SPEAKER having read the
King's Speech,
move
Mr. LESLIE (in the uniform of a
Deputy-Lieutenant): I beg to
"That an humble Address be presented
to His Grace in the following words:-
"Your Grace,
We, His Majesty's most dutiful and
loyal subjects, the Senate of the Parliament of Northern Ireland, beg leave
to offer our humble thanks to Your
Grace for the speech which Your Grace
has addressed to both Houses of
Parliament."
We meet to-day to open a fresh
chapter in that book of history which we
began some eleven years ago when we met
for the first time as one of the constituent elements of the newly-constituted
Parliament of Northern Ireland in
Belfast City Hall. I feel that a great,
I may say a signal, honour has been conferred upon me in the invitation which I
have received to make the first speech
from the floor of our new Chamber. T
know that my powers, alike of thought
and utterance, are inadequate to satisfy
the requirements of so epoch-marking an
occasion as this, the first meeting of our
Senate here at Stormont, and therefore I
must ask leave to cast myself upon the
consideration and forbearance of the
House.
The circumstances justify-even, perhaps, demand-something of retrospection, but my principal subject must be
the Gracious Speech and the thoughts.
Debate on the Address
Debate on the Address
THE SENATE
[Mr. Leslie]
which it awakens. That Speech refers to
the tremendous happenings of the last
few days, and those happenings carry
our minds back to the opening of the
newly-created Parliament of Northern
Ireland in 1921, when our people's loyal
and heartfelt salutations to their
Majesties, the King and Queen, rang in
our ears just as our people's recent
greeting to the people's Prince is ringing
in our ears to-day. We rejoice to have
had these opportunities of voicing our
loyalty and devotion to the Crown and
the Royal Family, for that loyalty and
devotion is the cherished inheritance of
Northern Ireland-the birthright of the
people which has never yet been sold or
bartered, and which will never be
surrendered. And we like to tell each
other and to tell the Empire, and the
world, about it.
To me this magnificent building appears
as a monument not only of the art and
competence of its architect and builders,
but also of the faith and honour of two
Parliaments. It represents the fulfilment
of that promise of the Parliament of the
United Kingdom embodied in the wording
of Section 34 of that Statute which is so

we may say, is recognised by this building.
which stands to-day, and will remain for
years to come as a conspicuous token
that, as His Royal Highness was so good

Parliament has not disappointed the hope
which His Majesty the King expressed
eleven years ago.
I think that we may fairly claim to
have made, and to have maintained, good
title to the House of Promise.
But we
had no need to press any claim at all.
Our title was never in dispute. Throughout the negotiations, and in the arrangement, design, and actual construction of
the building we were met, not half-way,
but more than half-way, by the authorities
across the water. We have been treated,
I may say, with a generosity which is
almost as affectionate as it is Imperial, and
the culmination came last week when His
Royal Highness the Prince of Wales himself came to Northern Ireland to be
present at the official transfer of the
building, to declare it open, to address
the people, and to move amongst us. His
Royal Highness, as will be remembered,
addressed us in our own central hall, and
gave us the gracious message which His
Majesty had commissioned him, as his
direct representative, to give, that
message to which I have already referred
as being an acknowledgment that we had
not disappointed his hopes.
I do not suppose that anybody who
was present on Wednesday last in this
House can possibly forget the spectacle
he beheld. The memory of that brief and
impressive ceremony will, I think, remain
with him for ever. People who came
rejoiced, I am sure, at the opportunity
of welcoming the representatives of his
Majesty's Government in Great Britain.
They were pleased to see the Home Secretary, and the First Commissioner of Works,
to both of whom a good deal is owing in

the older ones will recall with special

Cushendun, and Londonderry were of the

regret that Sir William Moore was
not well enough to be present with his
comrades of former fighting days. I
desire most heartily to congratulate the

the complete and splendid success which
has crowned their consistent efforts to
produce the result which we behold.

Agriculture, too, finds a place, and
indeed agriculture at present is in a very
sad condition. I doubt if there ever was
an occasion when prices were so low, and
the unfortunate producer finds himself
unhappily without return for his capital.
We shall welcome, I am sure, any wellconceived scheme which seeks to obtain its
object through the goodwill, not the compulsion of the producer, and which limits
itself to the interest of the producer, and
does not lend itself to further exploitation on the part of the middleman. Such
a scheme will meet with universal support.

Mr. LAVERY (in morning dress): In
rising to second the Address of Thanks
for the Gracious Speech from the Throne
I feel deeply the honour paid to me, a
very junior Member of the Senate,
though by no means in years, of being
entrusted with this important duty. I
feel the compliment is further enhanced,
by having at this early stage in our proceedings the privilege to address the
Members of the Senate in our new
Parliament Buildings.
If I may, I should like to congratulate
the hon. and learned Member on his
eloquent and appropriate words in
moving the Address. It is most appropriate at the moment that special
reference be made to the recent visit of
His Royal Highness the Prince of Wales
for the purpose of opening on behalf of
His Majesty the King that beautiful and
noble pile of buildings known as the
Houses of Parliament for Northern Ireland. We are grateful to His Royal
Highness for honouring us by his presence, performing the opening Ceremony,
and promising another visit.
I was much impressed whilst I sat
within that magnificent entrance hall and
heard the Right hon. Ormsby-Gore,
First Commissioner of Works, Westminster, hand over, and the noble
Viscount Craigavon (Prime Minister)
accept for the people of Northern Ireland such a stupendous gift, that at the
moment I felt a strong impulse to rise
and cheer at the magnanimity of England,
and her greatness in being true to her
character and obligation in carrying out
her Treaty with Northern Ireland. Thus,
thanks to the Mother of Parliaments,
we have for all time, or while granite
and stone lasts, legislative buildings.
built and dedicated to our use.
The problem of the long continued depression in agriculture, linen and shipbuilding calls for earnest consideration
by all the Members of this assembly,
irrespective of party. Farmers, we all
know, are suffering, not from inferior or
small crops, but from inadequate prices,
such as twopence per stone for potatoes,
whilst they are retailed at fourpence.
The retailer should have a fair profit,
but surely not one hundred and over per
cent. There are many other farm products sold in the same proportion of
profit, and I understand this is fairly
general. Whilst on the subject of farm
production I cannot, nor have I ever
been able to understand why we have to
import so much flax yarn into this, the
nursery and manufacturing centre of the
most handsome of linens and damasks the
world over, with the best land, the very
best water, and a climate unequalled for
retting and bleaching. I think it is
surely up to those in power to have this
state of affairs put right. I am in favour
as in shipbuilding, of some kind of subsidy or guarantee for this, the most important of our key industries. The money
would not be lost; of this I am satisfied,
and it would in the end be a saving to
the Ministry of Labour account. As outlined in the Speech, we are all looking
forward with the highest hopes to the
results of the Ottawa agreements in the
hope that they will be of assistance to
agriculture and linen.
With regard to shipbuilding, I am much
in favour of continued assistance by
guarantee. It is, to my mind, money
well spent, because it provides employment for a class of men whom we all know
are clamorous for it. I am satisfied that the
facilities afforded by the Loans Guarantee
Acts have contributed largely towards
providing employment, and I hope the
Government will be able to see its way
to continue this temporary assistance.
We are all glad to know there is a slight
improvement, and though scrapping the
obsolete is sometimes expensive, let us
hope in this case it will in the long run
prove to be economy.
Unemployment in most trades is still
with us, acting as a night-mare, and
though we had a steady fall in the figures
during the summer months, this has not
been quite maintained. At the present
moment the cotton industry, a kindred
industry to linen, so far as female employment is concerned, because when healthy

it gives enormous employment to women
in the Counties of Down, Antrim, and
Armagh, is and has been in a feeble state
of health for a long time past, brought
about largely through much stuff from
Manchester, the seat of the industry,
being shipped to Eastern countries in the
web for cutting up, hemming, smoothing
and ornamenting all at very low wages
and long hours. I am sorry to say I see
very little hope of the return of that we
have lost. We are holding on tenaciously
to the remainder, but, unfortunately, at
a loss. It is obvious this cannot go on.
It is well that all should know the true
state of this once important industry from
which Belfast and some of the provincial

Utility works by public bodies have

lowest rate of interest and the longest term

be
Outdoor relief is, as most of us know, a
very knotty subject. I have always been
favourable to money relief and not relief
in kind, principally for the reason that in
provincial towns and in the country the
people are known, and if they abuse the
help, which is seldom, the relieving officer
gets to know and with good results. The
aged and the infirm, with doctor's line,
are favourably considered and helped
accordingly; but the young and ablebodied unemployed is a problem difficult
to solve. I am prepared to admit it calls
for more general help than is provided for
at present. Local Government administration could, for the present, be left alone
without undue discomfort to the individual or to local bodies.
It is with much satisfaction we learn,
as we had expected, that all will in due
course work out favourably for the
implementing of the Budget at the end
of March next. I should think that
every Member of this House is pleased
and delighted with the promise of a Bill
being introduced for the better regulation
of moneylenders. Other Measures are
outlined, all of them worthy of the fullest
consideration, especially that relating to
an amendment of the bankruptcy law,
which to all honest men calls for drastic
changes. In bringing in such a Bill the
Government is at least bringing forward
a Measure which has long been needed,
and which will be of very valuable service
to the commercial community if it is, as
I am sure it will be, a Bill to do away
with some of the blatant absurdities which
exist at present. For example, a person
may get through his bankruptcy with the
payment of 1/-, and he can set up the
next day in his wife's name or in an
assumed name, without any penalty.
This is simply putting a premium on the
dishonest trader, and is a heavy handicap
on the honest trader to carry on his business in a fair and just manner. I do
not wish to criticise our courts, but the
delays seem to me unnecessary. In conclusion, I should like to express my sincere
thanks for the consideration with which
I have been heard by hon. Members.
beg to second.

VISCOUNT CHARLEMONT (Leader
of the House): It is my duty in the first

express the gratitude which I personally

brings to bear upon the various matters
which are submitted to this House. He
has fulfilled his task to-day with all the
eloquence and facility which we have
learned to expect from him. It is, I
think, only fitting that one who has been
so prominent in our deliberations since
the establishment of our legislature
should open this, the first debate in our
new home.
The hon. Senator who seconded the
Motion, whilst comparatively new to our
assembly, has already earned our esteem,
an esteem which is not by any means
lessened by the manner in which he has
to-day carried out the duty entrusted to
him.
