The tenor of the Gracious Speech from
the Throne continues, alas, to
governed, as it must be, by the influence
of the depression of trade and commerce
which we, in common with the other
countries of the world are experiencing.
This House will share my regret that the
dark cloud of unemployment still casts.
its depressing shadow over the land. It
can only be effectually dispersed and
driven away by a genial trade wind, a
phenomenon which we, in common with
all civilised peoples, profoundly hope for.
The Government is not, however, merely
watching the heavens for portents of its
arrival; it is actively pursuing its policy
to create employment, particularly for
those who most need it. I refer especially to the encouragement and financial
assistance given to local authorities to
promote works of public utility.
The
benefits which we derive, as a community,
from the carrying out of such schemes
are not to be measured in material terms
only. There is the gain in the improved
morale of those who have been without
work for a protracted period. The serious
effect of enforced idleness on personality
and skill is clearly recognised. Since
this House met in June last schemes of
which the total cost is estimated
£552,554 have been approved.
at
In the last month or so every endeavour has been made, and will continue
to be made in the future, to secure the
accleration by local authorities of such
schemes as I have mentioned with a view
to easing the position during the winter
months. In this connection I am sure
I am expressing the gratification of the
House at the recent decline in the unemployment figures. In July last the figures
were 76,854; in October-the latest figure
available they were 69,934. I feel sure
hon. members will share my hope that
this welcome decline will continue.
The Royal Commission, which has been
examining exhaustively the working of
the Unemployment Insurance scheme in
Great Britain, has now issued its final
Report, and, as the present provisions
of the scheme come to an end in June of
next year, the Government of the United
Kingdom has announced its intention to
introduce, during the coming Session at
Westminster, amending legislation which
will necessitate corresponding legislation
by the Parliament of Northern Ireland
in view of the correspondence between
the two schemes in Northern Ireland and
Great Britain. In order to maintain the
identity of the schemes, it will be
necessary also to give effect here to the
small interim amendment in the scheme
which has been made in Great Britain in
order to secure the observance of more
uniform standards by public assistance
committees in the assessment of the needs
of applicants for transitional payments.
The points on which uniform standards
are to be secured are in connection with
disability pensions, workmen's compensation and the calculating of savings.
My
Distress became very acute during the
year and presented to the poor law
authorities a very difficult problem.
right hon. Friend the Minister of Home
Affairs kept in touch with the situation
through the various boards of guardians
who were fully alive to the condition of
affairs, and special measures were taken
in many districts to alleviate the distress
caused by trade depression. The situation was, of course, most accentuated in
the city of Belfast where an Exceptional
Distress Order has been in operation for
some years, and where special arrangements were in operation whereby the
local bodies concerned, with the assistance
of the Government, provided schemes of
work known as Distress Relief Schemes
on which applicants for outdoor relief
can be given work. These schemes are,
of course, apart from the ordinary Relief Schemes assisted by the Ministry of
Labour.
Throughout the summer the number
of applicants for outdoor relief in Belfast.
rapidly increased, and complaints were
made that the allowances given by the
Board of Guardians were insufficient.
Conferences were held between the
Ministry of Home Affairs and the Board
of Guardians to investigate this very
difficult question and to arrive at some
basis on which allowances should be
given. It is important to note that,
while it is very desirable that there should
be some recognised standard for dealing
with cases, it would be neither desirable
nor proper to fix a definite scale, as it is
essential that the circumstances and the
Debate on the Address
Debate on the Address
22 NOVEMBER 1932
merits of each particular case should be
taken into account. It would be fatal, 1
think, to dispense with investigation of
individual cases or with adjudication on
the merits of each case.
Such a procedure would lead to extravagance and
injustice.
Considerable progress had been made
towards arriving at a basis for the
allowances when advantage of the situation was taken by a certain section to
organise disturbances in the city. It
cannot be doubted that these disturbances
were part of a preconceived plan which
was put in operation not merely in the
City of Belfast but in other areas in Great
Britain. The disturbances in Belfast
were dealt with firmly but with great
forbearance by the police authorities
and order was soon restored. In the
meantime the
the Ministry pursued its
negotiations with the Board of Guardians,
and ultimately a standard of relief was
evolved which was fair to those who,
through no fault of their own, are unable
to obtain employment and have to resort
to the Poor Law Authorities for
assistance.
At the same time another serious
problem has presented itself in connection
with the administration of special outdoor relief in the city. For some time
the burden of work thrown upon the
Guardians has become very great. It is
one of the cardinal features of the Poor
Law Code that the Guardians should personally deal with each case, and this now
necessitates the special outdoor relief
committees sitting many hours a week
and it involves that the members of the
committees should devote more time to
this voluntary
voluntary work than they can
possibly be expected to spare from their
own businesses. In these circumstances
it has been necessary for the Guardians
to arrange to set up an organisation to
which they can delegate much of this
work, and the Guardians are engaged in
setting up this organisation as rapidly
as possible.
During recent months the position of
the farming community has unfortunately
been rendered even more difficult than
hitherto as a result of the continued fall
in prices, especially in the cases of cattle,
sheep, pork and potatoes. But although
the past year has been one of disappointment to farmers, owing to the continued
deflation in prices and restricted demand,
it has also been one of encouragement
by reason of the important changes in the
fiscal and agricultural policy of the
United Kingdom, which have laid the
foundation for a rapid return to conditions of greater agricultural prosperity
in the near future.
The completion of the new policy with
regard to agriculture had to await the
result of the negotiations with the
Dominions which took place at Ottawa
this summer, and at which Northern Ireland was represented. The Ottawa
Agreements Act which has just become
law has substantially increased the duties.
on certain classes of agricultural produce
entering this country, notably butter,
eggs and apples.
As a
In regard to imports of meat, the
Imperial Government has preferred to act
by means of the quota rather than by
means of a tariff. During the past week
or two important negotiations have been
taking place with representatives of the
great meat importing firms, and with representatives of the countries which
supply the United Kingdom with the
bulk of our supplies of bacon.
result of these discussions a substantial
reduction will immediately be effected in
the imports of meat and of bacon.
Moreover, a considerable number of
schemes for the better organisation of
agricultural activities in this country are
at present being considered. The most
important of these is undoubtedly the
scheme for the re-organisation of the pig
industry, which, it is hoped, will bring
about a rapid expansion of pig production
in this country, coupled with a progressive restriction in the volume of imports of bacon and hams.
Throughout all the discussions in regard
to agricultural policy and the changes
in the fiscal system which have occurred,
the interests of Northern Ireland farmers
have been specially guarded. In order,
however, that full advantage may be
taken of the new conditions a greater
degree of organisation in order to ensure
the efficient marketing of agricultural
produce is essential. It is proposed,
therefore, to introduce an Agricultural
Marketing Bill which will enable farmers
and other agricultural producers
producers to
exercise a greater degree of control over
every stage in the marketing of their
produce than has been possible in the
past.
A reference in the Gracious Speech to
the subject of local government reform.
will be noted. A general scheme for the
re-organisation of the Local Government
Debate on the Address
Debate on the Address
THE SENATE
[Viscount Charlemont]
Services was contemplated as a result of
the deliberations of the Local Government Commission, and was placed before
the representatives of the local authorities.
Whilst local opinion was not unfavourable
to the general scheme subject to the
discussion of details, it became evident
that there was a very strong feeling that
the present was not an opportune time
for dealing with this question, since it
was felt that any such scheme must involve a considerable initial expenditure,
and for the first few years would entail
augmented annual charges. In view of
the financial stringency and depression
the local authorities urged strongly that
this scheme should be deferred until a
more favourable opportunity.
There are, however, a number of miscellaneous matters not involving expenditure which call for remedy. The
Local Government Act of 1898, which is
the basis of Local Government Law in
Northern Ireland, has provided in the
main a most satisfactory piece of legislation, but since 1898 there have been
many changes in conditions and there are
many points of importance with regard to
which legislation has become out of date.
The Bill which it is proposed to introduce
will deal with a large number of these
miscellaneous points which have arisen as
a result of experience, and with regard to
which representations have been received.
from local authorities.
The Bill for the amendment of the
bankruptcy law proposes to limit, as in
England, the absolute priority over ordinary debts hitherto enjoyed by the Crown
in the distribution of the property of a
bankrupt. The effect of this Bill will be
that instead of the whole balance of a
Crown debt, such as income tax, being
recoverable as at present, the Crown will
only be entitled at most to one year's debt
in full, and afterwards to a dividend along
with the other creditors. This reform
recommended by the Bankruptcy
Law Amendment Committee appointed in
1926, but was not included in the Act
passed in 1929 to give legal sanction to
the majority of the recommendations of
that Committee. The delay has been due
to the fact that the Parliament of Northern Ireland had not power to deal with
the matter, which concerned reserved
taxation; but these powers have now been
secured by the passage at Westminster of
Section 6 of the Northern Ireland (Miscellaneous Provisions) Act, 1932.
The Bill dealing with dangerous drugs
is a necessary consequence of the enactment of the recent Dangerous Drugs Act
by the Imperial Parliament. The latter
Measure is designed to give effect to the
Convention held at Geneva in July, 1931,
for the purpose of supplementing the provisions of the previous International
Opium Conventions, which had for their
object the more effective control of the
traffic in dangerous drugs. It will be
realised that it is of the first importance
that the control of the traffic in dangerous
drugs in Northern Ireland should be no
less effective and rigorous than in Great
Britain, and for this purpose it is essential that the law in Northern Ireland in
this respect should follow in strict conformity with legislation in the Imperial
Parliament. The Bill, which it is now
proposed to introduce will, therefore,
apply to Northern Ireland provisions corresponding to those of the recent Imperial
Act.
I need not, I think, delay the House
further regarding the provisions of the
other Bills mentioned in the Speech from
the Throne, the text of which will in due
course be laid before you for your consideration.

Speech. The Constabulary and the
Special Constabulary receive some little
remuneration for their services when acting in that position, not but I agree they
deserve this for the capable way in which
they carried out their duties; but I do
think that those loyal citizens who offered
their services that day, the stewards who
lined the route are undoubtedly entitled
to our very best thanks. The arrangements in those districts which were lined
by these stewards were extremely efficient,
and while they were only working men
wearing a purple badge they discharged
their duties in a highly creditable
manner, which I am sure could not fail
to have impressed His Royal Highness.
Reference is made in the Gracious
Speech to the state of the local shipbuilding industry. While we are very much
indebted to the Northern Government for
the encouragement they have given to
provide employment in the shipbuilding
industry, through the medium of the
Loans Guarantee Acts, at the same time
I think the employers themselves have
done more than their share to obtain
orders for ships. I think they have put
forth every effort, and done everything
they possibly could to obtain work for the
city of Belfast, and I think we ought to
recognise the great services they have
rendered to the workers of the city.
The plight of the agricultural workers
is also referred to in the Gracious Speech.
Although I am a citizen of Belfast I
should be very glad if something could
be done to improve the lot of the farmer
and the agricultural worker. I recognise
that at the present time the agricultural
industry is in a very bad state. For their
produce they are receiving very little for
the money, time, and labour they have
expended on its production, although at
the same time the consumer is paying
a very fair price for that same produce.
It, therefore, follows that an unfair proportion of the profit is going into the
pockets of someone, and it would be a
very good thing if this disparity in the
price the farmer receives and the price
the consumer has to pay could be rectified
so that the farmer would receive a better
return for his produce.
The Speech from the Throne also contains a reference to an amendment of
the Bankruptcy Law. That is a matter
that requires strict and immediate attention. One feels very sore to find that a
trader convenient to one's own home,
who, through high living and extravagance, is unable to pay his debts, settles
with his creditors by paying 2/6 or 31in the pound, while the honest trader is
required to pay 20/- in the pound. It
is not fair that an unscrupulous trader
should be able to pay his just debts by
paying only 2/6 or 3/- in the pound,
while the honest trader is compelled to
pay 20/- in the pound. If the proposed
Bill has the effect of remedying this state
of affairs it will give great satisfaction
to the trading community.
In conclusion, I should like to say how
pleased I am to be one of the original
Members of the Senate, and to have been
spared to meet in this magnificent new
building. The completion of the new
Parliament House has been rather delayed, because when we first met in the
Belfast City Hall, and afterwards in the
Assembly's College, we thought it would
be only a year or so before the new
Houses of Parliament would be ready for
occupation. A number of years have
passed since then, but nevertheless I am
very pleased indeed to be able to be present in the very elaborate and very comfortable building which has now been
provided for us, and to enjoy the very
pleasant and comfortable accommodation
that has been allotted to the Members of
this House. I feel very proud to be here
this afternoon, and to meet some of the
other original Members of the Senate.
Mr. QUIGLEY: I should like to join
in congratulating the mover and seconder
of the Address upon the way in which
they have carried out their duties. We
are all glad to see Senator Leslie, who
takes such a keen interest in our affairs.
He does not hesitate to devote his valued
services to the public welfare, and it is
a pity that we have not more gentlemen
of his standing and influence interesting
themselves in the affairs of Ulster.
Senator Lavery has spent a lifetime in the
public service, and we are pleased to see
him here to-day as active and as ardent
as ever.
I am sure we are all sorry that the
outlook is not brighter. Unemployment
is still rife in our midst, and it is little
consolation to know that the industrial
depression is world-wide. We hear our
Northern Government severely criticised
by Nationalists and Socialists, but I hold
that our administrators are not to blame
in any way. They have done everything
humanly possible to grapple with a grave
situation, and are deserving of our
warmest thanks.

Debate on the Address
THE SENATE
Mr. Quigley]
May I give the House a few particulars

as to what the Government has done.
Existing industries have been helped in
various ways. For instance, loans
amounting to about £11,000,000 have
been guaranteed in order to encourage
shipbuilding, and the linen trade has
materially assisted. We regret that our
shipbuilding yards are in such a depressed
state, and hope that there may be a
speedy return to better times.
It is
deplorable that some Nationalist Members seem to rejoice at the idleness of our
shipyards and other works. Quite
recently in the British House of Commons
Mr. Devlin represented Ulster as a
derelict in the industrial shipwreck."
That is not the way to attract British
industrial firms to place orders with us.
Yes, it looks as if the Nationalists would
like to see the Ulster State become bankrupt and the Ulster people reduced to
such straits that they would be ready for
incorporation in the Free State. However, I will not pursue that subject
further.
As I have said, our Northern Government has worked hard to create employment and relieve distress. Free sites are
now available out of moneys provided by
Parliament, and option is given to local
authorities to forego rates for a certain
number of years. Through the Ministry
of Labour, upon which has rested a heavy
responsibility, over £100,000 is paid
weekly in unemployment insurance and
social services, and of this amount
£49,550 is expended in Belfast. That
does not suggest that the Government is
unmindful of the people. Since May,
1922, no fewer than 857 public utility
schemes have been approved in Belfast,
and 774 outside Belfast at an estimated
cost of £7,980,494.
Hon. Senators know how agriculture
has been aided. I think the work of the
Ministry of Agriculture is beyond all
praise. Let me give a few figures. The
value of the annual production of eggs
and poultry is in the neighbourhood of
three million pounds, of which rather
more than one-half represents income
from exports. The yearly output of cattle
is valued at two million pounds, pork at
£1,600,000, sheep and wool at £600,000;
while the production of milk and butter is
reckoned at slightly under three million
pounds. Those are interesting figures
and speak trumpet-tongued of the effective
efforts of the Ministry. The Electricity
Board appointed by the Government is
also rendering splendid service, and soon
we shall have a network of electric cables
throughout Northern Ireland providing
cheap supplies for both light and power.
I might also refer to our educational
system which has altered in a most
marked degree the material conditions in
which the pupils receive their instruction,
especially owing to the provision of better
buildings and the attention now bestowed
upon the pupils' health and comfort.
Perhaps I should here say that we are
all indebted to our Prime Minister, who
has steered our ship of State with such
conspicuous success during the past eleven
years. I am sure we all hope that the
events which have occurred in international affairs during the past few years
may now begin to have an effect in lightening the gloom which has been hanging
over the industrial world, and should this
be so and affairs become more and more
active, there is not much doubt that Northern Ireland in its many ramifications
will be one of the first countries to feel
the benefit.
We
I should not care to sit down without
referring to what may be regarded as the
most important event in our recent his-
Parliament
tory-the opening of this
building by the Prince of Wales. We
were all delighted to see the Prince.
knew he would receive a magnificent welcome, but the demonstration of loyalty
and affection quite astonished some cross-
Channel people. I am afraid that across
the water a large number of people couple
us with the Free State, and think that
we are just as disloyal as those in the
South. Well, they had an eye-opener,
and the Prince has opened for us a building which will stand for all time as the
embodiment of a great Imperial tradition,
and as the symbol of our unique constitutional position within the Empire. Of
course we are charged by our political
opponents with separating ourselves from
the rest of Ireland. It cannot, therefore,
be too often asserted that we were not
the cause of the separation and the setting
up of a parliamentary boundary. The
people who brought about the separation
were the Free Staters and Republicans.
They broke away from the United Kingdom. They did not want King George
to be their King. They wanted no connection whatever with the British Empire.
On the other hand, we in Ulster maintained, in the face of strenuous opposition,
our place in the United Kingdom. We

stuck loyally by the King, and for so
doing we are now condemned by our
political enemies.
Well, such condemnation will not
affect us very much. Nothing will
weaken our loyalty and devotion to the
Crown and Constitution of Great Britain.
It was significant that even in Northern
Ireland the Nationalist and Socialist
Members declined to join in welcoming
the Prince of Wales. They showed the
cloven hoof. They showed what we
might expect if we in Ulster came under
the iron heel of a Parliament in Dublin.
But I don't think there is much fear of
such a calamity now. Here we have our
own Parliamentary building, noble and
inspiring, and it will stand all down the
ages as a sentinel guarding the rights.
and liberties of the people-a precious
heritage secured to them by sacrifice,
endurance, and much suffering.
Mr. DORMAN: I do not intend to
occupy the time of the House at this late
hour at any considerable length, but as
the only Socialist in this House I wish
to repudiate completely, entirely and
whole-heartedly the imputation which
has been made, in connection with my
absence from the opening of this House,
that there was any intention whatever of
insulting any member of the Royal
Family. I have real, conscientious
objections to doing obeisance to any
fellow-man, but I was not absent from
that particular function with any
intention of insulting His Royal
Highness or any member of the Royal
Family.
I wish to congratulate the hon. and
learned Senator Leslie on the very
eloquent manner in which he proposed
the Address in Reply to the Speech from
the Throne, and also Senator Lavery.
There are some things mentioned in the
Speech that are not capable of settlement
by any local, provincial, or national
efforts whatever, and I think it is near
time that the statesmen of the world
should take a large and broad outlook
upon the system of society in which they
live in the principal countries of the
world. If they would do so they would
see that many of the flimsy and shallow
remedies that are put forth to solve this
problem, which is described in the
Gracious Speech from the Throne as the
adverse world conditions which for
several years past have exercised a very
baneful influence upon every countryupon every country, not merely upon
Northern Ireland and Great Britain, but
in every country in the world, and more
especially in those countries which are
best prepared and best furnished with
the means of producing all the things
necessary to human life.
Those countires in which industry is
most highly developed and most widely
rationalised, whose industries have more
or less a monopoly in the markets of the
world are the countries suffering most
through these adverse conditions referred
to in the gracious Speech from the Throne.
The very best provided countries for the
production of all the necessaries of human
life, wellbeing, and happiness are Great
Britain, Germany, and the United States
of America. In the United States of
America to-day it is estimated that there
are 12,000,000 human beings that have
no means of livelihood but to sell their
labour power from day to day, seeking
and praying for the opportunity of excercising their labour power in order that
they may support their dependants from
week to week. In Germany there are
6,000,000, and there are approximately
3,000,000 in Great Britain, and in Northern Ireland between one-third and onefourth of our population who have no
means of livelihood but to sell their
labour from day to day are unemployed.
Therefore, we are justified in believing
that it requires a large outlook in order to
grasp the underlying conditions.
We have recommended to us an increase
in our foreign trade, an increase in
imports, and an increase in exports as a
solution of these adverse conditions that
are present in all the countries of the
world. But what are the facts? The
state that leads the world per capita
is New Zealand. New Zealand is the
largest exporter per head of the population of any country in the world. It
has also the largest importation of manu-
It
factured and other goods per head.
leads the world, and Great Britain, the
mother of industry, the country that had
a monopoly of the markets of the world,
is now in an equally bad condition with
New Zealand. The reason is this: that
the statesmen of the world to-day have
been endeavouring in every available manner to compel their own people to produce as large a proportion as possible of
their own use values from year to year.
Those use values that many of the nations,
which had not been industrialised, have
been accustomed to obtain from industrially developed countries they themselves are now producing.
Debate on the Address

Debate on the Address
THE SENATE
[Mr. Dorman]
It is a well-known fact that no nation
can proceed to industrialise itself without
placing itself in a position to supply more
than the needs of their own population,
and they are bound to seek a world market
for the disposal of their surplus goods.
Therefore, the position is a unique one,
and no local provincial or national efforts
can solve this great international problem.
We cannot solve the unemployment
problem. Unemployment is a necessary
part of the system of society in which we
live. Long before there was
machinery for the purpose of relieving
unemployment when our trade was most
prosperous there was never less than 2 per
cent. of the working-class population unemployed from week to week and month
to month. Now it has come that in
Northern Ireland some 70,000 of the
270,000 insurable individuals in our particular State are unemployed. It
requires statesmen to direct their
attention to the foundations:
any
I was reading the other day the case
of a Band of Hope Society in England
which, 50 years ago, obtained a lease of
premises for the purpose of their headquarters and recently, last year I think
it was, their lease fell out. Originally
they held the lease at £5 a year, and
immediately the lease expired the ground
rent was raised to £250, an increase of
over a thousand per cent., demanded as
tribute from one small moiety of the
community by another moiety.
I will not occupy the time of the
House, but this morning I came across
an extract which I think I would be
justified in quoting and which covers the
whole ground. I would like to quote
it before I sit down. The statement is
from a working-class magazine entitled
the Plebs Magazine:
The trouble with the present system
of producing and distributing wealth
is really very simple to see and understand. There is no problem about the
production of the necessities of life.
Wealth has been, and can be, produced in such profusion that there is
no reason why every man, woman, and
child in this country should not enjoy
at least a sufficency of food, adequate
shelter, and satisfactory clothing,
whilst still finding time for leisure and
living. Unfortunately-and it sounds.
rather absurd-those who produce
these things produce them for other
people; not for themselves. As is
well known to students of economics,
the worker gets his wage-roughly just
enough to keep him and his family
whilst working, but meantime he (the
worker) is producing for his employer
values far in excess of those consumed.
Of course out of this surplus have to
be kept the employer and his family,
all those engaged in non-productive
avocations, and all those who follow no
occupation at all. Even then there is
a big surplus, and a growing surplus,
because wealth production is becoming
technically easier week by week. The
increase in population is nothing compared with the increase in actual and
potential production.

But all this has been going on for
years" it can be legitimately said.
Yes, but previously there was an
important outlet for the surplus and
the surplus itself was not so large. All
around the globe there were outlets,
but in recent years these importing
countries have developed their own
industry and they, in turn, now want
an outlet for their surplus. So long
as the capitalist system lasts this will
not only continue, but grow worse.
This, then, is the seat and source of all
the trouble. This and nothing else—
not money systems, not tariffs, not
foreigners, nor kings, nor comedians,
nor governments-just the capitalist
system.
Hence the difficulty of absorbing the
unemployed again. The statesmen of the
world have been directing the attention
of their people to producing the greatest
possible proportion of use values. I
think the Government would be well
advised on the agricultural question to
direct attention to the needs of the
people of Northern Ireland and to producing goods in such a manner as would
be most readily marketed. Thereby
they would do much to settle the agricultural question. But every farmer,
without consulting anybody or consulting the general market, produces what
he thinks proper, with the result that
to-day the farmer is receiving a shilling
a hundred weight in the Belfast market
for potatoes, a sum not equivalent to the
payment of the necessary labour of putting the potatoes in the ground.
I wish to join also, before I sit down,
in expressing my gratification at the
arrangements which have been provided
for us, and especially, of expressing my
Debate on the Address

appreciation of the acoustic properties
of this chamber.
Mr. SAMUEL CUNNINGHAM: I
should like to join in the congratulations
to the mover and seconder of the Address
for the very able manner in which they
have discharged their duty. I remember the first occasion on which this House
met in the City Hall, when the Address
in Reply was moved by His Grace the
Governor, and I had the honour of
seconding it. I would like to compliment
Senator Lavery, who has described himself as the junior Member of the Senate,
on the very interesting speech he delivered
in seconding the Address. If he continues in the way he has begun he will
soon become one of the best orators in this
House.
I cannot refrain from referring to the
great gratification we all felt last week
at the visit of His Royal Highness the
Prince of Wales. He has now seen our
country, and although I am afraid he
came over here with an impression of the
people rather different from that which
he took away, I feel sure his visit made
him realise the very warm hearts the
people here have for himself, for His
Majesty the King, and for all the Menibers of the Royal Family. I think His
Royal Highness's visit will do Ulster a
great deal of good on the other side of
the water.
I notice it is proposed to introduce a
Bill dealing with destructive imported
animals. I am rather sorry the term
"'imported" is used, because I believe it
is a fact that a tenth of the produce of
our people is destroyed by vermin, principally rats and mice. I think the Government should take the necessary powers,
if they do not possess them already, to
compel local authorities throughout Ulster
to wage war against vermin which cause
so much destruction of valuable produce.
I am informed that in an ordinary season
ten per cent. of the crops are destroyed by
vermin, and if these vermin could be exterminated this amount of produce would
be saved. I hope that when this Bill is
going through the House the opportunity
will be taken to deal with this very important matter.
There is another matter which, I am
sorry to say, is not referred to in the
gracious Speech. Some time ago in this
House the question of the advisability of
the repeal of the Malicious Injuries Act
was raised, and I am under the impression we were then told that the Government would consider the matter, and
would introduce legislation on the subject. Therefore, I am sorry it is not
alluded to in the gracious Speech. When
we consider the great trouble and expense
local authorities are put to when a riot
or civil disturbance takes place in meeting
claims for malicious injuries, I think it
would be far better to get into line with
England and make the owners of
property insure against loss. I knew of
one case where hay was burned. The
owner made a claim under the Malicious
Injuries Act, and he got a very fine price.
for the hay. It would be very much
more satisfactory if the owner of that
hay had had it insured and had made his
claim against the insurance company. I
hope the two matters I have referred to
will be considered by the Government
and that the necessary action will be
taken in the near future. I join in the
hope that there will be a speedy improvement in trade and industry, and I trust
that you, Mr. Speaker, may long be
spared to occupy your present position in
this beautiful Chamber.
On Question, Motion agreed to.

Records of Proceedings.
Mr. SPEAKER: I have to announce
for the information of hon. Members
that in future the Minutes, Proceedings,
and Records of the Senate, Orders of the
Day, and Bills, will not be placed on the
Table of the House. This was done for
convenience in the old House, but in
future they will be available for Members at the Vote Office.

(Viscount Charlemont.)
Question to Minister.
EXPIRATION OF DRIVING
LICENCES.
The following question stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Mr. Leslie :-
To ask the Leader of the House whether
any representations have been received by
the Minister of Home Affairs urging that
persons holding motor licences should be
advised when the date of the licence expires; whether the issue of such notice
is already a practice of some county
councils; what will be the estimated cost
to the Road Fund of the general adoption
of such a practice; and whether steps will
be taken to meet the wishes of motorists
in the matter.
Mr. LESLIE: In asking this question
I desire to point out that it was my intention to refer to motor driving licences.
The question as printed on the Paper
refers to motor licences. I do not propose to say anything else.
Viscount CHARLEMONT: I have
been informed by my right hon. Friend,
the Minister of Home Affairs, that he has
received representations from the Association of Northern Ireland Chambers of
Commerce, and the Coleraine and Ballymoney Chambers of Commerce.
Oral Answers
Oral Answers.
THE SENATE
[Viscount Charlemont]
It was the practice with Down and
Fermanagh County Councils, to a limited
extent, to issue reminders, but that
practice has ceased as the result of the
Ministry's letter of the 30th August,
1932, to Down County Council, and of
verbal instructions given to the Fermanagh Local Taxation Officer. These
instructions were issued because of the
urgent necessity for effecting economies in
the expenditure from the Road Fund-a
necessity which has obliged the Ministry
and local authorities to curtail, very considerably, the expenditure to be incurred
not only on road improvement works but
also on road maintenance.
The cost of issuing such reminders
would depend, to some extent, on the
nature of the reminder sent in each case,
whether it was merely a postcard or
whether an application form was enclosed,
but as there are 50,000 motorists in
Northern Ireland it will be appreciated
that, in addition to the cost of postage
and printing, it would be necessary to set
up special machinery for ensuring that the
issue of reminders would be automatic;
that is, to provide a separate card index
system and additional staff, and in consequence, an additional charge of £1,000
per annum on the Road Fund might
reasonably be expected.
The issue of reminders was never
general in either Great Britain or
Northern Ireland, and in the former
country it ceased entirely two years ago.
Mr. LESLIE: Arising out of the
answer of the noble Viscount, might I ask
whether there is any information available as to the amount that would be saved
by getting licences renewed on the due
date. It occurs to one that if a licence
is allowed to run out, and after six weeks
is then renewed, there is a loss to the
revenue in respect of those six weeks
which, sooner or later, amounts to a considerable sum of money.
Viscount CHARLEMONT: I am
unable to follow the hon. and learned
Senator's argument.
If my
licence
expires on 1st November, and I take or
send it to be renewed for another year,
there is no loss to the revenue.
Mr. HILL DICKSON: I think the
figure of £1,000 given by the noble
Viscount as the cost of carrying out the
suggestion contained in the question is
It is
rather exaggerated. We in County
Down have been issuing these reminders
to motor drivers for a number of years,
and the postage costs only £16.
done by the clerk who issues the licences,
and there is no extra cost apart from the
postage. The total cost is only a matter
of £16. We receive notice when our
wireless and other licences expire, and I
do not see why the same thing should not
apply in the case of the expiration of
motor driving licences. A person like
myself, who has three or four licences,
cannot possibly be expected to remember
when each of them expires. As against
the cost entailed in issuing reminders
there is the cost of prosecutions, although
I know that does not fall on the County
Councils. We pay £78,000 a year to the
Road Fund, and it does seem ridiculous
that the Government will not sanction a
suggestion which would entail a cost of
only £16.
Mr. McCORKELL: The very fact that
there are only 50,000 motor driving
licences affected makes it possible for the
suggestion to be carried out. If there
were 5,000,000 licences involved there
might be a difficulty. I am in agreement
with the remarks of the last speaker. In
a household where there are several
licences it is impossible to remember when
each of them expires. It must be borne
in mind that a person may hold licences
in respect of other matters as well, and
for which we do receive reminders, and
it would be a great boon if the same thing
was made to apply to motor driving
licences.

Legislative Procedure Bill.
Viscount CHARLEMONT: In moving
the Second Reading of this Bill it is, I
think, unnecessary for me to give any
very lengthy explanation of the purely
formal provisions contained in this short
Measure.
Hon. Members will remember that by
the Northern Ireland (Miscellaneous
Provisions) Act, 1932, recently passed by
the United Kingdom Parliament, the
Parliament of Northern Ireland is empowered to make laws dealing with certain particular matters which would
otherwise be outside its powers as defined
by the Government of Ireland Act, 1920,
provided that the consent of the appropriate Imperial Government Department
has been obtained.
The Bill which is before the House
prescribes that where in such cases the
Clerk of the Parliaments of Northern
Ireland has certified that the required
consent has been obtained, a recital of
the consent shall appear in the preamble
of the Bill concerned. This recital may
be in the Bill as introduced or may be
inserted or amended at any stage.
It is also provided that any alteration
of a Bill which is confined solely to this
purpose shall not be deemed to be an
amendment.
The procedure is designed to avoid inconvenience and delay, and does not in
any way prejudice the rights of hon.
Members with regard to any Measures
brought before this House. I beg to
move.
Mr. LESLIE: I venture to think that
it is very appropriate that the first Bill
which we have to consider in our new
habitation should be a Bill of great importance, and, if I may say so, a Bill
which will have some influence on our
Parliamentary procedure, and therefore
on our constitutional practice. The Bill is
a very important one, for the reasons given
by the noble Viscount, who moved the
Second Reading, and also for reasons
adumbrated in the memorandum printed.
on the outside of the Bill. The effect of
the Measure will, I think, necessarily be
to make very greatly for the convenience
of the persons concerned, a far larger
number of people perhaps than at first
sight one would imagine.
The Bill necessarily is of a somewhat
technical character, but of course it must
be examined and considered. It will, in
fact, affect a great deal more what we
call our Local or Private Bill legislation.
than it will affect public legislation. We
have just been reminded of Sections 6
and 9 of the recent Miscellaneous Provisions Act of 1932. That Act, of course,
is the one which gives us power to deal
in this way with the matters at issue.
It is not this Bill, as hon. Members will
see, that gives any special value to those
consents. What this Bill does is to provide means of recording that those consents have in fact been given.
But
That is a very important matter.
for this Bill the Chairman of a Bill Committee, whether it be an unopposed or an
opposed Bill, would necessarily, before he
admitted or allowed his Committee to
find the Preamble proved, require definite
proof that the consent of the appropriate
Department had been obtained. He
would naturally say to the promoters of
the Bill, "Can you prove this portion of
the Preamble?" And I think he would
be bound to say that unless the consent
of the appropriate Department is produced or signified, he could not allow that
part of the Preamble to be regarded as
proved. The result would undoubtedly
be delay and some inconvenience.
As I say, there are two Sections of the
Imperial Act, Section 6, which deals with
purely priority matters in bankruptcy,
and that matter, according to the
Gracious Speech, will be dealt with by
this Parliament very shortly. That will
probably be all you will be asked to
deal with under Section 6. Section 9 will
remain. It relates to the Board of Trade,
the Commissioners of Woods and Forests,
and the solution of problems arising out
of that will be the permanent contribution of the Bill before us. Of course
the Sections I have quoted are the
Sections of the Imperial Act, and not
the Clause of the Bill before us.
At the same time it may be said that
Parliament, by allowing certain matters
to be certified by the Clerk of the Parliaments, is delegating its authority, but I do
not think that is a matter that need concern
us very much. The main point we have
to keep in mind is, to provide that those
421782
39 Legislative Procedure Bill-

THE SENATE
Dangerous Drugs Bill-
[Mr. Leslie]
consents are properly recited. There is
little else
ourselves
about.
need
we
concern
find
As regards the fact that the Bill is
necessarily somewhat technical, we
naturally turn our minds to the question
of what precedents, if any, are to be
found for the action we are taking. I
confess I did not, myself,
much
very
in the way of Parliamentary precedent.
Indeed I do not think there is very much
to be found. The first thing that occurs
to us all is naturally the Parliament Act
of 1911, which provided for a distinct
variation of the words of enactment, and
those words of enactment are, after all,
practically the only Preamble in nine
Bills out of ten. The Parliament Act
provided definitely for the variation of
the words of enactment, and for that being
done without what may be called the
interposition of either House of Parliament. Indeed, in that particular case,
there was only one House.
There is, perhaps, to some extent also,
the precedent of the Commencement
of Statutes Acts of the century before last, which directed that certain
matters should be recorded on
a Bill
after it had received the Royal
Assent. In this case the Clerk of
Parliament was to endorse the Bill. He
was told, also, how to do it. He was to
do it in English words. That was in 1793
or 1795, in order to avoid perpetuating
the practice of using a language which, as
has been pointed out, the majority of
British citizens do not understand.
But there is another and more important precedent, it seems to me, for dealing with it in this way. That is the
practice which obtains in most Chambers
of First Instance of putting in italics
certain parts of a Bill which have not yet,
at the time of printing, received the
sanction of a Money Resolution in that
House. That appears to me to be an
exact precedent. Such words are not
regarded as part of the Bill. They are
not there. They are, so to speak, not at
home, which is the same thing metaphorically. The words are not in Bills
of that description until they are brought
to life by the vivifying power of a Money
Resolution. So here the words will not
be in the Bill until the necessary certificate has been given by the Clerk of the
Parliaments. I propose, at a later stage, to
suggest that we should use instead of the
two or three words in the text, the word
Sir Erskine May uses in the treatise on the
subject, which
which we all regard as the
greatest possible authority.
It has to be remembered, of course,
that the various consents with which the
Bill deals cannot possibly be given until
the last minute. The head of the appropriate Department must retain his right
as one of the trustees, after all, for the
people of the Empire-he must retain his
right until the very last minute to withhold his consent until he knows exactly
what the provisions of the Bill are. Therefore the consent may, perhaps, not be
given until the very last Parliamentary
stage. That is, I think, met by the provision we are making that the certificate
is only required to be given before the
Royal Assent.
I am afraid that the third Sub-Clause
of the first Clause may, perhaps, give
rise to a little misapprehension, particularly on the part of those concerned rather
with Private Bills than Public Bills.
Hon. Members will recognise that the
words of this Sub-Section are taken
directly from the Parliament Act,
to which I have already alluded.
I venture to suggest again that at
the proper stage we limit ourselves a
little more, that is to say, make it
quite clear that the alteration is only to
the Preamble of the Bill. That, I think,
will be of assistance outside the immediate
staff of the House.
I may conclude by congratulating the
Government on having dealt so very
promptly with a matter which, if allowed
to slide along, might have some day or
other caused very serious inconvenience.
As it is we are dealing with the matter in
some seven or eight months after the
Royal Assent was given in the Imperial
Parliament to the Act, which enables us
to do what we are about to do. I support
the Second Reading.
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Bill committed to a Committee of the
Whole House for Tuesday next.
Dangerous Drugs Bill.
Viscount CHARLEMONT: This Bill
is designed for the purpose of imposing
a stricter control on the traffic in dangerous drugs. I am sure there is no hon.
Member in this House who will not
welcome the taking of any reasonable
Dangerous Drugs Bill-
Second Reading
6 DECEMBER 1932
steps to make it difficult for dangerous
drugs to get into the hands of people who
may put them to improper use; the dire
results of drug addiction are so well
known that I do not think it is necessary for me to take up the time of the
House with any lengthy discourse on the
subject.
The Bill contains the necessary provisions for securing in Northern Ireland
the safeguards which were agreed upon
by the last Convention held at Geneva
in July, 1931, for the purpose of supplementing the provisions of previous International Opium Conventions and to
provide for the more rigorous control of
the traffic in these drugs. Happily, the
control of this traffic has now become of
world-wide interest. By this Bill we
propose to bring the law in Northern
Ireland on this subject into conformity
not only with the law of Great Britain
but of all countries whose representatives
were signatories to the League of Nations
Convention.
As hon. Members are no doubt aware,
there are really two Codes of Laws dealing with the traffic in dangerous drugs
operating in Northern Ireland:
:-
(1) The Imperial Code, which applies
in Northern Ireland only in so far as
it affects matters respecting which the
Parliament of Northern Ireland has
no power to make laws; and
(2) The Northern Ireland Code,
which deals with such matters as come
within the control of the Northern Ireland Parliament, the provisions of
which it is obviously desirable should
be identical with the corresponding
provisions of the Imperial Statutes.
The Measure before the House is, of
course, confined to that portion of the law
as respects which our Parliament has
power to legislate.
Clause I of the Bill enacts a revised
list of drugs for incorporation in Part III
of the Dangerous Drugs Act, 1920 (as that
Act applies to Northern Ireland). Sub-
Clause (1) (2) of the Clause gives power
to bring new derivatives of morphine.
and cocaine within the scope of the Code,
even if they in themselves are not
dangerous drugs, but are capable of being
converted into a substance which is or
is likely to be productive, if improperly
used, of ill effects similar to those produced by morphine and cocaine.
Clause 2 prohibits the manufacture or
trade in any products of certain alkaloids

of the coca leaf, until these new products
have been classified as of scientific or of
medical importance. Power is given to the
Governor to carry out such classification
when His Majesty in Council has done so
in England; and when such classification
has been effected transactions in the drugs
may be permitted, always, of course,
subject to the restrictions imposed by
Part III of the Act of 1920. Subclause (3) of Clause (2) gives power to
bring codeine and dionin, should it be
ultimately considered desirable to regard
them as dangerous drugs, within the
scope of the Dangerous Drugs Act, with
such modifications as may appear necessary.
Clause 3 relates to offences in Northern
Ireland in relation to certain transactions
outside Northern Ireland and contains,
in the first place, a provision giving to
Justices of the Peace powers of search
and seizure as respects documents connected with transactions or intended transactions outside Northern Ireland which
were, or would be if carried out, offences
against corresponding laws in the places
outside Northern Ireland.
The Clause contains, secondly, a provision making a person guilty of an offence
"who in
against the Act of 1920,
Northern Ireland aids, abets, counsels, or
procures the commission, in any place
outside Northern Ireland, of any offence
punishable under the provisions of any
corresponding law in force in that place.

It will, I am sure, be realised that such
provisions are necessary if the full advantages of international co-operation are to
be achieved.
Clause 4 gives the Governor power to
vary or revoke Orders or Declarations in
Council.
Clause 5 contains the Short Title and
Citation, as well as a saving in respect of
those matters which are reserved to the
United Kingdom Parliament, and provision for the determination of "appointed
days." I beg to move.
Question proposed, "That this Bill be
now read a Second time."
Colonel SINCLAIR : As the noble
Viscount has just explained, the object of
this Bill is to bring the law in Northern
Ireland on the subject of dangerous drugs
into conformity with the law in Great
Britain. I should like to ask the noble
Viscount whether he is in a position to
give the House any assurance that at an
Dangerous Drugs Bill-
Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Colonel Sinclair]
early date an extension of the Dangerous
Drugs Act can be hoped for. A year ago
a combined letter from the Medical
Faculty of Queen's University and the
Pharmaceutical Society of Northern
Ireland was sent to the Home Office, suggesting that the time had arrived when a
third part might be added to the
Dangerous Drugs Act with the object of
protecting the public from the use of a
number of organic preparations of great
potency. These potent preparations, it
was suggested in the letter, might form
the subject of a third part in a still
further amended Act. I have in mind
several drugs, one of which is thyroid.
extract, which is very widely used in an
indiscriminate way by the fair sex in the
fashionable craze for slimming.
It is
only right and proper that some safeguard should be placed on its sale and
use. Then there is the organic extract
called pituitrin, a powerful agent in midwifery, which if incautiously used by unqualified persons may lead to disastrous
results, such as rupture of the womb, and
death to both mother and child. This
drug can be obtained by anybody apart
from trained midwives, and when used
by unqualified persons, such as handy
women, it may have very grave consequences.
Perhaps I might give one other
illustration of more general interest.
That is, tuberculin, which can be bought
by anyone at a chemist's or druggist's
under present conditions without any
limitation or restriction. This drug can
be employed to render an infected animal
relatively and temporarily immune, so
that when a veterinary surgeon or
inspector comes to apply the test for
tuberculosis the animal fails to react
owing to the dope which has been more
or less nefariously injected.
This may
lead to such animals being included in a
dairy herd, or even being offered for sale
in butchers' shops. It is very necessary
that the drugs I have mentioned should
be subjected to some regulation and supervision, and I think this could best be
accomplished by their being classified as
potent drugs under the Dangerous Drugs
Act.
The Pharmaceutical Society of Northern Ireland is a very highly organised
body, as hon. Members are aware, but
unlike the General Medical Council, and
the Dental Board, it does not possess
disciplinary powers. It has more to do
with education and examination. It has
no power to strike off its register unworthy members until a conviction has
been secured by the police or Home
Office officials. Pending a conviction, the
society has to depend upon the police and
salaried inspectors, and I am very happy
to say that they carry out their duties
very efficiently. I am glad to be able to
say that the Pharmaceutical Society of
Northern Ireland has rapidly attained a
high standard of ethical conduct, and its
efforts should be supported by a further
Amendment of the Dangerous Drugs Act
which would ensure the regulation of the
sale and use of these new and potent
organic preparations.
Already, in some of our Dominions,
notably Victoria, Australia, a schedule of
potent drugs has been added to their
legislation dealing with dangerous drugs,
to provide for the sale of these potent
drugs under safeguards for their proper
use. In these days there are so many
idle people seeking various forms of
stimulus that it is highly desirable that
provision should be made for the proper
regulation of the sale and use of the drugs.
I have mentioned, and to prevent the misuse of organic preparations which, when
used indiscriminately, can cause very
serious mischief. Science is very progressive, and each month or so adds to
the list of powerful drugs that may be
employed without due restriction and
supervision, and the sooner we take steps
to bring our legislation in this respect
up to the standard attained in Victoria,
Australia, the better it will be for the
public and all concerned.
Viscount CHARLEMONT: I do not
know that it would be competent to incorporate in this Bill the interesting suggestion made by the hon. and gallant
Senator, because the object of this
Measure is to bring us into line with the
decisions of the Geneva Convention. At
the same time I am glad to be able to
give the hon. and gallant Member some
hope of advancing along the path he has
indicated, because there is, at the present
moment, a Pharmacy and Poisons Bill
before the Imperial Parliament, and the
progress of that legislation dealing with
something the same as the hon. and
gallant Member suggests will be watched
with interest by the Government of
Northern Ireland.
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Bill committeed to a Committee of the
Whole House for Tuesday next.
45 Destructive Animals Bill-
6 DECEMBER 1932

Second Reading.
Destructive Imported Animals Bill.
Viscount CHARLEMONT: In moving
the Second Reading of this Bill, may I
point out that its object is to prohibit
or control the importation into Northern
Ireland or the keeping in this area of the
musk rat. This small animal is a native
of North America and is valuable for
fur-bearing purposes. Unfortunately,
however, the animal, when allowed to
roam at large, does serious damage by
making burrows under water courses,
river banks, railway embankments and
reservoir enclosures, and undermines
these structures.
The great difficulty in dealing with the
pest, once introduced and at large, lies
in its power of reproducing itself. For
example, five musk rats were introduced
into Prague in 1905, and it is now understood that as a result of this importation no less than 100,000,000 musk rats
are at large in Central Europe. It is a
tribute to the efficiency of the gentleman
responsible for the collection of statistics
in Central Europe that they were able to
count them. (Laughter.) The damage
caused by this modern plague has been
so extensive that the Governments concerned are now obliged to incur very
considerable expenditure in an endeavour
to keep this animal under some sort of
control.
The animal was first brought to Great
Britain in 1928, but already there is
abundant evidence of its destructive
qualities on certain Scottish rivers and
in Shropshire. The Imperial Parliament
being anxious as to the position thus created
passed in March last a Measure the lines
of which the present Bill closely follows.
It was provided that that Act should not
extend to Northern Ireland, but it was
also laid down that similar legislation
in Northern Ireland would be within the
powers of this Parliament. Fortunately
at least as far as we are aware-the
musk rat has not yet invaded Northern
Ireland, but having regard to the destruction and consequent
and consequent expenditure
caused by this animal elsewhere, hon.
Members will, I am sure, agree that it
is prudent to accord to the Government
the powers sought in this Bill in order
that this happy condition may be preserved.
As will be observed from Clause 8 of
the Bill, powers are taken to extend its
provisions to non-indigenous animals of
destructive habits. In this connection we
have in mind animals such as the grey
squirrel and the nutria beaver. There
is one respect in which our Measure
differs from the Imperial Act. When the
British Measure was introduced musk rat
farming had already been established in
Great Britain, and it was considered
desirable to make provision for the award
of compensation in certain cases. Since
at present Northern Ireland is free from
the musk rat, this Bill does not contain
any powers to award compensation for
losses that may be sustained by musk
rat farmers. I beg to move.
Colonel PAKENHAM: As this is the
first time I have risen in this Chamber
I would be much obliged if hon. Members who cannot hear me would just say
Speak up."
Sir E. HERDMAN: We can.

animal of the species designated Fiber

so on.
Now we have got an entirely destructive animal which breeds in an impossible
fashion and produces furs. Well there
are thousands of furs women can wear
without bringing in the musk rat. I
think the Government should be stronger
and say, never shall these animals come
here. If you could see their depredations. I have been in Shropshire and

destroy river banks, canal banks, and

They destroy them by burrowing under

water. I say keep them out of the

We
one difference between Great Britain and

can control them if they get in, but I

Looking at the Bill itself there are,
as I say, several. matters that provoke
comment. First of all the Bill is
modelled, as nearly as possible, on the
English Statute. I am rather glad it
is so, because I cannot agree myself that
the first Clause is too well expressed. I
venture to suggest that anybody reading
the first Clause would think that the
first paragraph related to more than one
species of animals. I think it should not
be read in the plural but in the singular.
Then the Bill talks about the precautions
to be taken, and it seems to me to follow
that a relatively considerable area will
be required to be set apart for musk
rats. I hope they are not to be allowed
here, but the Bill does not indicate that
they are definitely excluded. But if an
area of sufficient size is to be allotted to
them, and if precautions are to be taken
against escape, what are those precautions. It would seem to me to be
necessary that all the soil of the area—
it might be five acres or fifty acres—
ought to be excavated and a concrete
foundation put in, the soil replaced
and a concrete wall built all round, and
water should be provided and concreted
and railed off. That is simply an impossible proposition. It is obvious that any
attempt to proceed on those lines is
simply ridiculous, but I believe nothing
short of that would afford sufficient protection.
Then we hear about fees. We are not
told what they are, and we do not care
very much.
But the mention of fees reminds us that there is nothing in the Bill
at all about finance. Councils may be
invited to do certain things at the expense of the Ministry of Agriculture, but
nothing is said as to where the funds are
to come from to cover that expense. i
presume it will be from moneys provided
by Parliament and voted annually. In
Clause 5 we have departed from the English Act and I find some difficulty in the
words "at large." I cannot pass over
Clause 6 without extending my congratulations to the Minister on having at last
arrived at the conclusion that it is unnecessary to ask Parliament to allow the
introduction of a minimum fine. 1 am
glad to see that that particular form of
rat is not included in this Bill.
Then we come to Clause 8, which really
seems to me a little difficult. Under that
Clause there is no definite provision made
for compensation for any injury that
might be caused by the operation of the
Act. The noble Viscount reminded us
that we do not require to make any pro-
Vision for compensation in respect of
musk rats, because we have none, but if
Clause 8 should ever come into operation
by virtue of a Resolution of each House of
Parliament, will that Resolution give the
necessary financial authority for paying
such compensation as may be necessary?
I should like to be assured on that point
before the Bill goes any further, because
we cannot prohibit, say, silver fox, without paying some compensation to the
people who are engaged in that industry.
I notice that no date for the commencement of the Bill is mentioned, and
I presume the Minister is satisfied the
Bill may be brought into operation
immediately it has received the Royal
Assent. On the whole, I support the
Bill, but I hope that immediately it
becomes an Act the Minister will make
an order, not for regulation, but for
absolute prohibition in the case of the
musk rat. I think all the powers that
are asked for are required, but the point
that seems to me to be most essential is
that the importation of the musk rat
should be definitely prohibited.
Viscount CHARLEMONT: With regard to the question of compensation, I
cannot give the hon. and learned Member a definite answer now, but I will do
so at a later stage.
On Question, Motion agreed to.

Motion for Adjournment.
POLICE PROTECTION IN
GREENISLAND.
Viscount CHARLEMONT: I beg to
move, "That this House do now adjourn
until Tuesday next."
Mr. BARCLAY: I wish to raise the
question of police protection in Greenisland. Fifteen or twenty years ago
Greenisland was nothing but fields in
grass and hay. Since that time house
property has been erected over a wide
area, and roads and gardens have been
made, but the police protection has not
been extended in any way during those
fifteen years.
The result is that Greenisland has become a happy huntingground for burglars and daylight thieves.
Men come round to see if a house is
closed and the people away, and then they
ransack it. Some of them come during
the night and break in even when the
occupants are at home, and the people of
Greenisland think something should be
done to protect them from this kind of
people roving about after dark. I have,
therefore, been asked by the Greenisland
Ratepayers' Association to raise this
question here, as they think it might be
more effective than the letters they have
written to the police authorities
Carrickfergus.
at
The suggestion of the Ratepayers'
Association is that we should have a
double patrol, one from Whiteabbey and
the other from Carrickfergus. The
present patrol comes from Carrickfergus,
and Whiteabbey does not seem to have
anything to do with Greenisland. The
committee think that Greenisland is not
large enough to have a police barracks of
its own, but they think that if a motorcycle patrol came from Carrickfergus and
another from Whiteabbey they could
soon be on the spot if they received a
telephone message that suspicious characters were about.
A short time ago there were a couple
of suspicious characters roaming about
after dark, and they were kept under
observation by four young men. Two of
them went to the railway station and
telephoned to the police barracks at
Carrickfergus. They asked that police
should be sent, and promised to keep the
men under observation. However, the
police did not arrive until eleven o'clock
the next day, but the young men kept so
close on the trail of the strangers that
they thought it advisable to remove themselves from the neighbourhood. Unfortunately the came again the next night
and did some damage at a house. I raise
the question in the
the hope that the
Minister responsible for the administration of the police force will use his
influence to see that Greenisland has
better protection in the future.
Viscount CHARLEMONT: I will
bring the hon. Member's representations
before the notice of the Minister of Home
Affairs.

Viscount CHARLEMONT (Leader of
the House): I beg to move,
That Standing Order No. XXV be suspended for this
day's sitting for the purpose of taking the
Second Reading of the Transitional Payments (Determination of Need) Bill, the
Stormont Regulation Bill, the Preferential
Payments in Bankruptcy Bill, and the
Appropriation Bill.”
Mr. LESLIE: I suppose we may take
it that the proposal of the noble Viscount
is really a means to an end, and the end is
to conclude the business, subject, of course,
to the Royal Assent, during the current
week. Having regard to the near approach
of the usual date of what is called the
festive season, it is perhaps more difficult
than usual to oppose the Motion, but I
cannot refrain from pointing out that the
Bills selected for this accelerated procedure are singularly ill adapted to rapidity
of action. As regards the Transitional
Payments Bill, it is almost certain, I
think, that Members will want to refresh
their memories as regards the Order which
is referred to. The next Bill is the Stormont Regulation Bill, and that Bill, perhaps, contains a Clause of some difficulty
which, at all events, ought not to be discussed within twenty-four hours of its
presentation to this House.
The next Bill is the Preferential Payments in Bankruptcy Bill, and I may say
at once that I shall myself strongly oppose
any attempt to take such a very technical
Bill in anything less than the ordinary
time. The final Bill, the Appropriation
Bill, of course, does not matter so much,
and we are quite prepared to take it
through all its stages in one day if necessary. I do hope we shall not again be
asked to go at such extreme pace, and I
might add that I do not think that the
Second Reading of four Bills, and the
Committee Stages of three others, is a
reasonable allowance for one afternoon's
work. (Hon Members: Hear, hear.) It
can only have the effect of abbreviating
the discussion. I know the circumstances are
unusual, but if anybody else really desires
more time between the First and Second
Readings I should feel bound to support
them, although I will not myself go so
far as to oppose the Motion.
Viscount CHARLEMONT: There is
every justice in what the hon. and learned
Member has said. With regard to the
Transitional Payments Bill, we are rather
in a quandary. We have got this Measure
as quickly as we could have received it,
and it is very important that it should be
law by Christmas time. Its provisions
are well known and have been debated in
another place, and as it is necessary that
it should become law by Christmas it gives
us only a week. With regard to the other
Bills that have been included in this
Motion, it is not really necessary that they
should be concluded this Session, with the
exception of the Appropriation Bill. If
the House will agree to give them a
Second Reading to-day I am quite prepared to allow the Committee Stages to
wait until the House meets after the
adjournment. I am sorry there is such
extreme urgency that the Transitional
Payments Bill should be passed, but
under the circumstances I cannot help it.
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Question to Minister.
ROAD TAX LICENCES.
The following qhestion stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Mr.
Barclay:-
To ask the Leader of the House,
whether the Government will take steps
to enable poor motor-car owners to take
out licences for twelve or twenty-four
hours, or for a week-end during the
winter months, as many of them are
not able to license their motor-cars for
more than three or six months in the
year.
Mr. BARCLAY: In asking the Leader
of the House the question which stands in
my name, I should like to point out that
Oral Answer
Transitional Payments Bill
THE SENATE
[Mr. Barclay]
there are a great number of poor motorcar owners in the City of Belfast and outside the city. They are not in a financial
position to take out licences for the full
year; in fact, some of them are not in a
financial position to take out licences for
six months. In conversations I have had
with some of them they tell me their cars
are closed up in the garage for six or perhaps nine months in the year. They say
that if it was possible for them to get a
licence for a shorter term than one month
it would prove very beneficial to them.
Therefore, I have suggested in the question
that licences should be issued for twelve
hours, or twenty-four hours, or for a
week-end. To-day, coming to this House,
it was suggested to me that licences might
be issued for a week, say Christmas week,
when people would be going away to visit
their friends. My reason for suggesting
the hours in the question is that a great
many people get a call to the country in
the case of sickness, or to attend a funeral
or a wedding or some social gathering. In
such a case they have to hire a taxi, which
costs them considerably more than if they
were able to take out a licence for the
necessary number of hours.
I also think that such a system would
prove a source of revenue to the Government. Supposing the licence fee was fixed
at 2/6 or 5/-, according to the number of
hours for which it was issued, there would
be so much for the revenue. At the same
time, these motorists would be using a
certain amount of petrol from which the
Government would also benefit to the extent of 8d per gallon. Then it is possible
that if some of those motorists used the
licence after it had expired the police
might catch them, with the result that a
good fine might accrue to the Exchequer.
Therefore, I think my suggestion is quite
a reasonable one, because it would benefit
the poor motor-car owner and it would
also benefit the Exchequer to a certain
extent.
Viscount CHARLEMONT: My right
hon. Friend, the Minister of Home Affairs,
has from time to time given careful and
sympathetic consideration to the question
of the issue of short-term licences, but is
convinced that the administrative and
other difficulties which would attend such
a scheme are so great as to outweigh the
advantages which would be afforded to
motorists and the increased revenue which
might accrue to the Government. Perhaps
I might point out some of the difficulties.
One is that if the suggestion of the hon.
Member was adopted the issue of licences
for periods between those which the hon.
Member mentions and those for which
licences are issued at present could not
possibly be refused. Another consideration
is that one of the most important means
of detecting the use of unlicensed vehicles
is the colour scheme adopted for licences,
by means of which the police can readily
see whether the licences displayed are current or not. There are about seventeen
classes of colours, and if the hon. Senator's
scheme was adopted our possible range of
colours would be exhausted, and we would
have to go into mixtures of colours, which
would not be an advantage. Such a
scheme as suggested would enormously increase the difficulties of the police. Their
reports on the number of unlicensed
vehicles are already something like 3,500
in the year, and if this scheme was
adopted they would run into something
like 10,000 in the year, and perhaps more,
and the opportunities for using unlicensed
vehicles would be greatly increased.
Therefore, I am afraid the hon. Senator's
request cannot be granted.

57 Transitional Payments Bill-
13 DECEMBER 1932

Second Reading
Transitional Payments (Determination
of Need) Bill.
Viscount CHARLEMONT: In presenting this Bill to the House I should like to
make it clear at the outset that it is essentially an enabling Bill; it does not curtail
any advantage that has already been given
to insured persons claiming transitional
payments. What it does is to prescribe in
certain respects a minimum standard below which public assistance committees
may not go, but above which they are at
liberty to in such cases
go
as in their
judgment require that course.
I say this to prevent any misunderstanding; for the Government is satisfied
that, generally speaking, public assistance
committees in Northern Ireland have
acted with proper sympathy whilst determining the needs of persons claiming
transitional payments. There has, however, been some lack of uniformity amongst
committees, and this Bill will secure more
uniform handling of a few of the problems
with which the committees have been confronted. The Bill follows generally the
lines of a Measure which recently passed
through the British Houses of Parliament.
When assessing the amount of transitional payment to which a particular
claimant is entitled, a public assistance
committee, as hon. Members are no doubt
aware, inquires into the income and resources of the family living in the same
house. It then investigates the needs of
that family and comes to a conclusion as
to the amount required to satisfy those
needs. It sets off one against the other,
and in this way determines the amount of
transitional benefit to which the claimant
is entitled.
Under this Bill it is proposed that in
assessing the income, receipts of certain
classes should not be taken into account;
at least half a disability pension, half the
weekly payments of workmen's compensation, and 7/6 per week of health insurance
benefit are to be treated as if they did not
exist. There will thus be a statutory
minimum protection for disability, compensation, and health benefit cases quite
irrespective of the needs of the claimants.
As regards savings, the question is a
little more difficult. On the one hand,
it has been felt that when a family or a
person has actually a reasonably substantial sum of money to fall back upon, it is
a little difficult to say that they are in
need of public assistance so long as that

resource is unexhausted. On the other
hand, it is realised that a thrifty family
that have denied themselves small comforts and luxuries in comparatively good
times in order to be prepared against bad
times, might feel themselves harshly
treated if their savings were to be called
upon to the last penny before they were
entitled to the relief that a more improvident neighbour might secure at once.
This Bill endeavours to give some consideration to both sides of the case-to
avoid discouraging saving and yet to allow
a reasonable safeguard on the interests of
the taxpayer. It provides that the first.
£25 of capital should be disregarded and
that sums from that up to £300 may be
treated as being worth 1/- a week for each
complete £25 of capital. Thus, if a man
has savings amounting to, say £45, they
will be not be reckoned at all; if he has
savings amounting to £70, they may be
considered as worth 1/- a week to him,
and so on. I should, perhaps, explain that
this does not imply that it is thought that
a man can usually get 1/- a week interest
on £25; what it means is that by making
only a small weekly demand upon his
capital it is spread over a long period. It
is calculated that, by proceeding on this
principle, the savings would last for at
least ten years, and that appears to be a
reasonably long period to budget for.
Lump sum payments made as workmen's
compensation will be treated under this
rule.
Special consideration is also given to the
case of an applicant who owns the house
in which he lives. From one point of view
it could be argued that a house is a definite
asset which the owner might be called
upon to sell and to exhaust the proceeds
before he was entitled to draw upon the
public purse. In fact, however, it has not
been the practice of public authorities to
push logic to this extreme and the Bill
gives statutory cover to the claimant; it
prohibits a committee from refusing transitional payments merely because the
applicant owns the house in which he
lives. I think this explanation will be
sufficient to enable hon. Members to understand the main features of the Bill.
beg to move.

Mr. JOSEPH CUNNINGHAM: I am

59 Transitional Payments Bill-

THE SENATE
Second Reading
[Mr. Joseph Cunningham]
continue to give our workers on this side
