benefits as they have on the other side.
It is very unfortunate that usually with
State aid the industrious and thrifty person is penalised. It has been the case in
nearly all this legislation. Why a person
who has saved a little should receive less
from the State than a careless individual
who has spent his money and enjoyed
himself is sometimes a puzzle. That is
usually so. But in this case the workers
are to receive the same benefit as they do
on the other side.
There has been considerable controversy
ranging, round this question and particularly by those people who endeavour to
create disturbances amongst the workers.
They use this matter as a lever to create
disorder and trouble. So far as the North
of Ireland is concerned there is not the
same reason to complain of the administration of transitional benefit as they have
'probably on the other side of the Channel.
It appears to me that the committees administer this thing in a very wise and
common-sense way, and when they do take
into consideration benefit, compensation,
pension, and such like they do not deprive
the applicants of a large amount of transitional benefit on that head.
There are many people in Northern
Ireland who are owners of their own

seems to me that the merits of the proposal

Mr. DORMAN: The principle underlying this Bill I think will meet with the
approval of every hon. Member in this
House. It is not reasonable to expect
that transitional benefits should be
accorded to individuals who have in the
aggregate an income in the home sufficient
to supply them with a reasonable standard
of comfort, and from that point of view
one cannot but approve of the attempt
made by the Government in introducing
this Measure to impress upon the heads
of families their obligations-the obliga-
61 Transitional Payments Bill—
13 DECEMBER 1932

Second Reading
tion of each member of the family to the
other members of the family in time of
unemployment and distress. I think if
this method of dealing with the great
family income of the nation, as a whole,
was given due consideration by the Government, they would see that if they had
acted in a paternal manner towards the
citizens, as they now expect each member
of the family to do in relationship to the

introduce this Measure into the House.
An enormous amount of suffering has
taken place in the homes of self-respecting,
upright, industrious men, who have refrained from taking advantage of the
privileges extended to them of applying
to public assistance committees for the
support of their own homes during the

I
Only last night an ex-soldier who volunteered to go to the war for the purpose
of defending his country came to me. He
was told his country was in need of his
services; that a grateful country would
never forget him, and that he would come
back to a country fit for heroes to live in.
He was drawing unemployment benefit for
himself, his wife, and three children until
the month of April last, when he was
turned off. He has been in receipt of
a disability pension of 40 per cent.
hold his papers in my hand, and those
papers show that he is suffering from four
different causes: a disordered action of
the heart, bronchitis, the results of a
gunshot wound and a compound fracture
of the right foot, myalgia. When he was
turned off, having exhausted his guaranteed benefits, in April last his total income for himself, his wife, and three
children was 16/- per week. Out of that
he had to pay 3/6 per week for rent, 2/-
for a bag of the cheapest coal he could
possibly get to keep the heat in their
bodies, and 1/- for insurance. He had no
desire that, should he prematurely pass
away, his wife should have the burden of
his interment cast upon the widows' and
orphans' pension to which she would be
entitled in the event of his demise. From
April to November, three weeks ago, that
man's total income was, as I have stated,
16/- per week. If we deduct from that
the 7/6 of necessary payments for keeping
the home and divide the remainder over
three meals a day we discover that that
particular individual, who may be personally certified to be a sober, industrious,
and upright man, had at the rate of 1d
per meal to purchase the necessary meals
for himself and his family during the
seven months from April to November,
when he made application to the Public
Assistance Committee for assistance.
That
Now what is the position? The lowest.
standard of comfort that we as a civilised
people consider ought to be afforded to our
destitute citizens is the standard of comfort set up by the Poor-Law Guardians
in the workhouses of the country.
has always been recognised as the minimum standard upon which a fellow human
creature should be expected to subsist
in this country. This particular man
has not had one-fourth of what it
would cost Members of this House as ratepayers to feed him and his family had he
embraced the opportunity of declaring
himself destitute and going into the workhouse. Now, supposing we spread that
particular aspect of this to the nation as
a whole, what happens? We find that
men like this, who have risked the sacrifice of their lives and the mutilation of
their bodies during the war, have
emerged from that war in a state such
as I have just described.
On the other hand, had our Governments taken a paternal interest in their
people, as they are now expecting the
members of each house to take in their
families, it would not have been necessary for legislation of this description to
be introduced into this House. On
examination of the revenue figures it has
been found that a certain portion of the
State, considered as the family, enriched
themselves during the period of the war,
when this man and other men were risking the loss of their lives. to the amount
of over £5,000,000,000. It was estimated
before the war broke out that the total
wealth of Great Britain and the whole of
Ireland was £16,000,000,000. It is now
estimated that the total wealth amounts
to £24,000,000,000, one portion of the
community, of the family, having
enriched themselves to the extent I have
just quoted, while the other section
risked their lives for the protection of the
property and the country of this other
section of the family. The men who made
the sacrifices have been impoverished and,
instead of coming back to a country fit
for heroes to live in and to a grateful
country which they were told would never
forget them, they now have got one penny
to purchase the meals for the homes in
which they live.

Second Reading
THE SENATE
[Mr. Dorman]
The Government ought to recognise
that they have a right to regard themselves in a paternal character and to consider these sections of the community.
Moreover, had successive Governments
taken the necessary steps to protect the
classes for whom this legislation is being
placed upon the Statute Book, had they
protected them from the continuous
worsening of their conditions of life from
week to week by the continued reduction
of the standard of comfort coming into
their homes, I do not believe it would have
been necessary for this Measure to have
been introduced. In support of that I
wish to quote from a standard work published almost a year ago. Speaking
about the labouring classes, which this
Measure is introduced to protect from the
over-assessment of their pensions, the
writer says:
"A labourer cannot have too many
wants. He should desire good food,
good clothing, a cleanly and comfortable home, and education for his children. If the standard of want could be
universally raised the natural price of
labour would rise in proportion, for if
each labourer was determined not to
render himself unable to gratify these
wants all could command the wages that
would supply them. The degree in
which this principle operates determines
the natural rate of wagesrewhich, by the way, has been reduced in
the last ten years by the sum of
£800,000,000 per annum. The particular
class for whom we are legislating to-day
have had their consuming powers
duced to the amount of £800,000,000 per
annum over the last ten years, and this
Measure is largely necessary because of
that continuous worsening of the purchasing capacity of the vast masses of the
people. The writer goes on to say:
"Where it has no influence, as in
Ireland and many parts of Asia, the
wages are only sufficient to support life
upon the commonest food and to provide the most squalid clothing and.
habitations."
The standard of comfort of the great
masses of the people who exhaust their
total incomes from week to week does more
towards stimulating trade internally and
externally than do that section who have
had their incomes increased to such an
enormous extent in the way of rent, interest, and profit during the last ten years
while the weekly incomes of the wageearners have been reduced by over sixty
per cent. I contend that if the Government would take a paternal interest in
the people, and would see to it that the
income of the nation was equitably and
justly distributed, instead of continuously
depressing the purchasing capacity of
their greatest customers, whose prosperity
means the prosperity of the nation as a
whole, legislation such as this would be
unnecessary.

Stormont Regulation and Government
Property Bill.
Viscount CHARLEMONT: I beg to
move: "That this Bill be now read a
Second time."
re-
In the first place the Bill accords to
the Government the necessary powers to
enable it to preserve the amenities of the
Stormont estate; for that purpose it is
provided that the Ministry of Finance
may make regulations regarding the
admission of the public to the Estate;
park-keepers or "rangers" appointed by
the Ministry are, in securing the due
observance of those regulations, endued
with the powers, privileges and
sponsibilities applicable to constables of
the Royal Ulster Constabulary when acting in the execution of their duties.
will be noted that the Regulations made
by the Ministry of Finance under the
powers contained in the Bill must be displayed on the estate and are to be laid
before Parliament, provision being made
for their annulment by resolution of
either House. Penalties are prescribed
for contravention of the regulations, the
giving of false name or address to a
"ranger" or constable, and for the
making of an assault upon a "ranger" in
the execution of his duty.
It
The Bill secondly makes provision with
regard to the acquisition and disposal of
land for the public service. Whilst
certain powers in this connection already
reside in the Ministry of Finance, as
successors in Northern Ireland to the
Commissioners of Public Works in
Ireland, some difficulties have been
experienced in working, more especially
in the disposal of land. Clause 5 of the
Measure, which is before you, is intended
to remove these difficulties and to clarify
the whole position regarding the acquisition of land by the Ministry of Finance
and its disposal by that Ministry-or by
other Ministries with its consent.

[Viscount Charlemont]

Mr. LESLIE: This Bill is divided into
two parts, and I am afraid I have to say
that I wish the marriage of those two
parts had not taken place. I should have
preferred that the two matters dealt with
in the Bill had been kept entirely distinct,
and had been the subject of separate
Measures. The first four Clauses of this
Bill deal with the general management of
the Stormont demesne, that is, the land
which surrounds this building. For those
four Clauses I have nothing but praise.
They are founded on the Parks
Regulation Act of 1872, the main
provisions of which are reproduced
almost word for word, and I shall
not pass from the subject without expressing my gratification that it has been
found possible to make use of the good old
English word "ranger" in describing the
officer who is to have some sort of jurisdiction on the estate. I am sure there
is nothing in those four Clauses that will
give rise to objection, and if the Bill had
stopped there I should certainly not have
opposed for one moment the proposal that
we should take the Committee Stage tomorrow and put the whole Bill forward
for the Royal Assent next week.
unhappily we have Clause 5, and if the
Government could see its way to lighten
the ship by throwing overboard Clause 5
and the remaining part of the Bill I think
it would ease the passage of the
remainder.
But
Clause 5 deals with the acquisition and
disposal of property. We are not told
whether these are new powers or not. The
earlier part of the Clause. speaking more
particularly of Sub-Clause 2, seems to be
based on the Settled Land Act of 1882,
and the latter part recalls Section 14 of
the Crown Lands Act of 1927, which is,

of course, an Imperial Statute. The
connection, however, is not complete, for
the expression "contract for sale" does
not appear in the Crown Lands Act, and
I am unable to find it in any Statute or
in any book of reference. If it is an
alternative expression for "contract for
sale" the suggestion seems to be that one
party to a bilaterial contract could rescind or vary that contract without the
consent of the other party, and that, I
may say, would be contrary to the common law. I do not wish to be understood
as suggesting in fact that the
powers in this Clause would be held
sufficient for that purpose, but I think
we ought to be told. The only connection
between the two parts of the Bill is in the
powers of the Ministry of Finance, and
that might be thought to be outbalanced
by the disconnection comprised in the provision that nothing in this Clause is to
apply to Stormont. I shall move to leave
out the whole Clause and its ancillaries,
including part of the Title, and that will
give us an opportunity of clearing up the
whole question, and when doing that I
hope Paragraph c, of Sub-Clause 7, will
not be forgotten.
Viscount CHARLEMONT: Clause 5
was included in the Bill as it was considered that the powers in it were
required. As the hon. and learned

the Bill, I think it would be desirable for
the Senate to consider this Clause along
with the others. I promise not to take

Legislative Procedure Bill- 70
Bankruptcy Bill
13 DECEMBER 1932
Preferential Payments in Bankruptcy
Bill.
Viscount CHARLEMONT: The main
object of this Bill is to remedy an element
of hardship which is inherent in our existing law of bankruptcy in Northern Ireland. I refer to the provisions under
which the Crown takes priority over other
debtors in the distribution of a bankrupt's
assets.
The Committee appointed some time ago
by my right hon. Friend the Minister of
Commerce to recommend amendments of
the then existing bankruptcy law, included in its report a recommendation on
this matter, but it was not found possible
to give effect to their recommendation in
the Bankruptcy Amendment Act of 1929
-which was based upon that Committee's
Report-owing to the fact that, when the
1929 Act was passed, the Parliament of
Northern Ireland had not power to legislate on this point, which affected reserved
matters.
That difficulty has now been removed
by Section 6 of the Northern Ireland
(Miscellaneous Provisions) Act, 1932, recently passed by the United Kingdom
Parliament (about which we had some
discussion in this House last week in
another connection), and the Government,
in the Bill which is now before you, proposes to give legislative effect to this outstanding recommendation of the Bankruptcy Law Amendment Committee.
The Measure consolidates and clarifies
the existing provisions as to preferential
payments in bankruptcy, but amends the
law with the object of securing that instead of the whole balance of a Crown
debt receiving preferential treatment as
at present, the Crown will only be entitled at most to one year's debt in full,
the balance being paid pari passu with
other debts. The Bill also makes a minor
change in order to bring the law as to
priority of debts due to trustee savings
banks into line with the existing law in
Great Britain. I beg to move,
"That
this Bill be now read a Second time."
Mr. LESLIE: I do not want to make
another speech. I, therefore, only rise
to say that one welcomes this Bill, which
has already been foreshadowed more than
once in the debates in this House, even
during the short Session we have already
experienced. I do, however, ask that we
may be given further time to examine
it, not that I expect to find anything to object to in the Bill,
but I do want time to appreciate its
merits. As the House will see, we are
proposing to repeal about a dozen enactments. The Bill is, to some extent, a
consolidating Measure, and I venture to
submit that any Bill of a consolidating
character ought to receive most careful
examination. The Bill is intended to remove hardships and it will be found of
benefit. I hope that the Bill will be
passed, but that the Committee Stage will
be postponed.
Viscount
CHARLEMONT: Further
time will be given for consideration of the
Bill.
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Bill committed to a Committee of the
Whole House.

Mr. LESLIE: I beg to move in Clause
1 to leave out "be treated as being" ("shall
not be treated as being") and insert

I have proposed in the clause does not
make, and is not intended to make, any
difference in the sense of the Bill. I
merely suggest it for the reason that I
gave in the Second Reading that it is
desirable in an important Bill of this
kind to give the clearest possible pointer
as to what our real intention is.
I suggest the same word which is used by Sir
Erskine May in the treatise, to which
anybody seeking for information will
naturally refer, in regard to the analagous
case of the italicised words of certain Money
Bills. I think, on the whole, the word
"form" expresses our intention slightly
better than the words in the Bill, which
are used in the Parliament Act in the
positive sense instead of as here in the
negative.

form at a glance. The Government are
pleased to be able to accept it.

Mr. LESLIE: I beg to move in page 2,

"Any alteration in the Preamble of a Bill
which may be necessary to give effect to
this Act shall not be deemed to be an
amendment of the Bill."
This Amendment is also to bring out a
little better, perhaps, the intention of the
Bill. It is not always possible, I think,
to take in the meaning of a Bill at such
extraordinary speed as suggested by the
noble Viscount a moment ago. I think it
will be agreed that by alluding to the
Preamble as the place where the alteration
is to be made, which is what we mean,
we shall make it a little bit easier to
understand. It is very important, particularly to those concerned with local
Bills that the wording should be as simple
as possible. The words of the text, I
pointed out before, are taken out of the
Parliament Act, 1911, where they follow
a special form of enactment, which was
set out in that Act. I think that the
alteration will make for clarity, and I
suggest it be made.
Viscount CHARLEMONT: I welcome
this Amendment very much, and the
Government are pleased to accept it.

CLAUSE 1 (Power to prohibit or control the importation or keeping of musk
rats).
(1) The Ministry of Agriculture may
prohibit by order either absolutely, or
except under a licence granted under
this Act, the importation into and the
keeping within Northern Ireland of any
animal of the species designated Fiber
zibethicus or Ondatara zibethica, and
commonly known as the musk rat, or
musquash.
Colonel PAKENHAM: I beg to move,
in page 2, line 5, to leave out from "Agriculture" ("Ministry of Agriculture") to
"except" ("or except under") in line 6,
and insert "shall prohibit."
The Amendment which I move is not
in hostility to the Bill or to the Government proposing it. It is simply to make
the hand of the Government so strong
that these deleterious animals shall never
get into the country. There was a great
deal of talk about musk rats in
the House of Lords last week, and
Lord de la Warr, speaking for
the Government, told the Members of
that House some of the depredations of
the musk rat. I will read hon. Members
a short paragraph to show what might
happen if these animals got out of control in this country. He said:
"The Ministry had thirty trappers
working in the Shropshire area, where
the musk rat menace was most
dangerous. They were catching from
sixty to eighty rats a week. The
smallness of the figure might astound
the House, but he would point out the
trappers were working from the outside
of the area, where the rats were scarce.
It was hoped that the trappers would
reach the centre of the area affected
before next summer."
a
There are thirty trappers earning
good wage per week. It is costing the
Government a very large sum of money
to destroy this infernal animal. Lord de
la Warr added.
"We are not complacent about this
matter. I am quite prepared to confess that we are not happy about the
situation, but we are satisfied that
everything that we can do is being
done."
We
That is the situation in Great Britain.
With the importation of these animals a
similar situation might arise here.
are not as wealthy as they are in Great
Britain, and it would be a very severe
drain on the resources of Northern Ireland if we had to put down £10,000 or
£12,000 a year to catch these animals. I
hope that the Government will see their
way to accept this Amendment, and if
they will not accept it I hope they will
give some assurance to this House that
there is no intention to allow the importation of the musk rat. Of course, the Bill
reads "may" and talks about giving a
licence.
Personally I am quite sure hon.
Members of this House are not in favour
of getting a musk rat industry in this
country. The hon. and learned Senator,
Mr. Leslie, on the Second Reading of this
Bill outlined what it would roughly cost
to lay down a musk rat habitation in this
country. It would mean, in the first
instance, the expenditure of a very large
sum of money in concreting the whole
country round for miles. I, therefore,
move this Amendment in the sure hope
that something will be done by the
Government either by accepting the
Amendment, or, if they do not accept it,
by giving us such a complete assurance
of the wish on their part to stop these
animals coming in that I shall be able to
withdraw this Amendment.
Viscount CHARLEMONT: I am
entirely in sympathy with the hon. and
gallant Member's object, and I am,
therefore, glad to be able to give him an
assurance that an order definitely prohibiting the importation of these animals will
be issued by my right hon. Friend, the
Minister of Agriculture, immediately
after the passing of the Measure. I can
give the further assurance that no order
modifying this prohibition will be made
until a copy in draft has been laid before
Parliament. Having regard to the

CLAUSE 5 (Provisions as to musk rats
found at large).
At any time while an order under
section one of this Act is in force, the
following provisions shall have effect:-
a
(a) The occupier of any land who
knows that musk rats, not being
musk rats kept by him under
licence, are to be found at large on
such land shall forthwith give notice
to the Ministry of Agriculture;
(b) The Ministry of Agriculture, so
soon
as the said Ministry becomes
aware that musk rats, not being musk
rats kept under a licence, are to be
found at large on any land, may take
such steps as the said Ministry considers necessary for the destruction
of the musk rats, and for that purpose it shall be the duty of the
occupier of the land to afford all such
facilities as are within his power to
any authorised officer or other
authorised person;
(d) No action for damages shall lie
in respect of the killing or wounding
of any musk rat which is found at
large.
Mr. LESLIE: I beg to move, in page
3, line 28, to leave out "at large" ("at
large on such land").
These words occur four times in the
Bill, and the first two instances are to
be found in the Clause we are dealing
with. They are to be found again in
Paragraph (d) of the same Clause and
once again in Clause 8, to which reference
was made during the Second Reading. I
do not think the words help us very much.
It seems to me that if a musk rat, or any
animal which by virtue of Clause 8 is
declared to be subject to the same conditions as the musk rat, was alive in the
country and was found anywhere where he
was not under a licence, notice of his
presence ought to given and he ought to be
destroyed. That really disposes of the
two words "at large." The words themselves are, of course, in common use, but
they are generally explained by the context. You talk about a cow being at
large in a field and you know what it
means.
There might be a couple of musk
rats, or Clause (8) animals, caught and
put into an outhouse. Then, apparently,
they would not come in. I think we
must not let anything into this Bill which
would suggest that the two expressions
"not covered by licence" and "at large"
are dissimilar.
Mr. LONG: The Government are
prepared to accept this Amendment.
Amendment agreed to.

Transitional Payments (Determination
of Need) Bill,
Order for the House to be put into
Committee read.
Moved, "That this House do now
resolve itself into Committee on the said
Bill." (Viscount Charlemont.)
On Question, Motion agreed to.
In Committee accordingly.
[Viscount BANGOR in the Chair.]
CLAUSE 1 (Determination of Need).
Mr. QUIGLEY: I desire to refer to
paragraph (a), of Clause 1. which reads:
any wound or disability pension taken
into account shall be treated as if it were
reduced by one-half.
com-
I mentioned this matter in my speech
yesterday. It has been stated in another
place that the public assistance
mittees may disregard 75 per cent. of
the disability pension, or even the entire
pension if they think it advisable. Therefore I would like to know what authority
or what instructions the public assistance
committees will receive in order to work
on the basis of disregarding 75 per cent.
or more of the pension.
Mr. LESLIE: As regards that, I
should be rather inclined to submit to the
House that the purpose of the whole of
Sub-Clause 1, of Clause 1, is to enable
the public assistance committee to introduce elasticity in their decisions, but
elasticity subject to the limitation that
the deduction made in respect of a pension shall not exceed one-half of that
pension. I think that is the purport of
the Clause, as I understand it. The intention is to make it absolutely definite
that not more than one-half of a
pension shall be taken into consideration, and leaves it entirely open
to the authorities concerned to make a
greater allowance if in the special circumstances of the particular applicant they
see fit. That is the way I understand the
Sub-Clause, and the noble Viscount in
charge of the Bill will no doubt tell us
whether that view of the situation is the
correct one.
Viscount CHARLEMONT: That is the
correct interpretation. The Bill proposes
that such disability pensions as are
"taken into account" shall be treated as
if they were reduced by one-half. If a
committee takes a pension into account,
they must regard it as being worth onehalf, and may then treat that half in
such manner as they deem the circumstances to require. This is in fact an
enabling Bill.
Clause ordered to stand part of the Bill.
CLAUSE 2 (Short title, citation, and
duration) ordered to stand part of the
Bill.
Preamble and Title agreed to.

without Amendment to the House.

resume.

Acoustics of the Chamber.
MOTION FOR ADJOURNMENT.
Viscount CHARLEMONT: I beg to
move, "That this House do now adjourn
until Tuesday next." On that day the
Royal Assent to Bills will be given.
Mr. QUIGLEY: A few days ago the
hon. and gallant Senator, Colonel
Pakenham, who, I am sorry, is not
present to-day, referred to the acoustic
properties of this Chamber, and said he
was unable to hear certain hon. Senators
on the other side. He said:
"I confess that when the hon. and
gallant Member was speaking on the
Dangerous Drugs Act I was able to
catch hardly anything he said.
It
seems to me that the acoustic properties
of the Chamber are nothing to boast
of." (Official Report, 6th December,
1932, vol. 15, col. 46).
I would like to confirm that statement,
and to say that I also find it most
difficult to hear. Last week a most
important speech was delivered by an
hon. and gallant Senator from
from the
opposite side of the House and I could
not hear it at all. There were other
speeches which I failed to hear or
could only hear imperfectly. During
the Adjournment I would respectfully
suggest, Mr. Speaker, that you should
take action in the matter and see if something cannot be done to improve the
hearing in the Chamber. After all there
is no use in our sitting here if we cannot
clearly make out what is said. It is
most unsatisfactory.
Mr. LESLIE: The complaint which
has just been voiced by the hon. Member
on my left has been voiced also by others,
both within and without the House. The
hon. Senator mentioned the name or
my hon. and gallant Friend (Colonel
Pakenham), whom I do not see in his place
to-day, and I may perhaps mention that
the hon. and gallant Member made a
suggestion on the last occasion when we
met that seems to have borne a certain
amount of fruit. The suggestion was
that those of us who sit at the far end
of these Benches, more towards you, Mr.
Speaker, should move a little to the left,
as by that means their voices are more
easily carried to the gallery where the
reporters sit. That does seem to have
some effect on the situation, and in any
Acoustics of the Chamber 86
Adjournment-
15 DECEMBER 1932
experiments that are going to be made.
perhaps that fact might be borne in mind.
I ought also to mention that I presume
the sitting on Tuesday will commence at
twelve o'clock noon.
Viscount CHARLEMONT: So far as
the acoustic properties of this Chamber
are concerned, it might perhaps be better
to wait until the resumption of the
Session after the adjournment, to see
how things work out, as a result of the
adoption of the suggestion of the hon.
and gallant Senator (Colonel Pakenham)
that Members should sit a little more to
the left and to the right. I think that
Members could be perfectly heard if they
would speak up a little more, because on
this side of the House I have had difficulty
in hearing some hon. Senators, but not
others. If the acoustic properties of the
Chamber were really bad none of the
Members could be heard, and that would
be a terrible thing. I can only suggest
that every Member should endeavour to
speak as loudly as he can for the convenience of other Members, and then
there would not be so much cause for
complaint.
a
a
Mr. BARCLAY: May I make
suggestion, and that is that if all the loud
speakers would sit at the back everybody
I myself have
might be heard.
difficulty in hearing. If hon. Members
did not speak so fast and spoke more
distinctly then everybody in the Chamber
would be able to hear the speeches. That
is my impression.
Sir CRAWFORD MCCULLAGH: I
think it would be rather unfortunate if
it went out to the public that the
acoustic properties of this Chamber are
not what they ought to be. As far as I
am concerned I have heard every word
spoken on the opposite side of the House,
and if Members would have a little bit
of patience until they get used to the
House, there would be no trouble. My
mind travels back to the time when the
Belfast Corporation went into their new
Council Chamber, and there they had
practically the same difficulty. Members
complained that they could not hear, but
it was simply because they were not used
to the chamber. As time went on it was
proved that everything was all right. I
think that if, as a result of to-day's
criticism, experiments were commenced
it would only do harm. Therefore I
would respectfully suggest that hon.
Members should have a little bit of
patience, and, when speaking, take a
little time and speak distinctly, and then
I think it will be found that this beautiful Chamber is all that could be desired.
Mr. HILL DICKSON: It is not alone
the Members of the Senate who complain,
but the reporters in the gallery above.
From the reports in Hansard it is evident
that they have difficulty in hearing what
takes place, because the reports are somewhat deficient compared with those in the
previous House. I do not know if
Members could be any better heard if
they sat on the Nationalist and Socialist
benches.

House do now adjourn until Tuesday
next," agreed to.

until Tuesday next.

Royal Assent.
Viscount CHARLEMONT (Leader of
the House): I am authorised by the
Prime Minister, on his behalf, to inform
the Senate that His Grace the Governor
of Northern Ireland, in the name of and
on behalf of His Majesty the King, has
been pleased to give his Assent to the
following Bills:-
Dangerous Drugs,
Appropriation,
Transitional Payments (Determination of Need).
Question to Minister.
VOLTAGE OF ELECTRIC CURRENT.
The following question stood on the
Order Paper in the name of Major
Dobbs :
To ask the Leader of the House whether
measures will be taken to see that in
Co. Antrim the voltage of the supply
of electric current by the generating
stations to the various supply companies,
and by the supply companies to their
consumers, is up to standard; and whether
he can state what percentage variation
of the standard voltage is allowed in the
case of the distributing companies and of
their consumers respectively.
has
Major DOBBS: In asking this question
I do not want it to be thought that I am
imputing any blame to the various small
companies, supplying electricity, that
have started as a result of the last
Electricity Act. Far from that; I think
they are to be congratulated on what
they have done. One small company,
fathered by an urban district council,
in the space of two years not only laid
down its main cables, its subsidiary
cables, but it has even gone so far as to
pay for the wiring of a number of small
houses. It has bought various electrical
appliances, electric fires, and so on, and
since it started supplying electricity
thirteen months ago it has actually bought
and hired out 200 electric cookers. I
think it is most praiseworthy, because it
is not looking to recoup itself at the
moment, but is looking forward to a big
demand for electricity to arise and so recoup itself for the money expended and
the trouble it has taken.
I am very much afraid that if adequate
measures are not taken to see that the
voltage supplied to those small householders is the standard voltage, that is
230 volts, there may be great disappointment, and that far from this electricity
scheme being a great success, all its good
points may be depreciated. The question
of the supply to the householder being at
230 volts depends primarily upon the
generating station. It seems a small
thing, but I would like to point out that
if there is a drop of five per cent. in the
voltage, it does not mean purely a loss of
efficiency of five per cent., but, I believe,
I am quite right in stating that it means
a loss of efficiency of between 25 and 30
per cent. It means that those electric
fires and lights and those electric cookers
are going not only to give indifferent
service-not through any fault of the
manufacturers, but simply because of the
loss of efficiency. It means that those
electric cookers are of practically no use.
I know of one case in which an electric
cooker within the last five or six weeks,
has done at times extremely well and at
other times it has been unworkable. I
think the reason of that is this drop in
the voltage, because the voltage must be
kept up, and it can only be maintained
by seeing that the generating stations
and supply companies keep up their
voltage. Otherwise, there is going to be
a big loss of efficiency.
Oral Answer
Adjournment
20 DECEMBER 1932
Viscount CHARLEMONT: The question the hon. and gallant Member has
raised is a most important one. Variations
in pressure of supply, both as between
the generating stations and the distributing companies, and between the latter
and their various customers, are governed
by regulations made by the former
Electricity Commissioners for Northern
Ireland, whose powers and duties have
now been absorbed by the Ministry of
Commerce.
So far as extra high tension supplies
are concerned, that is to say the supplies.
which the generating stations give to the
distributing companies, the supplier is
bound to give
give the
distributor the
minimum declared pressure. The supplier
may under certain circumstances exceed
this declared pressure, provided that such
excess is not more than 12 per cent.
Narrower limits have, however, been
fixed for variations of pressure allowed to
the distributing companies in maintaining their low tension supplies to their
consumers, domestic or otherwise.
Distributors are bound to maintain their
declared pressure, subject to a variation
not exceeding 4 per cent. above or below
the declared pressure, under any conditions of supply which the consumer is
entitled to receive, unless some special
arrangement is made with the express
consent of the Ministry.
Failure to observe these regulations is
subject to penalties, as the hon. and
gallant Senator will observe from a copy
of the regulations, which I shall be glad
to make available to him.
Moved:
Adjournment.
That this House do now
adjourn until such date as Mr. Speaker
shall appoint."-(Viscount Charlemont).
On Question, Motion agreed to.
Adjourned accordingly at twenty
minutes after Twelve o'clock
until such date as Mr. Speaker
shall appoint.
